The Hanafi Principle: Discharge (Ibra') as Isqat, not Tamlik
Explanatory commentary on the Hanafi principle of ibra'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between isqat and tamlik in the context of discharging unknown rights, and apply it to validate bara'ah from unknown defects.

Core Idea
Discharge (ibra') is dropping a right, not transferring it, so ignorance does not invalidate it.

This lesson explores the foundational Hanafi principle that discharge (ibra') is a form of dropping (isqat) a right, not transferring ownership (tamlik). This distinction allows the Hanafi school to validate discharges from unknown debts, defects, or liabilities, while other schools (like Imam Ahmad) reject it. We will unpack the logical argument and its analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's View
The opponent (Imam Ahmad) considers ibra' as tamlik (transfer), so ignorance invalidates it. Understand the opposing principle first.
Example: if a creditor discharges from an unknown debt, the opponent says it is invalid.
2
Understand the Hanafi Talil
The passage explains that ibra' is isqat because it does not require acceptance and can be worded as 'dropping'. The phrase 'dropped it from you' works without acceptance, unlike tamlik.
This is the core linguistic and logical move.
3
Analyze the Analogy
Compare to divorcing unknown wives or freeing unknown slaves: isqat works even with ignorance because it does not lead to dispute.
The analogy shows that ignorance is permissible in isqat.
4
Apply the Principle
Conclude that bara'ah from unknown defects in a sale contract is valid according to the Hanafi school, and the buyer's right to object to unknown defects is dropped.
This is the practical application.

Source Text

إذ بناء على أصله أن البراءة عن الحقوق المجهولة لا يصح عنده) ، وعندنا يَصحُ) اسقاط المجهول لا يَضُرُّه ؛ لأَنَّهُ لا يفضي إلى المنازعة ، ثُمَّ هذه البراءة" تشمل العيب [٤] الموجود، وأيضاً العيب الحادث قبل القبض عند أبي يوسف ، وعند محمد ه لا تَسْمَلُ العيب الحادث. [١) قوله : لا يصح عنده ؛ لأنَّ في الإبراء معنى التمليك ، حتى أن رب الدين إذا أبرأ المديون عن دينه فردّ المديون إبراءه لم يصح الإبراء، وتمليك المجهول لا يصح كبيعه، وبه قال أحمد له. [٢) قوله : وعندنا يصح ...الخ ؛ تقريره : أنَّ الإبراء عندنا إسقاط لا تمليك ؛ لأنه لا يصح تمليك العين بهذا اللفظ ، ويصح الإبراء بقوله : أسقطت عنك بلا قبول، كما لو طلق نسوته أو أعتق عبيده ولا يـ يدري كـم هـم ولا أعيانهم ، والإسقاط لا تبطله الجهالة ؛ لأنها لا تفضي إلى المنازعة، وزيادة التفصيل في «الفتح» ديني، ويتم الإبراء [٣) قوله : هذه البراءة ؛ إنّما قال: هذه البراءة احترازا عن البراءة التي شرط البائع في قوله على أنه بريء من كل عيب به، فإنّه لا يبرأ عن الحادث بالإجماع ؛ لأنه لما قال به اقتصر على الموجود. كذا في «البناية»(۳). [٤]قوله : عند أبي يوسف له ؛ وذكره مع الإمام » في «المبسوط»، وفي «الخانية» (٤) : إنّه ظاهر مذهبهما ؛ لأنَّ المراد لزوم العقدِ بإسقاط عن صفة السلامة ، وذلك بالبراءة عن الموجود والحادث. [0] قوله : لا تشمل العيب الحادث ؛ إذ المقصود هو البراءة عن العيب الموجود لا على العموم، فلا يدخلُ المعدوم هذا ، والتفصيل في «البحر » (ه)، وغيره من المبسوطات. (1) «فتح القدير (٦ : ٣٩). (۲) ((البناية» (٦ : ٣٧٢). (۳) (المبسوط» (۱۳ : ۹۳). (٤) (الفتاوى الخانية (٢ : ٢١٦). (٥) «البحر الرائق (٦ : ٧٢).
Because based on his principle that discharge from unknown rights is not valid according to him, but according to us, dropping the unknown does not harm him, because it does not lead to dispute. Then this discharge includes the existing defect and also the defect arising before possession according to Abu Yusuf, while according to Muhammad it does not include the defect arising. [1] His saying: 'not valid according to him' because in discharge (ibra') there is the meaning of transfer of ownership, such that if the creditor discharges the debtor from his debt and the debtor rejects the discharge, the discharge is not valid; and transferring ownership of an unknown thing is not valid like selling it, and Ahmad said the same. [2] His saying: 'according to us it is valid' etc.; its explanation: that discharge according to us is dropping (isqat) not transfer of ownership; because transfer of ownership of a specific thing cannot be valid with this wording; and discharge is valid by saying 'I have dropped it from you' without acceptance, just as if he divorces his wives or frees his slaves without knowing how many they are nor their identities; and dropping is not invalidated by ignorance because it does not lead to dispute. Further detail is in 'al-Fath'. [3] His saying: 'this discharge' – he only said 'this discharge' to exclude the discharge which the seller stipulates by saying 'on condition that he is free from every defect in it', for he is not free from the defects arising by consensus, because when he said 'by it' he restricted to what exists. Thus in 'al-Binaya'. [4] His saying: 'according to Abu Yusuf' – and he mentioned it with the Imam in 'al-Mabsut', and in 'al-Khaniyah' it is the apparent position of the two; because the intended meaning is the binding of the contract by dropping the description of soundness, and that is by discharge from the existing and the arising. [5] His saying: 'it does not include the defect arising' – because the object is discharge from the existing defect, not universally, so the non-existing does not enter. Detail is in 'al-Bahr' and other comprehensive works.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a previous matn that discussed the ruling on discharging the seller from defects. The commentator explains that the Hanafi school differs from some others (like Imam Ahmad) on the validity of discharging unknown rights. The opponent's view is that ibra' (discharge) is a form of tamlik (transfer of ownership), so ignorance of the specific right invalidates it, similar to selling an unknown object. The Hanafi view, presented as 'indana (according to us), is that ibra' is isqat (dropping) of a right, not transfer of ownership. This is proven by the fact that one can say 'I have dropped it from you' without the other party's acceptance, and by analogy to divorcing unknown wives or freeing unknown slaves. Dropping is not invalidated by ignorance because it does not lead to dispute. Then the commentary addresses the scope of the standard discharge clause in a sale contract. If the seller says 'I am free from every defect in it (bihi)', this only covers defects existing at the time of contract, not those arising later, by consensus. The key intra-Hanafi dispute is between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding defects that arise before the buyer takes possession. Abu Yusuf (together with Abu Hanifa) holds that the discharge clause also covers such defects, because the buyer's dropping of the description of soundness makes the contract binding. Muhammad holds that it does not, because the clause is limited to existing defects. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as it is the apparent position in the authoritative sources.

Key Terms
البراءة (al-bara'ah): Discharge or waiver from defects in a sale contract, often stipulated by the seller.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): The act of discharging a debtor from a debt or liability; in this context, it is synonymous with bara'ah.
إسقاط (isqat): Dropping or relinquishing a right without transfer of ownership.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership or making someone owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is discharge from an unknown debt or defect valid?
A) Because it is a form of transfer of ownership (tamlik) which does not require specification.
B) Because it is a form of dropping (isqat) which does not require specification and ignorance does not lead to dispute.
C) Because it is a contract of sale and sale of unknown is valid in some cases.
D) Because the Quran explicitly permits it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ibra' is isqat, not tamlik, so discharging unknown rights is valid.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Scope of Bara'ah on Defects Arising Before Possession
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of bara'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding whether a standard discharge clause covers defects arising before possession, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf include defects before possession; Muhammad excludes them — the relied-upon view is the majority.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi disagreement about the scope of a standard discharge clause (bara'ah) in sale contracts. While the clause 'I am free from every defect in it' (bihi) covers existing defects by consensus, the question is whether it also covers defects that appear before the buyer takes possession. Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa say yes, Muhammad says no. We will explore the reasoning and the tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Consensus
Understand that there is consensus that the clause 'bihi' (in it) restricts the discharge to existing defects only.
This is the starting point.
2
State the Dispute
When the seller says 'I am free from every defect' without 'bihi', does it include defects arising before possession? That is the point of disagreement.
The dispute is between Abu Yusuf (and Abu Hanifa) vs Muhammad.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's View
The intended meaning is to make the contract binding by dropping the description of soundness. This dropping applies to all defects before possession.
The drop of the description of soundness covers the period until possession.
4
Analyze Muhammad's View
The object is discharge from the existing defect, not universal. Non-existing defects do not enter the clause.
Muhammad restricts the clause to what is present at the time of contract.
5
Note the Tarjih
The text states that the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is the apparent position (zahir madhhabihima) in Al-Mabsut and Al-Khaniyah, and this is the mufta bihi.
This ends the dispute for fatwa purposes.

ظاهر مذهبهما (zahir madhhabihima): The apparent or preferred position of two imams (here, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).

Checkpoint

A) Muhammad's view that it does not cover them.
B) Abu Yusuf's view that it covers them.
C) There is no mufta bihi; the two views are equally strong.
D) It depends on the buyer's intention.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a general discharge clause covers defects arising before possession, following Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

The Five Classifications of Sale in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining the five classifications of sale in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five classifications of sale in Hanafi fiqh, distinguish between fasid and batil, and explain the legal effect of each type.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh distinguishes five types of sale: sahih, batil, fasid, makruh, mawquf—each with unique legal consequences.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of sales in Hanafi fiqh. Understanding these categories is essential for evaluating the validity of any transaction. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah lays out the definitions and discusses the key differences, especially between batil and fasid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five types of sale
List the five categories: sahih, batil, fasid, makruh, mawquf. Note their Arabic names and the order presented in the text.
The text explicitly enumerates them numbered 1-5.
2
Understand the criteria: essence (asl) and description (wasf)
Each type is defined by whether it is 'legislated' in its essence and description. The combination yields different rulings.
Essence refers to the core elements (e.g., offer-acceptance, lawful object); description refers to ancillary attributes (e.g., price known, deferred delivery).
3
Note the legal effect of each type
For each type, determine whether ownership transfers and whether the contract is binding or rescindable.
Sahih: full transfer; Batil: no transfer; Fasid: transfer only upon possession; Makruh: valid but disliked; Mawquf: suspended pending ratification.
4
Distinguish fasid from batil
The text emphasizes that fasid is not batil. Fasid can transfer ownership upon possession, while batil never does. This is a unique Hanafi position.
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) treat both as void.
5
Understand the linguistic meaning of fasid
The passage explains that linguistically, fasid means something that has changed in description but still allows some benefit, like rotten meat. This corresponds to the legal meaning: the sale's essence is valid (the object is valuable property), but its description is defective.
This linguistic underpinning supports the Hanafi view that fasid is not entirely void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيع الفاسد 
قوله : باب البيع الفاسد ؛ اعلم أنَّ البيع على أنواع :
صحيح : وهو المشروع بأصله ووصفه. 
٢. وباطل : وهو ضدّهُ ولا يفيدُ الملكَ : بوجه. 
. وفاسد : وهو المشروع بأصله دون وصفه، ويفيد الملك إذا اتصل به القبض. 
. ومكروه : وهو المشروع بأصله ووصفه ، لكن جاوره شيء منهي عنه. 
ه. وموقوف: وهو المشروع بأصله ووصفه ، ويفيد الملك على سبيل التوقف، ولا يفيد تمامه ؛ لتعلّق حق الغير. 
ثمّ إنَّ المصنّف له أخَّرَ الفاسد عن الصحيح ، لما أنه مخالف للدين ؛ ولكونه لا ينقطع به حق كل من البائع والمشتري ؛ لثبوتِ الفسخ لهما، بل يجب عليهما. وقدم الصحيح؛ لأنه موصل إلى تمام المقصود، وهو سلامة الدين التي لها شرعت العقود ؛ ليندفع التغالب، وللوصول إلى الحاجة الدنيوية، وكل منهما بالصحة.
وإنما عنون بالفاسد مع أن فيه بيان الباطل والمكروه والموقوف أيضاً ؛ لكثرة البيع الفاسد بتعدد وقوع أسبابه ، أو المراد بالفاسد الممنوع مجازاً عرفياً، فيعم الباطل والمكروه، وقد يذكر فيه بعض الصحيح تبعاً واستطراداً.
والفاسد لغة : من فَسَدَ كَنَصَر وعَقَدَ وكَرَمَ فساداً ضدّ صلح، والفساد في الحيوان أسرع منه إلى الجماد ؛ لأنَّ الرطوبة في الحيوان أكثر من الرطوبة في النبات، وحاصل المعنى اللغوي يرجع إلى أنه ما تغيَّر وصفُهُ وأمكن الانتفاع به، فإنه يقال : فسدَ اللَّحْم، إذا أنتن مع بقاء الانتفاع به.
وفيه مناسبة للمعنى الشرعي : وهو ما كان مشروعاً بأصله لا بوصفه، ومرادهم من مشروعية أصله كونه مالاً متقوماً لا جوازه وصحته ؛ لأنَّ فساده يمنعُ صحته، وأطلقوا المشروعية عليه نظراً إلى أنه لو خلا عن الوصف لكان مشروعاً، وفي النسبة بينه وبين الباطل مباحث لطيفة تركناها لغرابة المقام، وإن شئت فارجع إلى مبسوطات الأعلام.
Chapter of the Invalid Sale (al-bay' al-fasid). His saying: Chapter of the Invalid Sale; know that sale is of types: 1. Sound (sahih): that which is legislated in its essence and description. 2. Void (batil): its opposite, and it does not confer ownership at all. 3. Invalid (fasid): that which is legislated in its essence but not in its description, and it confers ownership if possession is connected to it. 4. Disliked (makruh): that which is legislated in its essence and description, but something prohibited is adjacent to it. 5. Suspended (mawquf): that which is legislated in its essence and description, and it confers ownership on a suspended basis, and does not confer its completion because of the right of another. Then the commentator delayed fasid after sahih because it is contrary to religion, and because the right of both seller and buyer is not concluded by it due to the establishment of rescission for them, rather it is obligatory upon them. And he placed sahih first because it leads to the complete objective, which is soundness of religion for which contracts were legislated, to repel conflict and to attain worldly needs, and both are by soundness. He only titled with fasid even though it contains explanation of batil, makruh, and mawquf as well, because of the frequency of fasid sale due to the multiplicity of its causes, or by fasid he means the prohibited by customary metaphor, so it includes batil and makruh, and sometimes some sound (sahih) is mentioned in it incidentally and digressively. Fasid linguistically: from fasada (to spoil) like nasara, aqada, karama; corruption (fasad) is the opposite of soundness. Corruption in animals is faster than in inanimate objects because moisture in animals is more than moisture in plants. The sum of the linguistic meaning goes back to something whose description has changed and benefit from it is still possible; it is said 'the meat spoiled' when it rots while still possible to benefit from it. There is a suitability to the legal meaning: that which is legislated in its essence but not in its description. Their intention by 'legislated in its essence' is that it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), not its permissibility and validity; because its corruption prevents its validity. They used 'legislated' for it considering that if it were free from the description, it would be legislated. There are subtle investigations regarding the relationship between fasid and batil which we leave due to the unfamiliarity of the context; if you wish, refer to comprehensive works of the prominent scholars.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on invalid sale (bab al-bay' al-fasid). It provides a foundational classification of sales in Hanafi fiqh into five categories: (1) Sahih (sound) – valid in both essence (asl) and description (wasf); (2) Batil (void) – opposite of sahih, confers no ownership; (3) Fasid (invalid) – valid in essence but defective in description, and it confers ownership only upon possession; (4) Makruh (disliked) – valid in essence and description but adjacent to something prohibited; (5) Mawquf (suspended) – valid in essence and description but ownership is suspended due to a third party's right. The commentator explains why the author placed fasid after sahih: because it is contrary to the proper objective of contracts (soundness of religion), and because it does not finalize the rights of parties—rescission is obligatory. The chapter is named after fasid despite covering other types because fasid sales occur frequently, or 'fasid' is used broadly to include all prohibited sales. The linguistic meaning of fasid is then given: something that has changed in description but still allows some benefit, like rotten meat. This corresponds to the legal meaning: a sale whose essence is valid (the object is valuable property, mal mutaqawwim) but whose description is defective. The distinction between fasid and batil is crucial: batil confers no ownership at all, while fasid confers ownership upon possession (qabd), but the contract remains rescindable. The commentator notes that there are subtle discussions about the relationship between fasid and batil, but defers them to larger works.

Key Terms
بيع صحيح (bay' sahih): A sale that is valid in both its essence and description; it fully transfers ownership and is binding.
بيع باطل (bay' batil): A sale that is invalid in essence; it does not confer ownership at all.
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale valid in essence but defective in description; it transfers ownership upon possession but is rescindable.
بيع مكروه (bay' makruh): A sale valid in essence and description but disliked because of proximity to something prohibited (e.g., selling after Friday adhan).
بيع موقوف (bay' mawquf): A sale that is valid but its effectiveness is suspended pending consent of a third party (e.g., a guardian's sale).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the unique characteristic of a fasid (invalid) sale?
A) It is completely void and transfers no ownership.
B) It transfers ownership immediately upon contract, like a sahih sale.
C) It transfers ownership only if possession (qabd) takes place.
D) It is valid but disliked.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of sales into five categories is fundamental to understanding the validity and consequences of transactions; the key distinction is between fasid (defective in description) and batil (defective in essence), with fasid allowing ownership transfer upon possession.

The Five Types of Sale in Hanafi Fiqh – Core Definitions
Defines and classifies the five types of sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and distinguish the five types of sale in Hanafi fiqh: sahih, batil, fasid, makruh, and mawquf.

Core Idea
Sale is classified into five types: sahih, batil, fasid, makruh, mawquf – each with unique legal effects.

In Hanafi commercial law, understanding the categories of sale is foundational. This lesson introduces the five types as defined in the classical texts, focusing on their origins and descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five types
List the five types as mentioned in the passage: sahih, batil, fasid, makruh, mawquf.
They are explicitly enumerated in the first part of the text.
2
Define each type
For each type, state the definition using the criteria of 'origin' (asl) and 'description' (wasf). Sahih: legislated in both. Batil: opposite, no ownership. Fasid: legislated in origin only. Makruh: legislated in both but adjacent to something prohibited. Mawquf: legislated in both but suspended due to third party right.
Note that makruh and mawquf are valid in both origin and description but have special circumstances.
3
Note the ownership effect
Understand which types confer ownership and under what conditions. Sahih confers immediate full ownership. Batil confers no ownership. Fasid confers ownership only if possession (qabd) occurs. Makruh confers ownership normally. Mawquf confers ownership on a suspended basis until third party right is resolved.
Ownership (milk) is a key consequence.
4
Contrast fasid and batil
Recognize that fasid is defective but can become valid upon possession, while batil is void entirely and cannot be cured.
This is a major difference from other schools like Shafi'i which equate the two.
5
Consider makruh and mawquf
Makruh is valid despite a disliked element; mawquf is pending due to a third party's right (e.g., sale by a slave without master's permission).
Mawquf can become sahih if the third party consents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيع الفاسد
اعلم أنَّ البيع على أنواع :
صحيح : وهو المشروع بأصله ووصفه.
٢. وباطل : وهو ضدّهُ ولا يفيدُ الملكَ : بوجه.
٣. وفاسد : وهو المشروع بأصله دون وصفه، ويفيد الملك إذا اتصل به القبض.
٤. ومكروه : وهو المشروع بأصله ووصفه ، لكن جاوره شيء منهي عنه. ه. وموقوف: وهو المشروع بأصله ووصفه ، ويفيد الملك على سبيل التوقف، ولا يفيد تمامه ؛ لتعلّق حق الغير.
ثمّ إنَّ المصنّف له أخَّرَ الفاسد عن الصحيح ، لما أنه مخالف للدين ؛ ولكونه لا ينقطع به حق كل من البائع والمشتري ؛ لثبوتِ الفسخ لهما، بل يجب عليهما. وقدم الصحيح؛ لأنه موصل إلى تمام المقصود، وهو سلامة الدين التي لها شرعت العقود ؛ ليندفع التغالب، وللوصول إلى الحاجة الدنيوية، وكل منهما بالصحة.
وإنما عنون بالفاسد مع أن فيه بيان الباطل والمكروه والموقوف أيضاً ؛ لكثرة البيع الفاسد بتعدد وقوع أسبابه ، أو المراد بالفاسد الممنوع مجازاً عرفياً، فيعم الباطل والمكروه، وقد يذكر فيه بعض الصحيح تبعاً واستطراداً.
والفاسد لغة : من فَسَدَ كَنَصَر وعَقَدَ وكَرَمَ فساداً ضدّ صلح، والفساد في الحيوان أسرع منه إلى الجماد ؛ لأنَّ الرطوبة في الحيوان أكثر من الرطوبة في النبات، وحاصل المعنى اللغوي يرجع إلى أنه ما تغيَّر وصفُهُ وأمكن الانتفاع به، فإنه يقال : فسدَ اللَّحْم، إذا أنتن مع بقاء الانتفاع به.
وفيه مناسبة للمعنى الشرعي : وهو ما كان مشروعاً بأصله لا بوصفه، ومرادهم من مشروعية أصله كونه مالاً متقوماً لا جوازه وصحته ؛ لأنَّ فساده يمنعُ صحته، وأطلقوا المشروعية عليه نظراً إلى أنه لو خلا عن الوصف لكان مشروعاً، وفي النسبة بينه وبين الباطل مباحث لطيفة تركناها لغرابة المقام، وإن شئت فارجع إلى مبسوطات الأعلام.
Chapter of Invalid Sale. Know that sale is of types: 1. Valid (sahih): it is legislated in its origin and description. 2. Void (batil): it is the opposite and does not confer ownership at all. 3. Invalid (fasid): it is legislated in its origin but not in its description, and it confers ownership if possession is joined to it. 4. Disliked (makruh): it is legislated in its origin and description, but something prohibited is adjacent to it. 5. Suspended (mawquf): it is legislated in its origin and description, and it confers ownership on a suspended basis, but does not complete it due to attachment of a third party's right. Then the author delayed invalid (fasid) after valid because it is contrary to religion, and because the right of both seller and buyer is not cut off due to the right of rescission established for them, rather it is obligatory upon them. He presented valid because it leads to the completion of the objective, which is the soundness of religion for which contracts were legislated to repel mutual suppression and to attain worldly needs, and each of them is with validity. He only titled with 'invalid' even though it contains explanation of void, disliked, and suspended as well, due to the frequency of invalid sales with the multiplicity of their causes, or he intended by 'invalid' the prohibited by customary metaphor, so it includes void and disliked, and he may mention some valid incidentally and by extension. Linguistically, 'fasid' is from fasada (to become corrupt), corruption in animals is faster than in inanimate objects because moisture in animals is more than in plants. The gist of the linguistic meaning is that its description has changed and benefit from it is possible; e.g., 'the meat became corrupt' when it stinks while still usable. It has a correspondence to the legal meaning: what is legislated in its origin but not in its description. They mean by 'legislated in its origin' that it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), not its permissibility and validity, because its invalidity prevents its validity. They applied 'legislated' to it because if it were free of the description it would be legislated. There are subtle investigations regarding the relationship between it and the void, which we have left due to the unfamiliarity of the context; if you wish, refer to the detailed works of scholars.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the types of sale in Hanafi fiqh. It begins with five categories: Sahih (valid), Batil (void), Fasid (invalid), Makruh (disliked), and Mawquf (suspended). A valid sale is one that meets all requirements of origin (subject matter being valuable property) and description (proper offer and acceptance, etc.). A void sale is the opposite—it confers no ownership whatsoever. An invalid sale is sound in origin but defective in description (e.g., a sale with an unknown price or deferred delivery without specifying time). It does confer ownership upon taking possession (qabd). A disliked sale is valid in both origin and description but is tainted by an external prohibited element, such as selling after the Friday call to prayer. A suspended sale is valid but its completion is pending due to a third party's right, like a slave's sale without his master's permission. The author’s ordering of these types is explained: he placed fasid after sahih because fasid is contrary to the ideal of the religion, and because it does not fully sever the rights of the parties (they may rescind the sale). Sahih is placed first because it achieves the complete purpose of contracts: upholding religion and fulfilling worldly needs. The chapter is titled 'al-bay' al-fasid' (invalid sale) even though it discusses other types because invalid sales occur frequently due to various causes, or by customary usage the term 'fasid' encompasses all prohibited sales. The linguistic meaning of fasad is corruption, which is analogized to the legal concept: a change in the essence while still allowing some benefit. This classification helps students understand how Hanafi jurists categorize sales and the consequences of each.

Key Terms
صحيح (sahih): Sale that is legislated in both its origin and description; it is valid and produces full ownership.
باطل (batil): Sale that is the opposite of sahih; it does not confer ownership at all (la yufid al-milk bi wajh).
فاسد (fasid): Sale that is legislated in its origin but not in its description; it confers ownership if possession is taken.
مكروه (makruh): Sale that is valid in origin and description but is adjacent to something prohibited; it is disliked but still valid.
موقوف (mawquf): Sale that is valid in origin and description but is suspended due to a third party's right; it confers ownership on a provisional basis until the right is resolved.
ملك (milk): Ownership; the legal right of possession and disposal conferred by a valid sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, which type of sale confers ownership only upon taking possession (qabd)?
A) Sahih
B) Batil
C) Fasid
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi five-fold classification of sale provides a nuanced framework that balances legal validity with practical ownership consequences.

Ownership and Possession in Fasid and Mawquf Sales
Defines and classifies the five types of sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions under which ownership is transferred in fasid and mawquf sales and distinguish them from sahih and batil.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, ownership only follows physical possession; in a mawquf sale, ownership is suspended until a third party right is resolved.

While sahih sales transfer ownership immediately and batil sales never do, fasid and mawquf sales occupy middle ground. This lesson explores the key provisions that affect ownership in these two types.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definitions
Review that fasid sale is legislated in origin but not description; mawquf sale is legislated in both but suspended due to third party right.
Focus on the ownership phrases: 'yufid al-milk idha ittasala bihi al-qabd' (fasid) and 'yufid al-milk ala sabi al-tawaqquf' (mawquf).
2
Analyze the phrase for fasid
The phrase 'yufid al-milk idha ittasala bihi al-qabd' means ownership is conferred only when possession (qabd) is attached. Without qabd, no ownership.
Qabd means physical or constructive possession.
3
Analyze the phrase for mawquf
The phrase 'yufid al-milk ala sabi al-tawaqquf' means ownership is given on a suspended basis; it is not complete because a third party's right is attached.
Completion depends on the third party's consent or resolution.
4
Compare with sahih and batil
Sahih: immediate and complete milk. Batil: no milk at all, even with qabd. Fasid and mawquf are intermediate.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply to scenarios
Given a hypothetical, determine the type and when/if ownership transfers.
Practice with examples: buying a house without specifying price (fasid) or a slave selling his master's goods without permission (mawquf).

The Author's Arrangement and the Linguistic Meaning of Fasad
Explains the author's arrangement and the linguistic-legal correspondence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author of the text delayed fasid after sahih and how the linguistic meaning of fasad relates to its legal definition.

Core Idea
The author places valid sale first to emphasize its ideal; fasid is delayed due to its religious deficiency, and linguistically fasad means corruption with remaining utility, mirroring the legal concept.

The ordering of topics reveals jurisprudential priorities. This lesson examines the author's choice to begin with valid sales and the linguistic roots of the term fasid, showing how language and law intertwine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ordering statements
Find where the passage discusses why fasid is placed after sahih: 'akkhar al-fasid an al-sahih' and the reasons given.
The reasons: (1) contrary to religion, (2) right of rescission is established for both parties.
2
Explan the first reason
Fasid is contrary to the ideal of religion (mukhalif li al-din), so it is not given priority. Sahih is in harmony with religious objectives.
This reflects the purpose of contracts: to uphold soundness of religion (salamat al-din).
3
Explan the second reason
In fasid, the rights of seller and buyer are not conclusively severed; they can rescind. This impermanence makes it inferior to sahih.
'La yanqati' bihi haqq kull min al-ba'i' wa al-mushtari'.
4
Analyze the linguistic meaning of fasad
Linguistically, fasad describes something corrupted but still usable (e.g., stinking meat). This parallels the legal fasid: the contract is valid in origin but defective in description, still capable of yielding some benefit (ownership upon possession).
The passage gives the example of meat becoming putrid while still being useful.
5
Connect to the legal definition
The legal definition of fasid (legislated in origin, not in description) aligns with the linguistic notion of 'changed description with possible benefit.'
'Wa fihi munasabat li al-ma'na al-shar'i'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

٤. ومكروه : وهو المشروع بأصله ووصفه ، لكن جاوره شيء منهي عنه. ه. وموقوف: وهو المشروع بأصله ووصفه ، ويفيد الملك على سبيل التوقف، ولا يفيد تمامه ؛ لتعلّق حق الغير.
ثمّ إنَّ المصنّف له أخَّرَ الفاسد عن الصحيح ، لما أنه مخالف للدين ؛ ولكونه لا ينقطع به حق كل من البائع والمشتري ؛ لثبوتِ الفسخ لهما، بل يجب عليهما. وقدم الصحيح؛ لأنه موصل إلى تمام المقصود، وهو سلامة الدين التي لها شرعت العقود ؛ ليندفع التغالب، وللوصول إلى الحاجة الدنيوية، وكل منهما بالصحة.
وإنما عنون بالفاسد مع أن فيه بيان الباطل والمكروه والموقوف أيضاً ؛ لكثرة البيع الفاسد بتعدد وقوع أسبابه ، أو المراد بالفاسد الممنوع مجازاً عرفياً، فيعم الباطل والمكروه، وقد يذكر فيه بعض الصحيح تبعاً واستطراداً.
والفاسد لغة : من فَسَدَ كَنَصَر وعَقَدَ وكَرَمَ فساداً ضدّ صلح، والفساد في الحيوان أسرع منه إلى الجماد ؛ لأنَّ الرطوبة في الحيوان أكثر من الرطوبة في النبات، وحاصل المعنى اللغوي يرجع إلى أنه ما تغيَّر وصفُهُ وأمكن الانتفاع به، فإنه يقال : فسدَ اللَّحْم، إذا أنتن مع بقاء الانتفاع به.
وفيه مناسبة للمعنى الشرعي : وهو ما كان مشروعاً بأصله لا بوصفه، ومرادهم من مشروعية أصله كونه مالاً متقوماً لا جوازه وصحته ؛ لأنَّ فساده يمنعُ صحته، وأطلقوا المشروعية عليه نظراً إلى أنه لو خلا عن الوصف لكان مشروعاً، وفي النسبة بينه وبين الباطل مباحث لطيفة تركناها لغرابة المقام، وإن شئت فارجع إلى مبسوطات الأعلام.
Chapter of Invalid Sale. Know that sale is of types: 1. Valid (sahih): it is legislated in its origin and description. 2. Void (batil): it is the opposite and does not confer ownership at all. 3. Invalid (fasid): it is legislated in its origin but not in its description, and it confers ownership if possession is joined to it. 4. Disliked (makruh): it is legislated in its origin and description, but something prohibited is adjacent to it. 5. Suspended (mawquf): it is legislated in its origin and description, and it confers ownership on a suspended basis, but does not complete it due to attachment of a third party's right. Then the author delayed invalid (fasid) after valid because it is contrary to religion, and because the right of both seller and buyer is not cut off due to the right of rescission established for them, rather it is obligatory upon them. He presented valid because it leads to the completion of the objective, which is the soundness of religion for which contracts were legislated to repel mutual suppression and to attain worldly needs, and each of them is with validity. He only titled with 'invalid' even though it contains explanation of void, disliked, and suspended as well, due to the frequency of invalid sales with the multiplicity of their causes, or he intended by 'invalid' the prohibited by customary metaphor, so it includes void and disliked, and he may mention some valid incidentally and by extension. Linguistically, 'fasid' is from fasada (to become corrupt), corruption in animals is faster than in inanimate objects because moisture in animals is more than in plants. The gist of the linguistic meaning is that its description has changed and benefit from it is possible; e.g., 'the meat became corrupt' when it stinks while still usable. It has a correspondence to the legal meaning: what is legislated in its origin but not in its description. They mean by 'legislated in its origin' that it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), not its permissibility and validity, because its invalidity prevents its validity. They applied 'legislated' to it because if it were free of the description it would be legislated. There are subtle investigations regarding the relationship between it and the void, which we have left due to the unfamiliarity of the context; if you wish, refer to the detailed works of scholars.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the types of sale in Hanafi fiqh. It begins with five categories: Sahih (valid), Batil (void), Fasid (invalid), Makruh (disliked), and Mawquf (suspended). A valid sale is one that meets all requirements of origin (subject matter being valuable property) and description (proper offer and acceptance, etc.). A void sale is the opposite—it confers no ownership whatsoever. An invalid sale is sound in origin but defective in description (e.g., a sale with an unknown price or deferred delivery without specifying time). It does confer ownership upon taking possession (qabd). A disliked sale is valid in both origin and description but is tainted by an external prohibited element, such as selling after the Friday call to prayer. A suspended sale is valid but its completion is pending due to a third party's right, like a slave's sale without his master's permission. The author’s ordering of these types is explained: he placed fasid after sahih because fasid is contrary to the ideal of the religion, and because it does not fully sever the rights of the parties (they may rescind the sale). Sahih is placed first because it achieves the complete purpose of contracts: upholding religion and fulfilling worldly needs. The chapter is titled 'al-bay' al-fasid' (invalid sale) even though it discusses other types because invalid sales occur frequently due to various causes, or by customary usage the term 'fasid' encompasses all prohibited sales. The linguistic meaning of fasad is corruption, which is analogized to the legal concept: a change in the essence while still allowing some benefit. This classification helps students understand how Hanafi jurists categorize sales and the consequences of each.

Key Terms
صحيح (sahih): Sale that is legislated in both its origin and description; it is valid and produces full ownership.
باطل (batil): Sale that is the opposite of sahih; it does not confer ownership at all (la yufid al-milk bi wajh).
فاسد (fasid): Sale that is legislated in its origin but not in its description; it confers ownership if possession is taken.
مكروه (makruh): Sale that is valid in origin and description but is adjacent to something prohibited; it is disliked but still valid.
موقوف (mawquf): Sale that is valid in origin and description but is suspended due to a third party's right; it confers ownership on a provisional basis until the right is resolved.
ملك (milk): Ownership; the legal right of possession and disposal conferred by a valid sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is one reason the author delayed the discussion of fasid sale after sahih?
A) Because fasid sales are less common
B) Because fasid is contrary to the ideal of religion
C) Because fasid is the most important category
D) Because the author forgot to include it earlier

Key Takeaway
The author's ordering and terminology reveal a pedagogical method that prioritizes the ideal and uses linguistic analogy to clarify legal categories.

The Voidness of Selling Non-Property: Blood, Carrion, and Free Persons
Commentary explaining the voidness of sale of blood, carrion, and free persons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling (batil) for the sale of blood, carrion, and free persons, distinguish between types of carrion, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding carrion for dhimmis.

Core Idea
Blood, carrion, and free persons are not property — their sale is void (batil).

This lesson explores a foundational ruling in Hanafi sales law: what cannot be sold because it lacks the quality of property (mal). The commentator explains the matn's examples — blood, carrion, and a free person — and clarifies important distinctions. Understanding these exclusions helps you recognize when a contract is fundamentally void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its examples
The matn states: 'The sale of what is not property is void, such as blood, carrion, and a free person.' Note that the commentator lists these as examples of non-property (ghayr mal).
The ruling is 'batil' — the contract confers no ownership.
2
Distinguish natural carrion from other carrion
The commentator explains that 'maytah' (fathah on mim, sukun on ya) refers to an animal that died naturally, while 'maytah' (with tashdid on the ya) refers to an animal that died from a non-slaughter cause. Natural carrion is unanimously not property; the second type is not property for Muslims, but for dhimmis there are two narrations.
This distinction affects rulings for non-Muslims.
3
Analyze the free person's special status
A free person is not property because freedom is inherent. The commentator notes that in the sharia of Prophet Jacob, a free person could become property through enslavement for theft, but in Islam, a free person is never subject to ownership.
The sale of a free person is batil from the outset and remains batil.
4
Differentiate batil from fasid
For dhimmis, one narration allows the sale of carrion as fasid (invalid but not void), meaning the contract is not totally null but still invalid. The commentator emphasizes that for Muslims, all carrion is simply not property, so the sale is batil.
Batil gives no rights; fasid can sometimes be corrected.

بطل بيع ما ليس بمال : كالدَّمِ والميتة والحر، والبيع به، وكذا
[١]قوله : بطل ؛ قدَّم البيعَ الباطل ؛ لأنه لا يفيد الملك أصلاً، ففيه خطر عظيم.
[٢] قوله : كالدم ؛ أصله وفي تثنيته دميان، وجمعه دماء. كذا في «البحر»، والمراد به المسفوح احترازاً عن الكبد والطحال، فإنّ بيعهما جائز.
[٣] قوله : والميتة ؛ - بفتح الميم وسكون الياء - : هي التي ماتت حتف أنفها، لا والميتة - بسبب - بفتح الميم، وتشديد الياء المكسورة - : هي التي ماتت بسبب غير الذكاة، كالمنخنقة والموقوذة، والقسم الأوّل ليس بمال عند المسلمين أهل الذمة اتفاقاً، والقسم الثاني ليس بمال في حق المسلمين اتفاقاً، وفي حق أهل الذمة روايتان ، كذا ذكره الطحطاوي في «حاشية الدر المختار»(۳).
وقال في «البحر»(۳) : إنَّ فيما لم يمت حتف أنفه بـل لسبب غير الذكاة روايتين بالنسبة للكافر ، في رواية : الجواز وفي رواية : الفساد، وأمَّا البطلان فلا، وأما في حقنا فالكل سواء.
[٤]قوله : والحر ؛ الظاهر أنه معطوف على الدّم، لكن قال في «جامع الرموز»: «إنه كان مالاً في شريعة يعقوب عليه السلام حتى استرق السارق على ما قالوا. كما في «شرح التأويلات» وغيره، فلا يقال : إنّه لم يكن مالاً عند أحد». انتهى(4). فينبغي أن يكون معطوفاً على ما ليس بمال.
[0] قوله : والبيع به ؛ أي بيع الشيء بواحد من هذه الأشياء، وهي الميتة والدم والحرّ، أعني جعله ثمناً بإدخال الباء عليه ، كأن يقول : بعتُ هذا الثوب بهذه الميتة مثلاً.
(٦) قوله : وكذا ...الخ ؛ أي كما أن بيع الدم والميتة والحر باطلاً، فكذا بيع هولاء باطل أيضاً، لكن ليس كبطلان بيع الحرّ، فإنّه باطل ابتداء وبقاء ؛ لعدم محلية المبيع بثبوت حقيقة الحرية، وبيع هولاء باطل بقاء لحق الحرية لا ابتداء ؛ لعدم حقيقتها، ولهذا
The sale of what is not property is void: such as blood, carrion, and a free person, and selling with these. [1] He said: 'batal' – he presented the void sale first because it does not confer ownership at all, so there is great danger in it. [2] He said: 'like blood' – its root, dual is damiyan, plural dima'. As in 'al-Bahr', by it is meant spilled blood, as a precaution against the liver and spleen, for their sale is permissible. [3] He said: 'and carrion' – with fatha on mim and sukun on ya: that which dies naturally, not carrion (with tashdid on ya): that which dies due to a cause other than proper slaughter, like strangled and beaten animals. The first type is not property for Muslims and dhimmis unanimously. The second type is not property for Muslims unanimously, and regarding dhimmis there are two narrations, as mentioned by al-Tahtawi in his Hashiya. It is said in 'al-Bahr' that regarding what did not die naturally but due to other than proper slaughter, there are two narrations concerning the disbeliever: one permits, the other declares it fasid (invalid), but not batil (void). As for us (Muslims), all are the same. [4] He said: 'and the free person' – apparently it is conjoined to blood, but 'Jami' al-Rumuz' said: 'He used to be property in the law of Jacob (peace be upon him) until the thief was enslaved as they said, as in 'Sharh al-Ta'wilat' and others, so it cannot be said that he was never property.' So it should be conjoined to 'what is not property'. [0] He said: 'and selling with it' – i.e., selling a thing for one of these, namely carrion, blood, and free person, making it a price by using the preposition baa, e.g., 'I sold this garment for this carrion.' [6] He said: 'and likewise...' i.e., just as the sale of blood, carrion, and a free person is void, so also the sale of these is void, but it is not like the voidness of selling a free person, for that is void from beginning to end due to the lack of the sold item's eligibility by the established reality of freedom, whereas the sale of these is void in continuance due to the right of freedom, not initially, due to lack of its reality, and therefore...
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling that the sale of items which are not considered property (mal) is void (batil). The commentator explains each example: blood (specifically spilled blood, as liver and spleen are permissible to sell), carrion (with a distinction between naturally dead and those killed by other means), and a free human. For carrion, the ruling is that it is not property for Muslims; for dhimmis, there are two narrations within the Hanafi school: one permitting its sale and one declaring it fasid (but not batil). However, the batil ruling is only for Muslims. The free person is not property, though it is noted that in the Sharia of Prophet Jacob, a thief could be enslaved, so it was property then. The commentator clarifies that selling a free person is batil from the outset because a free person is inherently not subject to ownership, whereas selling blood or carrion might be considered void only in terms of continuity because they are not property by nature. The passage also explains that the preposition 'ba' indicates making the item the price. Overall, this lesson clarifies the categories of items that cannot be sold and the level of voidness (batil) applied to such transactions.

Key Terms
ميتة (maytah): Carrion; an animal that died without proper Islamic slaughter. The text distinguishes between 'maytah' (naturally dead) and 'maytah bisabab' (died from cause other than slaughter).
دم (dam): Blood; specifically spilled blood (al-masfuh) as opposed to blood retained in liver and spleen, which can be sold.
حر (hurr): Free person; a human who is not a slave. The sale of a free person is batil because a free person is not subject to ownership.
بطل (batala): To be void; the legal effect that the contract is null and confers no ownership.
مال (mal): Property; something that is legally recognized as valuable and can be sold. The text discusses what is not mal.

Key Takeaway
Items that are not considered property (mal) in Islamic law—such as spilled blood, carrion, and free persons—cannot be validly sold; their sale is batil (void) and conveys no ownership.

Why Sale of Non-Property Items Is Void
Commentary on the ruling that sale of non-property items is void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the sale of blood, dead animals, and free persons is void in Hanafi fiqh, and distinguish which items are considered property (mal).

Core Idea
A sale is void if the subject matter is not property – blood, dead animals, and free persons cannot be owned or sold.

The Hanafi school defines property (mal) strictly: only what is naturally sought and storable. This ruling applies the definition to three key examples – blood, dead animals, and free persons – all of which lack the legal capacity for ownership. Understanding why these sales are void reveals the foundational role of property in Islamic contracts. The commentary also unpacks exceptions and nuanced rulings for different types of dead animals and the possibility of using such items as a price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items ruled void
From the text, list the three items: blood (poured-out), dead animal (maytah), and free person (hurr). The sale of each is declared batil (void).
Also note that selling by means of these items (as price) is also void.
2
Define mal (property) as per Hanafi school
Recall that Hanafis define mal as that which is naturally desired and can be stored for later use. This excludes blood (cannot be stored in its natural state), dead animals (no value in Islamic law), and free persons (human dignity prevents ownership).
The definition is implicit in the commentary; it is essential to understand why each item fails the test.
3
Apply the definition to each item
Explain why blood (poured-out), dead animals (natural death or death by cause for Muslims), and free persons are not considered mal. Note the exception for liver and spleen (not poured-out blood) and the two narrations regarding dhimmis for animals dying by a cause.
The commentary distinguishes between natural death (hataf anfiha) and death by cause; both are non-property for Muslims.
4
Analyze the consequence of the ruling
A void sale (batil) confers no ownership. If someone attempts to sell these items, the contract has no legal effect. The commentary emphasizes the great danger (khatar) in such transactions.
The distinction between void (batil) and corrupt (fasid) is also touched upon for dhimmis.
5
Distinguish selling as commodity vs. as price
The commentary explains that using these items as a price (thaman) is also void, but there is a nuance: selling a free person is void at inception and continuance, whereas selling blood or dead animal as a price is only void at continuance due to the right of freedom.
This subtle distinction highlights the robustness of the concept of property in sale contracts.

[2] قوله : كالدم ؛ أصله وفي تثنيته دميان، وجمعه دماء. كذا في «البحر»، والمراد به المسفوح احترازاً عن الكبد والطحال، فإنّ بيعهما جائز. [3] قوله : والميتة ؛ - بفتح الميم وسكون الياء - : هي التي ماتت حتف أنفها، لا والميتة - بسبب - بفتح الميم، وتشديد الياء المكسورة - : هي التي ماتت بسبب غير الذكاة، كالمنخنقة والموقوذة، والقسم الأوّل ليس بمال عند المسلمين أهل الذمة اتفاقاً، والقسم الثاني ليس بمال في حق المسلمين اتفاقاً، وفي حق أهل الذمة روايتان ، كذا ذكره الطحطاوي في «حاشية الدر المختار». وقال في «البحر» : إنَّ فيما لم يمت حتف أنفه بـل لسبب غير الذكاة روايتين بالنسبة للكافر ، في رواية : الجواز وفي رواية : الفساد، وأمَّا البطلان فلا، وأما في حقنا فالكل سواء. [5] قوله : والبيع به ؛ أي بيع الشيء بواحد من هذه الأشياء، وهي الميتة والدم والحرّ، أعني جعله ثمناً بإدخال الباء عليه ، كأن يقول : بعتُ هذا الثوب بهذه الميتة مثلاً. The sale of that which is not property is void: such as blood, a dead animal, and a free person, and the sale by means of it (i.e., taking it as a price). Likewise, the sale of that which is not property is void: such as blood, a dead animal, and a free person, and the sale by means of it. [1] His saying: 'is void'; he presented the void sale because it does not confer ownership at all, so there is great danger in it. [2] His saying: 'such as blood'; its origin, in the dual it is damyan, and its plural is dima'. Thus in al-Bahr, and what is intended is poured-out blood, as an exception to the liver and spleen, for their sale is permissible. [3] His saying: 'and a dead animal' — with fatha on the mim and sukun on the ya — it is that which died naturally (hataf anfiha), not 'dead animal due to a cause' — with fatha on the mim and tashdid on the kasra ya — it is that which died due to other than slaughter, such as the strangled and the beaten. The first category is not property according to Muslims and dhimmis unanimously, and the second category is not property with respect to Muslims unanimously, and with respect to dhimmis there are two narrations. Thus al-Tahtawi mentioned in his Hashiya on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. And he said in al-Bahr: concerning that which did not die naturally but due to other than slaughter, there are two narrations regarding the disbeliever; in one narration permissibility, in another corruption (fasad), but voidness (butlan) is not (possible), but with respect to us all are the same. [4] His saying: 'and a free person'; apparently it is conjoined to 'blood', but in Jami' al-Rumuz it is stated: 'It was property in the law of Jacob (peace be upon him) until the thief was enslaved as they said, as in Sharh al-Ta'wilat and others, so it cannot be said that it was not property among anyone.' End. So it should be conjoined to 'that which is not property'. [5] His saying: 'and the sale by means of it'; i.e., selling a thing by one of these things—the dead animal, blood, and free person—meaning making it a price by entering the ba upon it, as if one says: I sold this garment for this dead animal, for example. [6] His saying: 'and likewise...etc.'; i.e., just as the sale of blood, dead animal, and free person is void, so too the sale of them (i.e., as price) is also void. But it is not like the voidness of the sale of a free person, for that is void at inception and continuance due to the lack of the sold item being a proper subject (mahall) because of the established reality of freedom. And the sale of these (as price) is void in continuance due to the right of freedom, not at inception, because of the lack of its reality (i.e., the thing has no reality as property).
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that the sale of items not considered property (mal) is void (batil). The author begins by stating the ruling explicitly: the sale of blood, dead animals (maytah), and free persons is void, as is accepting these as payment (i.e., making them the price). The commentator then expands on each term. First, he explains why the author began with 'batil' (void): because a void sale does not transfer ownership at all, making it a serious matter. Second, he clarifies 'blood' means poured-out blood (masfuh), excluding the liver and spleen, which are permissible to sell. Third, he distinguishes two types of dead animals: those that die naturally (hataf anfiha) and those that die from a cause other than slaughter (e.g., strangled, beaten). The first type is unanimously not property for Muslims and dhimmis; the second type is not property for Muslims, but for dhimmis there are two narrations—one allowing their use (i.e., they are property for them) and one deeming it corrupt (fasad), but not void. Then he discusses 'free person', noting that in the law of Prophet Jacob (AS) a free person was considered property (as a thief could be enslaved), but in Islamic law it is not property; the text clarifies that 'free person' should be conjoined to 'that which is not property'. Finally, he explains that selling these items as a price (thaman) is also void, but there is a nuance: selling a free person as a commodity is void at inception and continuance because a free person is never a proper subject of sale. In contrast, selling blood or a dead animal as a price is only void at continuance because they lack reality as property, but initially they could be considered something of value (though not legally). The commentary thus provides a detailed analysis of the ruling, distinguishing between types and explaining the underlying reasoning.

Key Terms
مال (mal): Property: that which is naturally sought after and can be stored; excludes things like dirt and pure filth that have no value.
ميتة (maytah): A dead animal that died naturally or by a cause other than Islamic slaughter.
حر (hurr): A free person; not a commodity and cannot be sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which of the following items is permissible to sell?
A) Poured-out blood (dam masfuh)
B) Liver of a slaughtered animal
C) A free person
D) A dead animal that died naturally

Key Takeaway
The sale of blood, dead animals, and free persons is void because they are not considered property (mal) in the Hanafi school, though exceptions exist for certain organs and ikhtilaf for dhimmis.

8 min
Invalidity of Selling Umm Walad, Mudabbar, and Mukatab
Commentary expanding on the void sale of certain slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the sale of umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab is void in continuance and distinguish it from the sale of a free person.

Core Idea
Sale of slaves with a right to freedom is void in continuance, not at inception.

In this passage, the commentator explains that the sale of certain slaves—umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab—is invalid (batil) but in a specific way: they entered the sale as valid subjects initially, but later their right to freedom caused the sale to become void. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying the ruling in composite sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the three types of slaves: umm walad (slave mother of master's child), mudabbar (promised freedom after death), and mukatab (contract for freedom). Note their common feature: they have a right to eventual freedom.
Each definition is given in the commentary.
2
Understand the voidness nature
The sale of these slaves is not void at inception (they can be sold as part of a group), but void in continuance (the sale becomes invalid once the buyer/ seller tries to transfer full ownership because of the slave's right).
This is unlike a free person, who is never a valid subject.
3
Analyze the analogy with a free person
The author rebuts a potential objection: if these sales were void ab initio, it would be like selling a free person. The difference is that these slaves initially were proper subjects (mahall) for sale, so the sale of the whole group is valid for the other slaves, but void for these slaves due to their right.
The comparison is in the voidness itself, not in the description.
4
Apply the rule
In a sale involving a package of slaves including one of these, the sale is valid for the remaining slaves and void for the problematic one, with the price adjusted proportionally.
This is a practical consequence of void in continuance.

Source Text

[1] اعلم أنَّ المال عين يجري فيه التَّنافس والابتذال ، فيخرج منه التُراب ونحوه جاز بيعهم من أنفسهم. وبهذا التقرير بطل ما قيل : لو بطل بيعُ هؤلاء لكان كبيع الحرّ، ولزم بطلان بيع القن المضموم إليهم في البيع كالمضموم إلى الحرّ ؛ وذلك لأنهم دخلوا في البيع ابتداءً لكونهم محلاً له في الجملة ، ثمَّ خرجوا منه ؛ لتعلَّقِ حقهم، فبقي القن بحصته من الثمن ، والبيع بالحصة بقاء جائز بخلاف الحرّ، فإنّه لما لم يدخل في البيع لعدم المحلية، لزم البيع بالحصة ابتداءً وأنه باطل. كذا في «المنح»، فالتشبيه في نفس البطلان لا في وصفه. [3] قوله : غير متقوّم ؛ أي غير مباح الانتفاع به، والتقوم ضربان : عرفي : وهو بالإحراز ، فغير المحرز كالصيد والحشيش ليس بمتقوم. وشرعي: وهو بإباحة الانتفاع كذا في «التلويح»، والثاني هو المراد هاهنا منفياً. The void and disliked sale: the sale of umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab, and the sale of non-valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim) such as wine and swine for a price. The sale of umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab, and the sale of non-valuable property such as wine and swine for a price. [1] Know that property (mal) is a substance in which competition and free-spending occur, so dirt and the like are excluded; their sale among themselves is permissible. With this explanation, what was said is nullified: if the sale of these (i.e., umm walad, etc.) were void, it would be like the sale of a free person, and it would necessitate the voidness of the sale of the slave (qinn) included with them in the sale, like the one included with a free person. That is because they entered the sale initially by being a proper subject (mahall) for it in general, then they left it due to the attachment of their right (haqq). So the slave remains with his share of the price, and the sale by that share remains valid in continuance, unlike a free person who did not enter the sale due to its lack of proper subjecthood, so the sale by share is necessary at inception and it is void. Thus in al-Minah, so the comparison is in the voidness itself, not in its description. [2] His saying: 'umm walad...etc.'; Umm walad is the slave woman whom her master had intercourse with and she gave birth from him, and the master claimed the child, so the child becomes free when born following the father, and this slave woman becomes free after the master's death, even if he did not state it. Mudabbar is the slave to whom his master said: 'When I die, you are free'. Mukatab is the slave to whom his master said: 'If you pay me such-and-such dirhams, you are free'. Its ruling is that if he pays, he is freed, and by the kitaba he becomes free permanently, so he performs sale and purchase and acquires property, but he remains a slave in terms of legal capacity until he pays all the money. [3] His saying: 'non-valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim)'; i.e., that which is not permissible to benefit from. And valuability (taqawwum) is of two types: customary (urfi): which is by possession, so unpossessed things like game and grass are not valuable. And legal (shar'i): which is by permissibility of benefit; thus in al-Talwih. The second is intended here negated. [4] His saying: 'so dirt and the like are excluded'; such as pure filth; as for the sale of manure and dung...
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on invalid sales, covering two additional categories: (1) the sale of specific types of slaves—umm walad (a slave woman who bears her master's child), mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death), and mukatab (a slave who has made a contract to purchase his freedom)—and (2) the sale of non-valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim) like wine and swine. The commentator first explains that the sale of these slaves is void in continuance, not at inception, because they initially could be subjects of sale (as part of a slave's property) but later become free due to attachment of rights. Unlike a free person who is never a subject, these slaves are valid subjects initially. Then he defines each type: umm walad becomes free upon the master's death automatically; mudabbar is promised freedom after the master's death; mukatab is conditionally free upon payment. In all cases, their sale is void because they have a right to freedom that prevents permanent transfer. Next, he discusses non-valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim): items that are not permissible to benefit from, such as wine and swine. He explains there are two types of 'taqawwum' (valuability): customary (by possession) and legal (by permissibility of benefit). The legal type is the relevant one here: an item is valuable only if its benefit is permissible. He then gives examples: dirt and pure filth are not valuable, but manure (such as night soil) has some value. This ruling underscores that only items that are both possessed and legally beneficial can be sold.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A slave woman who bears her master's child and becomes free upon his death.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who is promised freedom after the master's death.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a contract to purchase his freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of a mudabbar slave void in continuance rather than at inception?
A) Because the mudabbar is like a free person at the time of sale.
B) Because the mudabbar initially enters the sale as a valid subject, but his right to future freedom makes the sale void later.
C) Because the mudabbar's sale is valid only if the master dies before payment.
D) Because the mudabbar is not considered property at all.

Key Takeaway
The sale of umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab is void in continuance, not ab initio, because their right to freedom prevents full transfer of ownership.

Invalidity of Selling Non-Usufructuary Property (Ghayr Mutaqawwim)
Explanation of non-valuable property and its effect on sale validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) and apply the condition that only legally beneficial items can be sold.

Core Idea
An item is sellable only if it is legally valuable (mutaqawwim), i.e., its benefit is permissible.

This part of the passage focuses on the second category of invalid sales: items that are not valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim) such as wine, swine, and dirt. The commentator explains the concept of 'taqawwum' (valuability) and distinguishes between customary valuability (by possession) and legal valuability (by permissibility of benefit). Only the latter qualifies an item for sale in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Define 'mal mutaqawwim'
Understand that mal is a substance in which competition and free-spending occur. Mutaqawwim means the item has value in the sense that its benefit is permissible (Shariah-approved).
The passage explicitly says 'ghayr mutaqawwim: i.e., not permissible to benefit from'.
2
Distinguish the two types of taqawwum
Customary (urfi): value through possession (e.g., captured game, wild herbs). Legal (shar'i): value through permissibility of benefit (e.g., food, clothing). The second is the relevant one for determining sellability.
Items like dirt or pure filth have no legal value even if they can be possessed.
3
Identify examples of non-valuable property
Items like wine, swine, and pure filth (e.g., undiluted human waste) are not legally valuable because their use is prohibited. They cannot be sold.
Manure (e.g., animal dung) may have some value as fertilizer; this is an exception discussed in the continuation.
4
Apply the condition
For a sale to be valid, the object must be mal mutaqawwim shar'an (legally valuable). If not, the sale is void (batil).
This is a condition (shart) of validity, not a pillar.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is both possessed and permissible to benefit from; only such items can be validly sold.

تقوم شرعي (taqawwum shar'i): Legal valuability: an item's benefit is permissible in Islamic law.
سرقين (sirqin): Manure (animal dung) used as fertilizer; considered valuable because its benefit is permissible.

Sale by Umm Walad, Mudabbar, and Mukatab: The Qiyas Argument
Qiyas argument about slaves with pending freedom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the validity of sale by an umm walad, mudabbar, or mukatab from themselves and apply the qiyas argument distinguishing them from free persons.

Core Idea
These slaves may sell from themselves because they are partly owned, unlike free persons.

This passage addresses a common question: can an umm walad, mudabbar, or mukatab engage in trade? Some might think such sales are void by analogy to free persons. The Hanafi school clarifies that they are valid because these slaves retain an owned share, and the sale is confined to that share.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The text rules on the sale of these slaves from themselves. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih).
The act is the slave selling their own property.
2
Understand the objection
Some argue that if the sale of these slaves were valid, it would be like selling a free person, which is void. This is the opposing view.
The objection is based on analogy (qiyas) to free persons.
3
Follow the qiyas response
The Hanafi response uses the same qiyas but distinguishes: these slaves entered the sale as valid subjects (they are owned), then exited due to their right, leaving the owned share. A free person never entered as a subject.
The asl (free person) is distinguished by the far' (slaves) in that the slaves have a share of ownership.
4
Analyze the illa
The effective cause (illa) is the attachment of a freedom right, which makes them partially like free persons but not entirely.
The illa is shabahah (similarity) not complete identity.
5
Conclude the ruling
The sale is valid for the owned share of the slave. This is unlike the sale of a free person, which is void from the start.
The practical result: the sale is sahih.

أما الميتة التي خُنقت ، أو جُرحَتْ في غير موضع الذبح كما هو عادة بعض
الكفار وذبائح المجوس فمال ، إلا أنها غير متقومة، كالخمر والخنزير.
ويخرج منه الحُرُّ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لا يجري فيه الابتذال، بل هو مبتذل. والمالُ الغير المتقوَّمُ مالٌ أُمِرُنَا بإهانته ، لكنَّه في غير ديننا مال متقوم.
فكل ما ليس بمال فالبيع فيه باطل سواءٌ جُعِل مبيعاً أو ثمناً. وكل ما هو مال غير مال غير متقوم، فإن بيع بالثَّمن – أي بالدراهم أو الدَّنانير -
[0]
נד فالبيع باطل ، وإن بيع بالعَرَض أو بيع العَرَضُ به فالبيعُ فاسدٌ والانتفاع به، والوقود به، فجائز. كذا في البحر» (۱) عن «السراج»، والمراد بالتراب الخالص، أما إذا اختلط برجيع جاز بيعه كما صرحوا به. ١ ] قوله : حتف أنفه ؛ في «الصراح»: حتف مرك حتوف ج ، يقال : مات فلان حتف أنفه : إذا مات من غير قتل ولا ضرب، ولا يبنى منه فعل. [٢] قوله : خنقت ؛ خنق بكسر النون خبه كردن كذا في «الصراح»، وخبه : بفتح أول وثاني : خفه است که کلو فشرون باشد و تاسه وتلواسه لا نيز كويند. كذا في «البرهان»، والأول هو المراد.
[٣] قوله : فمال ؛ لأنها أعيان يجري فيها التنافس، والابتدالُ بين الكفار ؛ لكونها من مطعوماتهم ومرغوباتهم، لكنها غير منتفعة بها في حقنا ؛ لأنَّ الشريعة المصطفوية أظهرت مضارها وأذاها ، فنحنُ ممنوعون عن الانتفاع بها. [٤] قوله : باطل ؛ لانعدام ركن البيع، وهو مبادلة المال بالمال، فإنَّ هذه الأشياء لا تعد مالاً فلا تكون محلاً للبيع. [0] قوله : فالبيع باطل ؛ لأنَّ المقصود من البيع عينُ المبيع ؛ لأنها هي المنتفع بها لا عين الثمن ؛ لأنها جعلت وسيلة إليه ؛ ولهذا يجوز ثبوتُهُ في الذمة، وإذا جُعِلَت الخمرُ مثلاً مبيعة تكون مقصودة، وفيه إعزاز والشرع أمرنا بإهانتها ؛ ولهذا يبطل بيعها. [٦] قوله : فالبيع فاسد ؛ في العرض فيملكه بالقبض ، فتجب قيمته لوجودِ حقيقة
(۱) «البحر الرائق» (٦ : ٧٧).
Know that property (mal) is a substance in which competition and commonness occur, so dust and the like are excluded. It is permissible for them to sell from themselves. By this clarification, what was said is refuted: if the sale of these (umm walad etc.) were invalid, it would be like the sale of a free person, and the invalidity of the sale of the owned slave included with them in the sale would be necessary, like the slave included with a free person. That is because they entered the sale initially as a valid subject, then left due to the attachment of their right, so the owned slave remains with his share of the price, and the sale by share remains valid, unlike the free person, for he did not enter the sale due to lack of capacity, so the sale by share is necessary initially and it is void. Thus in al-Munah. So the comparison is in the invalidity itself, not its description. ... The definition of umm walad, mudabbar, mukatab. Non-mutaqawwim means not permissible to benefit from. Mutaqawwim is of two types: customary (by acquisition) and legal (by permissibility of benefit). The second is meant here negatively. ... The free person is excluded because commonness does not apply to him; rather he is above that. Non-mutaqawwim property is property that we are commanded to treat with contempt, but in other religions it is valuable property. So everything that is not property, its sale is void whether made as the object of sale or the price. And everything that is property but non-mutaqawwim, if sold for a price (i.e. dirhams or dinars), the sale is void; if sold for a commodity (ard) or a commodity is sold for it, the sale is corrupt (fasid), and benefiting from it and using it as fuel is permissible.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of rulings on sales involving certain types of slaves and non-valuable property, found in the Hanafi madhhab. It begins by defining property (mal) as a substance that people compete for and commonly use, excluding dust and similar worthless items. The main ruling is that an umm walad (a slave mother of her master's child), a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom after the master's death), and a mukatab (a slave who has made a contract of manumission for payment) may sell goods from themselves (i.e., from their own property or earnings), contrary to a misconception that such sales are like selling a free person. The text refutes this by analogy: these slaves initially entered the sale as valid subjects (since they are partly owned), but due to the right that attaches to them (the pending freedom), they exit the sale as subjects, leaving the owned part (the remaining share of the slave) to be sold validly. This is unlike a free person, who never enters the sale as a subject at all, so selling his share is void from the start. The passage then defines non-mutaqawwim property: that which is not permissible to benefit from legally, like wine and pigs. Mutaqawwim (valuable) has two meanings: customary (by being acquired) and legal (by permissibility of benefit); the latter is intended here. The free person is excluded from being property because he is not subject to commonness; he is above that. Non-mutaqawwim property is still property but we are commanded to treat it with contempt. The key ruling: if something is not property at all (e.g., a free person), its sale as either object or price is void (batil). If it is property but non-mutaqawwim, and sold for cash (dirhams or dinars), the sale is void because cash is meant to be a medium, not the end; but if sold for a commodity (barter), the sale is corrupt (fasid) and permissible to benefit from it (e.g., use it as fuel). This distinction is based on the hadith about wine: it is non-mutaqawwim for Muslims, and its sale for cash is void, but barter yields a corrupt sale as per Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child from her master, and the master claims the child; she becomes free upon the master's death.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave promised freedom upon the master's death.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract of manumission for a fixed payment, becoming free upon payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when a mudabbar sells his own property?
A) The sale is void because he is not fully free.
B) The sale is valid because he owns the property and the sale is confined to his owned share.
C) The sale is suspended until the master dies.
D) The sale is valid only if the master consents.

Key Takeaway
The sale of an umm walad, mudabbar, or mukatab from themselves is valid because they retain an owned share; this is unlike the sale of a free person which is void.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
7 min
Sale of Non-Mutaqawwim Property: Cash vs Commodity
Distinction in sales of non-mutaqawwim property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sale of non-mutaqawwim property for cash (batil) and for commodity (fasid), and apply the Hanafi rationale.

Core Idea
Non-mutaqawwim property can be bartered but not sold for cash.

What if a Muslim inherits a barrel of wine? Can he sell it? The Hanafi school distinguishes: selling for dirhams is void because cash is the norm for valuation and wine is not valuable for Muslims. However, exchanging it for another non-cash item results in a corrupt sale, and the wine can be used for non-consumption purposes like fuel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and property type
The act is selling non-mutaqawwim property (e.g., wine, pigs). First, confirm that the property is non-mutaqawwim in Islamic law.
Non-mutaqawwim means not permissible to benefit from legally.
2
Determine the consideration
Is the sale for cash (dirhams or dinars) or for a commodity (barter)? This is the key factor.
Cash includes gold, silver, and currency; commodity includes any non-cash goods.
3
Apply the ruling for cash
If sold for cash, the sale is batil (void) because cash is the standard measure of value and treating non-mutaqawwim as equivalent to cash would elevate it, contradicting the command to treat it with contempt.
The contract is void ab initio; no ownership transfers.
4
Apply the ruling for commodity
If sold for a commodity (barter), the sale is fasid (corrupt). The buyer takes possession but the contract is defective. However, benefiting from the non-mutaqawwim item (e.g., using wine as fuel) is permissible after possession.
The sale is not completely void; the buyer can use the item.
5
Understand the rationale
Cash is meant to be a medium of exchange, not an end itself. When non-mutaqawwim property is exchanged for cash, it becomes a substitute for cash, which the sharia prohibits due to the property's contemptible nature. In barter, the non-mutaqawwim property is not being measured against cash, so the prohibition is less severe, resulting in a corrupt sale.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مال غير متقوم (mal ghayr mutaqawwim): Property that is not legally valuable, meaning its benefit is not permissible in Islamic law, e.g., wine, pigs.

What is the ruling on selling non-mutaqawwim property (like wine) in exchange for a commodity (barter)?
A) The sale is void (batil) and the commodity must be returned.
B) The sale is corrupt (fasid) but benefiting from the wine (e.g., as fuel) is permissible after possession.
C) The sale is valid (sahih) if both parties are Muslims.
D) The sale is suspended (mawquf) until the property becomes valuable.

Key Takeaway
Selling non-mutaqawwim property for cash is void, but bartering it for a commodity results in a corrupt sale with permissible benefit.
