Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Bāṭil (Void) Sales: Definition and Consequences
Defines bāṭil and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of a bāṭil sale and identify its key legal consequence (no ownership transfer).

Core Idea
A bāṭil sale is void from its foundation; it produces no legal effects whatsoever.

In Hanafi fiqh, not every invalid contract is the same. A bāṭil (void) sale is fundamentally flawed—its very essence is defective. This lesson unpacks the definition of bāṭil sales and why they differ from merely defective (fāsid) contracts. You will learn the precise legal criteria and the practical outcome: no ownership, no liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of sale
Determine if the object being sold is considered wealth (māl) in Islamic law. If not māl (e.g., blood, dead animals), the sale is bāṭil regardless of price.
Non-māl items have no value in Shariʿah.
2
Check if the object is mutaqawwim
If the object is māl, determine if it is valuable and usable by Muslims (māl mutaqawwim). If it is non-valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim, e.g., wine, impure oil), then check the form of exchange.
Māl ghayr mutaqawwim has legal recognition but not full value.
3
Apply the rule for thaman vs. ʿaraḍ
If the non-valuable object is sold for dirhams or dinars (thaman), the sale is bāṭil. If sold for a commodity (ʿaraḍ), the sale becomes fāsid (covered in next lesson).
The text specifies this distinction explicitly.
4
Understand the legal effect
In a bāṭil sale, there is no transfer of ownership. Even if the buyer takes possession, he does not own the object and is not liable if it perishes. The contract is a nullity.
No legal effects follow from a bāṭil contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكل ما ليس بمال فالبيع فيه باطل سواءٌ جُعِل مبيعاً أو ثمناً. وكل ما هو مال غير متقوم، فإن بيع بالثمن – أي بالدراهم أو الدنانير - فالبيع باطل، وإن بيع بالعرض أو بيع العرض به فالبيع فاسد. [0] قوله : فالبيع باطل ; لأنَّ المقصود من البيع عينُ المبيع ; لأنها هي المنتفع بها لا عين الثمن ; لأنها جعلت وسيلة إليه ; ولهذا يجوز ثبوتُهُ في الذمة، وإذا جُعِلَت الخمرُ مثلاً مبيعة تكون مقصودة، وفيه إعزاز والشرع أمرنا بإهانتها ; ولهذا يبطل بيعها. [٦] قوله : فالبيع فاسد ; في العرض فيملكه بالقبض، فتجب قيمته لوجودِ حقيقة البيع، وهو مبادلة المال بالمال، فإنَّ الخمر عند البعض مال، ولا يملك الخمر لبطلان البيع في الخمر، حتى لو هلكت عند المشتري لا يضمن ; لأنها غير متقومة عند الشرع. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر»). فالباطل هو الذي لا يكون صحيحاً بأصله ووصفه. والفاسد هو الصحيح بأصلِهِ لا بصفته. وعند الشَّافِعِي لا فرق بين الباطل والفاسد، وتحقيق هذا في أصول الفقه. [١] قوله : وتحقيق هذا في أصول الفقه ; قال الأصوليون : الباطل عبارة عن ما يقابل الصحيح، والصحة في العبادات: عبارة عن استحقاق الثواب وسقوط القضاء وموافقة أمر الشارع. وفي المعاملات : عبارة عن ترتب الآثار المطلوبة من ذلك الفعل عليه : كالملك، وعدم. فالبطلان في العبادات عبارة عن عدم استحقاق الثواب، وعدم سقوط القضاء ، موافقة الأمر، وفي المعاملات : عبارة عن خروج ذلك الفعل الباطل عن السببية للثمرات المطلوبة منه.
So everything that is not wealth, its sale is void whether made as a commodity or as a price. And everything that is wealth but non-valuable, if sold for the price (i.e., dirhams or dinars), the sale is void; and if sold for a commodity or a commodity is sold for it, the sale is defective. [0] His saying: 'the sale is void' because the object of sale is the intended thing itself; for it is what is benefited from, not the price itself; for the price is made a means to it; hence it is permissible for it to be established in the dhimmah. If wine, for example, is made the commodity, it is intended, and there is honor in it, and the Law commands us to dishonor it; therefore its sale is void. [6] His saying: 'the sale is defective'; in a commodity, one owns it by possession, so its value is obligatory due to the existence of the essence of the sale, which is the exchange of wealth for wealth. For wine is wealth according to some, but one does not own wine because the sale of wine is void, so if it perishes with the buyer, he is not liable because it is non-valuable according to the Law. This is in 'Majma' al-Anhur'. So the void is that which is not valid in its essence and description. And the defective is that which is valid in its essence but not in its description. According to al-Shafi'i, there is no difference between the void and the defective, and the verification of this is in usul al-fiqh. [1] His saying: 'and the verification of this is in usul al-fiqh'; the usuliyyun said: the void is what is opposite of valid. Validity in acts of worship is: deserving reward, lifting of repetition, and conformity to the command of the Lawgiver. In transactions it is: the entailment of the desired effects of that act, such as ownership and non-ownership. Thus nullity in acts of worship is: not deserving reward, not lifting of repetition, and non-conformity to the command; and in transactions it is: the exit of that void act from being causative for the desired fruits from it.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof lesson explaining the key Hanafi distinction between bāṭil (void) and fāsid (defective) contracts, particularly in sales (bayʿ). The text begins by stating that anything which is not considered wealth (māl) in Islamic law—such as blood, dead animals, or intoxicants—cannot be the subject of a valid sale; any sale involving them is bāṭil, whether they are the commodity or the price. If the item is wealth but non-valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim), like wine or impure items, the ruling depends on the form of exchange: if sold for dirhams or dinars (currency), the sale is bāṭil because the objective is the commodity itself, which the Law commands us to dishonor; but if sold for a commodity (ʿaraḍ) or a commodity is sold for it, the sale is fāsid (defective). The commentary explains that in a fāsid sale, the buyer acquires ownership by taking possession and must pay the market value (qīmah), since the essence of exchange (mubādalah) exists, even though the object lacks full legal value. The author then provides the precise definitions: bāṭil is a contract that is invalid in its very foundation (aṣl) and description (waṣf), so it produces no legal effects; fāsid is valid in essence but defective in description, thus it can transfer ownership upon possession but requires correction. The passage notes that Imam al-Shafiʿī does not distinguish between bāṭil and fāsid, considering any invalid contract as void. The commentary from usul al-fiqh clarifies that validity in transactions means the act entails its intended effects (like ownership), while nullity means the act fails to produce those effects. This distinction is fundamental to Hanafi contract law: a bāṭil contract is a nullity, whereas a fāsid one can be rectified (e.g., by removing the defect).

Key Terms
باطل (bāṭil): A contract that is invalid in its essence and description; it does not transfer ownership and has no legal effect.
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Valuable property that is lawful to use and compensable if destroyed; its sale is valid.
ثمن (thaman): Price, typically referring to dirhams and dinars (currency) in the context of exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi law, if impure oil (which is māl ghayr mutaqawwim) is sold for dirhams, the sale is:
A) Valid and the buyer owns the oil immediately.
B) Bāṭil (void).
C) Fāsid (defective) and the buyer can take possession after paying value.
D) Makrūh but valid.

Key Takeaway
A bāṭil sale is completely ineffective: no ownership, no liability, and the contract cannot be rectified.

Fāsid (Defective) Sales: Definition and Effects
Defines fāsid and its effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of a fāsid sale and explain its legal consequences, including ownership upon possession and liability.

Core Idea
A fāsid sale is valid in essence but flawed in its description; it transfers ownership upon possession but requires compensation.

In Hanafi fiqh, a sale can be flawed without being completely void. A fāsid (defective) sale has a valid foundation but a defect in its description, such as selling a non-valuable item for a commodity. This lesson explores the definition, conditions, and practical effects of fāsid sales, including the critical point that the buyer gains ownership by taking possession and must pay the market value.

Guided Path
1
Identify a sale as fāsid
A sale is fāsid when it meets the essence of a sale (exchange of wealth) but has a defect in description, such as selling māl ghayr mutaqawwim for a commodity (ʿaraḍ) instead of currency.
The defect is in the description (waṣf), not the essence (aṣl).
2
Understand the ownership effect
Unlike a bāṭil sale, a fāsid sale transfers ownership of the object to the buyer upon taking possession (qabḍ). The buyer becomes the owner and can use it, but owes the market value (qīmah).
The sale is not completely void; it has legal effects.
3
Recognize the liability rule
If the object perishes with the buyer before paying, he is liable to pay the market value because he owned it. However, if the object is ghayr mutaqawwim (like wine), it is not compensable—so if it perishes, the buyer is not liable.
The distinction between māl mutaqawwim and ghayr mutaqawwim affects liability.
4
Contrast with bāṭil
In a bāṭil sale, there is no ownership transfer and no liability. In a fāsid sale, ownership transfers and liability applies, but the contract is still defective and requires correction (e.g., by removing the defect).
The practical difference is significant in terms of rights and obligations.

Source Text

[٦] قوله : فالبيع فاسد ; في العرض فيملكه بالقبض، فتجب قيمته لوجودِ حقيقة البيع، وهو مبادلة المال بالمال، فإنَّ الخمر عند البعض مال، ولا يملك الخمر لبطلان البيع في الخمر، حتى لو هلكت عند المشتري لا يضمن ; لأنها غير متقومة عند الشرع. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر»). فالباطل هو الذي لا يكون صحيحاً بأصله ووصفه. والفاسد هو الصحيح بأصلِهِ لا بصفته. وعند الشَّافِعِي لا فرق بين الباطل والفاسد، وتحقيق هذا في أصول الفقه. Mixed Sale: Lawful and Unlawful Items Combined
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mixed sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a mixed sale of lawful and unlawful items, and evaluate the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
In a mixed sale, the entire contract is void according to Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn allow the lawful part if the price is itemized.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about a single transaction containing both a permissible item (e.g., a slave) and an impermissible one (e.g., a free person). The central question: does the unlawful element corrupt the whole contract or only its own part? We trace the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
State the act (mixed sale) and Abu Hanifa's ruling that the entire sale is void (bāṭil). Understand his reasoning: including a non-wealth item (free person, dead animal) as a condition corrupts the entire contract.
Non-wealth (ghayr māl) cannot be sold; making it a condition invalidates the contract entirely.
2
Recognize the Sahibayn's qualification
The two companions hold that if the seller specifies separate prices for the lawful and unlawful components, the lawful part's sale is valid (ṣaḥīḥ). Their reasoning: the transaction (ṣafqah) becomes multiple in meaning, so corruption is limited to the corrupting element only.
Itemization of price is the key condition for validity according to the Sahibayn.
3
Analyze the analogy with marriage
The Sahibayn analogize to a marriage contract combining one's sister (invalid) and a foreign woman (valid). In marriage, the contract is considered multiple, so the valid part stands. They apply the same logic to sales.
The analogy is from the same chapter of transactions; the commentator later rejects it as inappropriate.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The commentator (al-Marghinani?) notes that the analogy is flawed: marriage is not invalidated by corrupting conditions, whereas sale is. In marriage, a condition like 'if you marry my sister' does not void the entire contract, but in sale, such conditions do corrupt the whole.
This distinction is the basis for preferring Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa: the entire mixed sale is void. The Sahibayn's view is a minority opinion not adopted for fatwa.
Students should memorize the mufta bihi for application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : وبيع من ضم إلى حُرٍّ، وذكيَّةٍ ضُمّت إلى ميتة ; لأنّ الحرَّ غير داخل في البيع أصلاً، بكونه غير مال، وبضمه إلى القِنْ جُعِلَ شرطاً لقبول القنّ، وجعل غير المال شرطاً لقبول المبيع فبطل البيع، وكذا الميتة. وعندهما : يصح البيعُ في العبدِ والذكية إن بَيَّنَ الثمن ; لأنَّ الصفقة متعددة معنى بتفصيل الثمن، والفساد بقدر المفسد فلا يتعدّده كما لو جمع بين أخته وأجنبية بالنكاح، هذا ولا يذهب عليك أنَّ هذا التنظير ليس بمحله ; لأن النكاح لا يبطل بالشروط المفسدة، والبيع ليس كذلك، فتأمل (۳).
[2] His saying: 'and the sale of one to whom a free person is joined, and a properly slaughtered animal joined to a dead animal' because the free person is not included in the sale at all, being non-wealth; and by joining him to the slave, he is made a condition for the slave's acceptance, and non-wealth is made a condition for accepting the sold item, so the sale becomes void. Similarly for the dead animal. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the sale of the slave and the properly slaughtered animal is valid if he specifies the price, because the transaction (safqah) is multiple in meaning by the itemization of the price, and corruption is limited to the extent of the corrupting element, so it does not extend to the whole, like the case where one marries his sister and a foreign woman together. But you should not be unaware that this analogy is not appropriate, because marriage is not invalidated by corrupting conditions, while sale is not like that, so reflect.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a transaction that combines a lawful item with an unlawful one. The issue is: if someone sells a slave along with a free person (who cannot be sold because he is not property), or sells a properly slaughtered animal mixed with a dead animal (which is impure and not permissible to sell), is the entire contract valid? The position of Imam Abu Hanīfa (and it is reported as his view) is that the sale is bāṭil (void) in its entirety. The reasoning is that the free person is not māl (wealth) and cannot be part of a sale; including him as a condition for accepting the slave makes non-wealth a condition for the sale, which corrupts the entire contract. Similarly for the dead animal. The two companions (Sāḥibān: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybānī) hold that if the seller specifies the price for each component separately—for example, stating the price for the slave and for the free person separately, even though the free person's price is not enforceable—the transaction is valid (ṣaḥīḥ) for the slave and the slaughtered animal. Their reasoning: the transaction (ṣafqah) becomes multiple in meaning by itemizing the price; the corruption is limited to the extent of the corrupting element (al-fasād bi-qadr al-mufsid). Thus the unlawful part does not invalidate the lawful part. They analogize to marriage of one's sister and a foreign woman in the same contract, where the sister's marriage is invalid but the foreign woman's marriage is valid. However, the commentator notes that this analogy is flawed because marriage is not invalidated by corrupting conditions, whereas sale is. Therefore, the correct position is that of Abu Hanīfa. The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position in the Hanafi school is that such a mixed sale is bāṭil. This lesson highlights the Hanafi principle that bāṭil contracts are null altogether, and that a defect in part can affect the whole if the transaction is indivisible.

Key Terms
ضم (ḍamm): The act of joining or including one item with another in a single sale contract.
صفقة (ṣafqah): The transaction or deal; a single bargaining unit. The Sāḥibayn argue that itemizing the price makes the ṣafqah multiple.
ذكية (dhakiyyah): A properly slaughtered animal that is lawful to eat and sell.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two companions (Sahibayn), if a seller combines a slave and a free person in one sale but specifies separate prices, the sale of the slave is:
A) Bāṭil because the free person cannot be sold.
B) Fāsid (defective) and requires correction.
C) Valid (ṣaḥīḥ).
D) Makrūh but valid.

Key Takeaway
In a mixed sale, the Hanafi school (following Abu Hanifa) rules the entire contract void when an unlawful item is included, because the contract is indivisible; the Sahibayn's view on itemization is not adopted for fatwa.

Batil vs Fasid in Sales: The Hanafi Distinction and Shafi'i Objection
Defines batil and fasid sales and notes Shafi'i no distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define batil and fasid in Hanafi sales law and explain the Shafi'i objection to this distinction.

Core Idea
Batil is invalid in essence and description; fasid is valid in essence but defective in description.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all invalid contracts are treated equally. This lesson unpacks the critical distinction between a void (batil) contract and an invalid (fasid) contract—a distinction that carries significant legal consequences. We will also note that the Shafi'i school rejects this differentiation, considering both as simply void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the Arabic definition: batil and fasid. Batil is described as 'not valid in its essence (asl) and description (wasf)'. Fasid is 'valid in its essence but not in its description'. Grasp the binary: asl and wasf.
Asl refers to the essential pillars (arkan); wasf refers to conditions (shurut) that are not pillars.
2
Contrast the legal effects
Think: if a contract is batil, it produces no legal effects at all—it is as if it never existed. If it is fasid, the contract is fundamentally valid (asl intact) but defective in some attribute (wasf), so it may be rectifiable in some cases (e.g., renegotiating a defective term).
In sales, a fasid contract may be corrected by removing the defect; a batil contract cannot.
3
Note the Shafi'i rejection
The text states that al-Shafi'i sees no difference between batil and fasid. Understand that this is a usul al-fiqh disagreement over how to categorize defective contracts. The Shafi'i school treats all defects as making the contract void.
This distinction has practical consequences in contract enforcement and remedies.
4
Relate to usul al-fiqh
The passage hints that the 'verification' belongs to usul. Recognize that the batil/fasid distinction is rooted in the Hanafi usul concept of 'asl' and 'wasf', which governs how conditions affect rulings.
You will encounter this in later lessons on conditions of contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالباطل هو الذي لا يكون صحيحاً بأصله ووصفه. والفاسد هو الصحيح بأصلِهِ لا بوصفه. وعند الشَّافِعِي له لا فرق بين الباطل والفاسد، وتحقيق هذا في أصول الفقه.
The void (batil) is that which is not valid in its essence and description. The invalid (fasid) is that which is valid in its essence but not in its description. According to al-Shafi'i, there is no distinction between void and invalid, and the verification of this is in Usul al-Fiqh.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage defines two key legal terms in Hanafi fiqh: batil and fasid. Batil means the contract lacks validity in both its essence (asl) and its description (wasf). Fasid means the contract is valid in essence but defective in description. The Shafi'i school does not distinguish between these two categories, considering both as void. The full discussion of this distinction belongs to Usul al-Fiqh.

Key Terms
الباطل (al-bāṭil): A contract that is invalid in both its essence and description, producing no legal effects.
الفاسد (al-fāsid): A contract that is valid in essence but has a defect in description, such as a missing condition that is not essential.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the difference between a batil (void) and a fasid (invalid) sale?
A) Batil is void in essence, fasid is void in description.
B) Batil is valid in essence, fasid is valid in description.
C) Batil is invalid in both essence and description, fasid is valid in essence but defective in description.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a contract may be fasid (defective in description) rather than batil (void in essence), and this distinction has practical remedies.

Combining Free Person or Dead Animal with a Salable Item: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Ruling on selling a free person with a slave or dead animal with slaughtered

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on combining a non-saleable item (free person/dead animal) with a saleable item in one contract, and evaluate the Sahibayn's analogy with marriage.

Core Idea
Combining a non-saleable item voids the entire contract according to Abu Hanifa, but Sahibayn allow partial validity if prices are separated.

This lesson examines a classical intra-Hanafi dispute regarding contracts that combine a permissible item (such as a slave or ritually slaughtered animal) with an impermissible item (a free person or carrion). The disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, turns on the divisibility of the transaction and the effect of including non-property. The source passage also critiques an analogy with marriage, revealing important distinctions between sale and marriage contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
First, understand the two scenarios: (1) selling a free person together with a slave, and (2) selling a ritually slaughtered animal together with a dead animal. In both, the impermissible item is combined with a permissible one in a single contract.
The free person is not property (mal), and the dead animal is najis and non-saleable.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and its basis
Abu Hanifa rules the entire contract void (batil). His reasoning: the free person/dead animal is not included in the sale from the outset; by combining it with the slave/slaughtered animal, the non-saleable item becomes a condition for the acceptance of the saleable item. Making non-property a condition invalidates the contract.
Abu Hanifa treats the combination as a single transaction where the invalid part contaminates the whole.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule the contract valid for the permissible part if the price for each item is specified. They argue that the transaction is multiple in meaning because the prices are itemized; invalidity is limited to the corrupting element and does not extend. They draw an analogy with a marriage contract that combines a permissible woman and an impermissible one (e.g., a sister and a foreign woman).
The Sahibayn treat the contract as divisible when prices are separated.
4
Analyze the critique of the marriage analogy
The commentator points out that the analogy is flawed because marriage is not invalidated by corrupt conditions (shurut mufsida), whereas sale is. In marriage, a corrupt condition does not void the contract, but in sale, corrupt conditions do void it. Therefore, the analogy is not apt.
This highlights a fundamental difference between the two contracts.
5
Determine the consensus point when prices are unnamed
The text notes that if the price for each item is not specified, the contract is void by consensus. Without itemization, the contract is considered a sale by share from the beginning, which is impermissible due to ignorance of the price (jahala).
This shows an agreed boundary: when prices are not separated, both Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn agree on invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع من ضم إلى حُرِّ، وذكيَّةٍ ضُمّت إلى ميتة، وإن سَمَّى ثمن كل واحد. لان الحر غير داخل في البيع أصلاً، بكونه غير مال، وبضمه إلى القِنْ جُعِلَ شرطاً لقبول القنّ، وجعل غير المال شرطاً لقبول المبيع فبطل البيع، وكذا الميتة. وعندهما: يصح البيع في العبد والذكية إن بين الثمن؛ لأن الصفقة متعددة معنى بتفصيل الثمن، والفساد بقدر المفسد فلا يتعدده كما لو جمع بين أخته وأجنبية بالنكاح. هذا ولا يذهب عليك أن هذا التنظير ليس بمحله؛ لأن النكاح لا يبطل بالشروط المفسدة، والبيع ليس كذلك، فتأمل. قوله : وإن ; لم يسمّي فائدة هذا القيد أنَّ في صورة عدم التسمية بطل البيع اتفاقاً؛ لأنه إذا لم يسم ثمن كل منهما، صارَ العقد بيعاً بالحصة ابتداء، وهو لا يجوز بجهالة الثمن.
And the sale of a free person combined [with a slave], and a slaughtered animal combined with a dead one, even if he named the price for each. Because the free person is inherently not included in the sale, not being property, and by combining him with the slave, he is made a condition for the acceptance of the slave, and making non-property a condition for accepting the sold item invalidates the sale; similarly for the dead animal. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the sale is valid for the slave and the slaughtered animal if he specified the price; because the transaction is multiple in meaning by specifying the price, and invalidity is limited to the corrupting element, so it does not extend, as when one combines his sister and a foreign woman in marriage. But be aware that this analogy is not apt; because marriage is not invalidated by corrupting conditions, whereas sale is, so reflect. His statement: 'And if he did not name' — the benefit of this condition is that in the case of not naming, the sale is void by consensus; because if he does not name the price for each, the contract becomes a sale by share from the beginning, which is not permissible due to ignorance of the price.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when a free person is sold together with a slave, or a ritually slaughtered animal with a dead animal in a single contract. According to Abu Hanifa, the contract is void (batil) because the free person is not property, and making non-property a condition for the sale corrupts it. The same applies to the dead animal. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), the sale is valid for the slave and the slaughtered animal if the price for each is specified, because the transaction is divisible. However, if the price is not named, the sale is void by agreement due to ignorance of the price. The text also critiques an analogy with marriage.

Key Terms
ضم (ḍamma): To combine two items in a single sale contract.
ذكية (dhakiyya): An animal that has been ritually slaughtered in accordance with Islamic law.
ميتة (mayta): An animal that died without proper Islamic slaughter; carrion.
صفقة (ṣafqa): Transaction or deal; in this context, the bundle of items sold together.
الفساد بقدر المفسد (al-fasād bi-qadr al-mufsid): Invalidity is limited to the corrupting element; a principle used by Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a free person and a slave are sold together without specifying the price for each?
A) The sale is valid for the slave according to Sahibayn.
B) The sale is void by consensus.
C) The sale is valid but disliked.
D) The sale is valid only if the buyer is unaware.

Key Takeaway
In a single contract combining a saleable item with a non-saleable item, Abu Hanifa voids the entire contract, while Sahibayn allow partial validity with specified prices; but without price specification, all agree the contract is void.

Sale of a Slave Combined with a Mudabbar: Validity and Madhhab Dispute
Ruling on mixed sale of slave and mudabbar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a slave together with a mudabbar or another's slave, explain Zufar's objection, and evaluate the Hanafi proof against the Shafi'i position on selling mudabbar.

Core Idea
A mudabbar cannot be sold, but a mixed sale with a regular slave remains valid for the unrestricted share.

This passage from al-Hidayah tackles a nuanced scenario: a seller combines a slave (qinn) with a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) or with another person's slave in a single sale. The core question is whether the presence of an inalienable or non-owned element invalidates the entire contract. The Hanafi position affirms validity under specific conditions, while Zufar and other schools raise objections. We will unpack the legal reasoning behind these views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and its Condition
Find the central ruling: the sale is 'sahih' (valid) provided the price is allocated to each item (bihissatihi). Note that the author immediately identifies the basis: the mudabbar is considered 'mahall li al-bay'' (a subject of sale) according to some, so its invalidity does not spread.
The condition 'if he names the price for each' ensures the price of the unrestricted slave is known, avoiding gharar.
2
Analyze Zufar's Objection
Zufar invalidates the sale because he views the contract's subject as the entirety (al-majmu'). Since the mudabbar lacks legal eligibility (mahalliyyah) for sale, the contract fails as a whole, similar to the previous case (mentioned earlier in the text).
Zufar analogizes this to selling a free person or an umm walad, where the entire sale is void.
3
Examine the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi argument (wa lana) asserts that mudabbar and umm walad can be sold by court order (qada'an) and mukatab with his consent. Thus they are 'subjects of sale' initially, but then exit due to their impending freedom. The combination is like selling two slaves when one is repossessed – the valid one remains sold. The sale of another's slave is valid as suspended (mawquf).
The key principle: the invalid element does not nullify the valid part when each price is specified.
4
Compare Inter-Madhhab Positions on Mudabbar Sale
The passage contrasts the Shafi'i view (permissible based on the hadith of Jabir), Ahmad's restriction (only for debt), Malik's permissibility after death for debt, and the Hanafi prohibition. Understand the Hanafi reliance on the hadith 'al-mudabbaru la yuba'u wa la yuhabu'.
This comparison shows how the ruling on mixed sale is tied to deeper principles about the mudabbar's status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح في قِنْ ضُمَّ إلى مُدَبَّرٍ ، أَو قِنْ غيره بحصته وإن سَمَّى ثمن كل واحد. لأن المُدَبَّرَ مَحَلٌ للبيع عند البعض، فبطلانه لا يسري إلى غيره. قوله : صح ...الخ ; خلافاً لزفر له ; فإنه لا يصح البيع عنده ; لأنَّ محلَّ العقدِ المجموع، ولا يتصوّر ذلك لانتفاء المحلية في المدبَّر ونحوه: كأم الولد، والمكاتب، وقد جعل قبول العقد فيه شرطاً لصحة العقد في المال، فيفسد كما في الصورة السابقة. ولنا : إن بيع المدبر وأم الولد جائز بالقضاء، وبيع المكاتب برضائه كما ثبت في موضعه، فيصير محلا للبيع، فدخلوا ابتداءً في العقد، ثم خرجوا عنه ; لاستحقاقهم أنفسهم باتصال الحريَّة بهم من وجه فصار جمع العبدِ مع كل منهم بمنزلة بيع عبدين استحق أحدهما، وبيع قنّ الغير يجوز موقوفاً فيصير محلاً للبيع. وبه قال الشافعي له قال : بجواز بيعه وغيره من التصرفات ، قال أحمد له لما روي عن جابرٍ له أنَّ رجلاً أعتق على ماله عن دبر منه، فاحتاج فأخذه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : «مَن يشتريه منّي ، فاشتراه نعيم بن عبد الله بكذا وكذا، فدفعه اليه»، متفق عليه. وعن أحمد الله : إنما يجوز إذا كان على سيده دين. وعند مالك الله له : لا يجوز بيعه حال الحياة، ويجوز بعد الممات إذا كان على مولاه دين. وعندنا : لا يباع ولا يوهب ; لقوله الله : «المدبَّرُ لا يباعُ ولا يوهب ، وهو حر من ثلثه.
And it is valid [to sell] a slave combined with a mudabbar, or another's slave, with its share, if he names the price for each. Because the mudabbar is a subject of sale according to some, so its invalidity does not extend to others. His statement: 'It is valid...' contrary to Zufar, for he does not deem the sale valid because the subject of the contract is the totality, and that is not conceivable due to the lack of eligibility in the mudabbar and similar: like umm walad, and mukatab. And he made the acceptance of the contract in it a condition for the validity of the contract in the property, so it becomes invalid as in the previous case. Our argument: The sale of the mudabbar and umm walad is permissible by judicial decree, and the sale of the mukatab with his consent, as established in its place, so they become subjects of sale. They enter the contract initially, then exit because they become entitled to themselves due to the connection of freedom from one aspect, so the combination of the slave with each of them is like selling two slaves when one is repossessed. And the sale of another's slave is permissible as suspended, so it becomes a subject of sale. The Shafi'i holds its permissibility based on the hadith from Jabir that a man had a mudabbar, needed money, and the Prophet bought it. Ahmad said it is only permissible if the master has debt. Malik said it is not permissible during life but after death if the master has debt. Our view: It cannot be sold or given as a gift because the Prophet said: 'The mudabbar cannot be sold nor given, and he is free from one-third.'
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage deals with selling a slave combined with a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon master's death) or with another person's slave. The ruling is that it is valid if the price is specified for each. The author explains that contrary to Zufar, the mudabbar can be a subject of sale because it is legally sold by court order. Then the text compares opinions of Shafi'i, Ahmad, Malik, and the Hanafi position. The Hanafi school holds that a mudabbar cannot be sold or gifted based on the hadith 'The mudabbar cannot be sold or given, and he is free from one-third.'

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whom the master has promised freedom upon the master's death, with the promise taking effect as a bequest from one-third of the estate.
قن (qinn): A male slave who is fully owned by the master.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, and is thereby freed upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling a mudabbar?
A) Permissible under any circumstances.
B) Impermissible; it cannot be sold or given.
C) Permissible only if specified in the contract.
D) Permissible if the master has no other assets.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds the validity of a mixed sale with a mudabbar when each share's price is specified, based on the principle that the ineligible element does not nullify the eligible part, while maintaining the general prohibition on selling a mudabbar alone.

The Validity of Selling a Qin Combined with a Mudabbar
Ruling and proof for valid sale of qin with mudabbar when price stated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a qin together with a mudabbar when prices are specified and explain the rationale and the dispute with Zufar.

Core Idea
The qin's sale remains valid even if combined with a mudabbar; the invalid part does not infect the valid one.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a key principle in Hanafi sales law: when a valid item is sold together with an item that is not fully valid for sale, the contract is not automatically void. The scholar explains the ruling, its proof, and responses to objections from Zufar and other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being sold? A qin (fully owned slave) combined with a mudabbar (slave whose manumission is tied to master's death). The ruling: the sale of the qin is valid (sahih) when the price of each is specified.
The mudabbar itself is not sold; only the qin is transferred.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Al-mudabbaru la yuba'u wa la yuhabu' (The mudabbar is not sold nor given). It prohibits selling the mudabbar alone, but does not forbid combining it with a valid item.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to Zufar's objection
Zufar held the entire sale is fasid because the mudabbar lacks legal capacity for sale (mahall). The Hanafi response: the mudabbar can be a subject for sale in certain contexts (e.g., judicial sale), and its invalidity does not spread to the valid part. The combination is like selling two slaves where one is later found to be owned by another; the valid sale remains.
This is based on istihsan (juristic preference).
4
Note cross-madhhab positions
Shafi'i allows selling a mudabbar outright; Ahmad permits it conditionally (if master has debt); Malik allows it only after the master's death. The Hanafi school holds it cannot be sold alone but can be combined.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) affects whether the contract is entirely void.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Zufar's view would void the entire sale, leaving the buyer with nothing. The Hanafi view upholds the qin's sale, protecting the buyer's right to the valid item.
The mufta bihi (operative position) is the Hanafi view that the qin's sale is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن سَمَّى ثمن كل واحد. وصح في قِنْ ضُمَّ إلى مُدَبَّرٍ ، أَو قِنْ غيره بحصته) : لأَنَّ المُدَبَّرَ مَحَلٌ للبيع عند البعض (۱) ، فبطلانه لا يسري إلى غيره [٢] قوله : صح ...الخ ; خلافاً لزفر له ; فإنه لا يصح البيع عنده ; لأنَّ محلَّ العقدِ المجموع، ولا يتصوّر ذلك لانتفاء المحلية في المدبَّر ونحوه: كأم الولد، والمكاتب، وقد جعل قبول العقد فيه شرطاً لصحة العقد في المال، فيفسد كما في الصورة السابقة. ولنا : إن بيع المدبر وأم الولد جائز بالقضاء، وبيع المكاتب برضائه كما ثبت في موضعه، فيصير محلا للبيع، فدخلوا ابتداءً في العقد، ثم خرجوا عنه ; لاستحقاقهم أنفسهم باتصال الحريَّة بهم من وجه فصار جمع العبدِ مع كل منهم بمنزلة بيع عبدين استحق أحدهما ، وبيع قنّ الغير يجوز موقوفاً فيصير محلاً للبيع. [٣] قوله : لأن ... الخ ; فإنَّ الشافعي له قال : بجواز بيعه وغيره من التصرفات ، قال أحمد له لما روي عن جابرٍ له أنَّ رجلاً أعتق على ماله عن دبر منه، فاحتاج فأخذه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : «مَن يشتريه منّي ، فاشتراه نعيم بن عبد الله بكذا وكذا، فدفعه اليه (۲)، متفق عليه. وعن أحمد الله : إنما يجوز إذا كان على سيده دين. وعند مالك الله له : لا يجوز بيعه حال الحياة، ويجوز بعد الممات إذا كان على مولاه وعندنا : لا يباع ولا يوهب ; لقوله الله : «المدبَّرُ لا يباعُ ولا يوهب ، وهو حر من ثلث المال»، أخرجه الدَّارَ قُطْنِيُّ (۱) من حديث ابن عمر له ; ولأنَّ التدبير سبب الحريَّة ; لأنَّ الحريَّة تثبت بعد الموت، ولا سبب غيره. ثم جعله سبباً في الحال أولى لوجوده في الحال، وعدمه بعد الموت ; لكونه كلاماً عرضاً لا يبقى ، فتعين أن يكون سبباً في الحال، وان شئت زيادة التفصيل في هذا المقام فارجع إلى شروح «الكنز»، وحواشي «الهداية»
And if he names the price of each one. And it is valid for a slave (qin) added to a mudabbar or another slave for its share [i.e., the share of the qin]. [Commentary: Because the mudabbar is a subject for sale according to some, so its invalidity does not spread to others...]
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states that if a slave (qin) is sold together with a mudabbar (a slave whose manumission is tied to the master's death) or with another slave not belonging to the seller, the sale of the qin is valid provided the price of each item is specified. The reason: the mudabbar, though not a valid subject of sale in Hanafi law, becomes a valid subject in the context of a composite contract because it is capable of being sold according to some scholars (like the Shafi'is). The Hanafi proof: the hadith 'The mudabbar is not sold nor given' indicates it cannot be sold alone, but it can be combined. The dispute with Zufar is addressed: he says the whole contract is void because the mudabbar lacks legal capacity for sale. Our (Hanafi) response: the sale of a mudabbar is valid judicially (when necessary), and the combination is like selling two slaves where one is later found to be owned by someone else. The passage also discusses positions of Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Malik.

Key Terms
مُدَبَّر (mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is conditional upon the master's death; treated as partially free.
قِن (qin): A fully owned slave with no right to freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale of both is valid.
B) The sale of the qin is valid; the mudabbar is not sold.
C) The entire sale is invalid (fasid).
D) The sale is suspended until the mudabbar is freed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds the validity of the qin's sale when combined with a mudabbar, provided the price of each is specified, and the invalid part does not infect the valid one.

Sale of Commodity for Wine: Fasid vs Batil
Ruling on sale of commodity for wine and its opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fasid and batil in a sale involving wine and state the ownership consequences for the commodity and the wine.

Core Idea
In a sale involving wine, the commodity is owned with compensation (fasid), but wine is never owned (batil).

In Hanafi law, contracts involving prohibited substances are not all treated equally. The distinction between fasid (voidable) and batil (void) turns on the nature of the consideration. This passage shows how a commodity that is otherwise valid is treated when exchanged for wine, which is not considered property for Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two opposite sales: commodity for wine, and wine for commodity. Note that the ruling differs for each side of the transaction.
2
Classify each contract side
For the commodity side, the sale is fasid (voidable). For the wine side, the sale is batil (void). Understand the basis: wine is not mal mutaqawwim.
Fasid affects the commodity, batil affects the wine.
3
Determine ownership and compensation
Under fasid, the commodity becomes owned by the buyer upon possession (qabd), but the buyer must pay its market value (qimah). Under batil, the wine is never owned by the buyer; it remains the seller's property (though ritually impure).
Ownership transfers for the commodity, not for the wine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفسد بيع العَرَضِ بالخمر، وعكسه : أي البيعُ فاسدٌ فِي العَرَضِ حتى يَحِبَ قيمته عند القبض ، ويُمْلَكُ هو بالقبض، لكنَّ البيع في الخمرِ باطل حتى لا يُمْلَكَ عين الخمر.
And the sale of a commodity for wine is fasid, and its opposite [i.e., wine for commodity] is [also fasid in the commodity] meaning: the sale is fasid in the commodity, so its value becomes due upon taking possession, and it is owned by possession, but the sale in the wine is batil, so the substance of the wine is not owned.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This is a ruling statement. It distinguishes between fasid (imperfect/voidable) and batil (void) contracts in the context of a barter involving wine. When a commodity ('ard) is sold for wine, the contract regarding the commodity is fasid: it transfers ownership of the commodity upon delivery, but the price (wine) is not valid consideration. The buyer becomes owner of the commodity but must pay its value (qimah). Conversely, the sale of wine for a commodity is batil: no ownership of the wine transfers, since wine is not property. The ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle that contracts involving non-property are batil, while contracts with a defect in the price are fasid.

Key Terms
عَرَض ('arad): A commodity other than gold, silver, or money; movable property.
خمر (khamr): Wine; intoxicating drink from grapes; not considered mal mutaqawwim in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of selling a commodity for wine?
A) Both the commodity and wine become owned by their new possessors.
B) The commodity becomes owned but the buyer must pay its value.
C) Neither item is owned due to the prohibition of wine.
D) The sale is suspended until the wine is disposed of.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a sale of a commodity for wine is fasid (ownership transfers, value owed), but the sale of wine for a commodity is batil (no ownership of wine).

Combined Waqf Sale and Wine Exchange: Fasid vs Batil
Rulings on combined waqf sale and wine exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a fasid and batil sale in Hanafi fiqh and state the validity of combined owned-waqf property sale.

Core Idea
Selling goods for wine is fasid, not batil; combined waqf-owner sale is valid in the owned portion.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, we encounter two distinct sale scenarios. First, when owned property is combined with waqf property and sold together, the sale is valid in the owned portion—a nuanced position with intra-madhhab difference. Second, the exchange of a commodity for wine is ruled fasid (voidable), while the reverse exchange (wine for commodity) is batil (void). Understanding the precise categories is crucial for correct application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct rulings: (1) selling a composite property that includes both owned and waqf portions; (2) exchanging a commodity for wine and vice versa.
2
Classify each ruling
For the combined sale: the ruling is that the sale is valid (sahih) in the owned portion, though there is a contrary narration. For the wine exchange: selling commodity for wine is fasid, and selling wine for commodity is batil.
Fasid vs batil distinction is central.
3
Understand the fasid-batil distinction
In a fasid sale, ownership of the commodity transfers upon possession and the buyer owes its value. In a batil sale, no ownership transfers at all.
Reason: wine is impure and cannot be owned, so selling it is inherently void.
4
Recall the Hanafi position on combined sale
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the sale is valid in the owned portion because waqf is considered mal (property) and the sale operates on the owned share.
This view is preferred by al-'Ayni and others.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a narration (from Abu al-Layth) that the combined sale is fasid in the owned portion, analogizing to selling a free man with a slave. However, the stronger view is validity.
This ikhtilaf has no effect on the other rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع/ البيع الفاسد والمكروه

كملك ضم إلى وقف في الصحيح. وفسد بيع العرض بالخمر، وعكسه) : أي البيع فاسد في العرض حتى يجب قيمته عند القبض، ويملك هو بالقبض، لكن البيع في الخمر باطل حتى لا يملك عين الخمر. ثلث المال، أخرجه الدارقطني من حديث ابن عمر له؛ ولأن التدبير سبب الحرية؛ لأن الحرية تثبت بعد الموت، ولا سبب غيره. ثم جعله سبباً في الحال أولى لوجوده في الحال، وعدمه بعد الموت؛ لكونه كلاماً عرضاً لا يبقى، فتعين أن يكون سبباً في الحال، وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل في هذا المقام فارجع إلى شروح «الكنز»، وحواشي «الهداية». [١] قوله: كملك...الخ؛ أي كما صح البيع في ملك ضم إلى وقف في الصحيح بالنظر إلى أصله الذي هو حبس العين على ملك الواقف، فحينئذ يجوز بيع الملك المضموم إليه بحصته، وقيل: لا يصح. ونقل في «مجمع الأنهر» عن «الفرائد»: «هذا في غير المسجد، أما في المسجد فلا يصح في الملك المضموم إليه، فلذا لا يصح بيع قرية لم يستثن منها المساجد والمقابر، وفيه كلام؛ لأنه يصح في الملك بصرف الكلام إلى الاستثناء المعنوي، وهو الأصح، كما في «المحيط».». وقال العلامة العيني في «شرح الكنز»: «وأما في الملك والوقف، فروايتان: في رواية: تفسد في الملك؛ لأن البيع لا ينعقد على الوقف، فصار كما لو جمع بين عبد وحر، ذكره الفقيه أبو الليث في «نوازله». والأصح أنه يجوز في الملك؛ لأن الوقف مال؛ ولهذا ينتفع به انتفاع الأموال.
Book of Sales / Invalid and Disliked Sales. Like ownership combined with waqf is valid. And the sale of a commodity for wine is invalid, and its opposite. That is, the sale is fasid regarding the commodity until its value becomes due upon taking possession, and he owns it by possession, but the sale regarding wine is batil such that the specific wine is not owned. One-third of the wealth; al-Daraqutni narrated it from the hadith of Ibn 'Umar... And because tadbir is a cause of freedom... Then he said: 'Like ownership combined with waqf...' i.e., just as the sale of ownership combined with waqf is valid in the sound view...
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage from the chapter of invalid sales in Al-Hidayah presents two rulings. First: when owned property is combined with waqf property and sold together, the sale is valid in the owned portion because the waqf is a mal (asset) and can be benefited from, though there is disagreement. The relied-upon position is that the sale is valid in the owned part, and the waqf portion remains. Second: selling a commodity (such as cloth) in exchange for wine is fasid (voidable)—the buyer takes possession of the commodity and must pay its value, but the wine is not owned because it is najis. Conversely, selling wine for a commodity is batil (void) and no ownership transfers. The passage also includes a digression on tadbir (a slave's emancipation after death) citing a hadith from al-Daraqutni, but this is not central to the sales ruling. The commentary by 'Ayni clarifies that the sale of a village including mosques and cemeteries is valid due to customary exception, as the mosque is not a mal.

Key Terms
وقف (waqf): A charitable endowment where the corpus is retained and its usufruct is dedicated to a religious or charitable purpose.
عرض ('arad): A commodity or movable property other than gold, silver, or currency.
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is defective due to a deficiency in its conditions or subject matter, but not fundamentally prohibited; ownership transfers upon possession but with an obligation to return or pay value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on selling a commodity (e.g., cloth) in exchange for wine?
A) The sale is batil (void) and nothing is owned.
B) The sale is fasid (voidable); the buyer owns the commodity upon taking possession but must pay its value.
C) The sale is sahih (valid) and both parties own what they receive.
D) The sale is makruh (disliked) but valid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the direction of exchange matters: wine as price makes a sale fasid, while wine as commodity makes it batil; combined waqf-owner sales are valid in the owned portion because waqf is considered property.

Ruling on Selling Uncaptured Fish or Fish in a Pen
States prohibition of selling uncaptured fish or fish in a pen not deliverable without trick

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fish in a pen and identify the key conditions of ownership and deliverability that determine validity.

Core Idea
A sale of fish is invalid if they are not owned or not deliverable without a trick; ownership and deliverability are decisive.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a sale depends on the seller owning the item and being able to deliver it. This lesson examines a specific case: fish that have not been caught (hence not owned) or fish that have been caught but placed in a pen from which they cannot be taken without extra effort. The matn clarifies when such a sale is permissible and when it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The text mentions two scenarios: (1) selling fish that have not been caught, and (2) selling fish that have been caught and thrown into a pen. Note the initial ruling: 'la yajuz' (invalid) for both.
The second scenario has subcases based on deliverability.
2
Understand the condition of ownership
Uncaptured fish are not owned by anyone (still in the public domain). Therefore, a sale is invalid because the seller does not own what he sells.
Ownership is a fundamental condition for a valid sale.
3
Understand the condition of deliverability
Even if fish are owned (caught), they must be deliverable. If they are in a pen and cannot be taken without a trick (hilah), the sale is invalid. If they can be taken without a trick, the sale is valid.
Deliverability means the buyer can take possession without unusual effort.
4
Apply the exception
The passage adds an exception: if fish entered the pen on their own and the entrance is not blocked, the sale is invalid even if they can be taken without a trick. Why? Because they are not considered to be in the seller's possession.
This prevents a loophole: fish entering on their own are still considered wild.
5
Note the buyer's option of sight
The commentary mentions that when the sale is valid, the buyer has khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight). Seeing the fish in water does not count as proper sighting because they look different out of water.
This protects the buyer from unseen defects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية

ولم يجز بيع سمك لم يُصَد، أو صيدَ وأُلْقِيَ في حَظِيرَةٍ لا يُؤْخَذُ منها بلا حيلة، وصح إن أُخِذَ منها بلا حيلة إلا إذا دخل بنفسه ولم يُسَدَّ مدخله.

[قوله: في حظيرة؛ قال في «المصباح المنير»: يقال لما حظر به على الغنم وغيرها من الشجر ليحفظها ويمنعها حظيرة، وجمعها حظائر. وفي «المنتخب»: حظيرة: جاي كه خرما خشك كنند و محوطه از جوب دني كه ازبر؛ أي حیوانات سازد. وهكذا في منتهى الأرب.
قوله: وصح... الخ؛ لأنه ملكه ومقدور التسليم، ويثبت للمشتري خيار الرؤية، ولا يعتد برؤيته في الماء؛ لأنه يتفاوت في الماء وخارجه، صرح به العلامة الزيلعي.
قوله: إلا إذا... الخ؛ استثناء منقطع من قوله: «صح إن أخذ منها بلا حيلة»، وقال في «البحر»: وإذا دخل السمك الحظيرة باحتياله ملكه، وكان بيعه على...
Cream of the End for the Support of Care. And it is not permitted to sell fish that has not been caught, or that has been caught and thrown into a pen from which it cannot be taken without a trick; and it is valid if taken from it without a trick, except when it entered by itself and its entrance is not blocked. [Commentary: Regarding 'pen', it is said... Commentary: 'And it is valid...' because it is his property and capable of delivery, and the buyer has the option of sight, and his sight in the water is not considered because it differs in water and outside... Commentary: 'Except when...' is a disconnected exception from the statement 'It is valid if taken without a trick'...]
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage from the book 'Zubdat al-Nihayah' (a commentary on al-Hidayah) presents the ruling on selling fish. The matn states that it is not permissible to sell fish that have not been caught (i.e., still in the water and not owned) or fish that have been caught but then placed in a pen (hazirah) from which they cannot be taken without a trick (hilah). However, if they can be taken from the pen without any need for a trick, the sale is valid. An exception is made when the fish entered the pen on its own and the entrance has not been sealed—in that case, the sale is not valid because they are not considered in one's possession. The commentary explains that the pen is an enclosure made of wood or branches to protect animals. The ruling rests on the principle that the seller must own and be able to deliver the item; uncaptured fish are not owned, and fish in a closed pen are not deliverable. The buyer also has the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah), and seeing the fish in water does not count as valid sighting due to differences in appearance.

Key Terms
حظيرة (hazirah): An enclosure made of branches or wood to protect animals or store goods, often used for fish or livestock.
حيلة (hilah): A trick, artifice, or special effort to obtain something; in this context, the fish cannot be taken without such an effort.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of sight: the buyer's right to cancel a sale after seeing the purchased item if it was not previously seen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a sale of fish that have been caught and placed in a pen valid?
A) Always, once they are in the pen.
B) Only if the fish are dead.
C) If the fish can be taken from the pen without a trick (hilah).
D) Only if the fish entered the pen on their own.

Key Takeaway
For a sale of fish to be valid, the seller must own the fish and the fish must be deliverable without a trick; fish that entered an enclosure on their own are not considered owned.

Conditions for Valid Sale of Fish in an Enclosure
Commentary on conditions for valid sale of fish in enclosure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the Hanafi school permits the sale of fish in an enclosure and identify when the sale is invalid due to lack of ownership or deliverability.

Core Idea
Fish in an enclosure are only validly sold if owned and deliverable without trickery; mere confinement is insufficient.

The passage discusses the permissibility of selling fish that are not yet caught or are confined. The key factors are whether the fish are considered owned (مملوك) and whether the seller can deliver them without resorting to tricks (حيلة). The commentator explains the implications of the fish entering on their own versus being placed, and the effect of blocking the entrance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Find the statement that selling uncaptured fish or fish in an enclosure is not permissible unless certain conditions are met.
The text begins with 'ولم يجز بيعُ سمك لم يُصَد' - it is not permissible to sell fish that have not been caught.
2
Determine the condition of ownability
Fish must be owned by the seller. Ownership is established by actually catching the fish or by active human intervention such as blocking the entrance after the fish enter.
The text distinguishes fish that enter on their own without entrance blocked vs. when the seller blocks the entrance.
3
Check deliverability without trick
The fish must be deliverable without a trick (حيلة). If the fish can be taken easily, the sale is valid; if a trick is required, it is invalid.
The text says: 'وصح إن أخذ منها بلا حيلة' - valid if taken without trick.
4
Note the exception
If fish enter on their own and the entrance is not blocked, the sale is invalid even if they can be taken without trick, because they are not considered owned.
The text says: 'إلا إذا دخل بنفسه ولم يُسَدَّ مدخله' - except if it entered by itself and its entrance was not blocked.
5
Understand delivery and option of seeing
The buyer has the option of seeing the fish, and sighting in water is not considered because the fish may appear differently outside water.
The text mentions 'يثبت للمشتري خيار الرؤية' and that sighting in water does not count.

And it is not permissible to sell fish that have not been caught, or that have been caught and thrown into an enclosure from which they cannot be taken without a trick, and it is valid if taken from it without a trick, except if it entered by itself and its entrance was not blocked. But it is not sold because of a right that attaches; that does not cause the contract to be void in what is joined to it, like the manager and the like, unlike the mosque, where the contract is void in what is joined to it, because it is not property, so it becomes like a free person. If one sold a village and did not exclude the mosques and graveyards, it is not valid for what we have mentioned. End. And our teacher al-Allama al-Tahtawi said: And al-Shaykh Zayn said in al-Bahr: It is not problematic, i.e., regarding the nullity of ownership when it is joined to a functioning mosque, what is in al-Muhit that if one sold a village and did not exclude the mosques and graveyards therein, the correct view is validity of ownership, because what is in it of mosques and graveyards is customarily excluded. This is the opinion of al-Allama Nuh, and what is in al-Bahr is more appropriate because what is known by custom is like what is stipulated. End. And Allah knows best what is correct. [1] His statement: in an enclosure; he said in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'It is said: what is enclosed for sheep and other animals from trees to protect them and prevent them, an enclosure, and its plural is enclosures.' End. And in al-Muntakhab: 'Hazira: a place where dates are dried and an enclosure made of wood or something to protect animals.' End. And similarly in Muntaha al-Arab. [2] His statement: and it is valid...etc., because it is his property and deliverable, and the buyer has the option of seeing, and sighting it in water is not considered because it varies in water and outside it. This is stated by al-Allama al-Zayla'i. [3] His statement: except if...etc., is a disjunctive exception from his statement: 'valid if taken from it without a trick.' And he said in al-Bahr: 'If the fish entered the enclosure by its own trick, he owns it, and its sale is on...' Even if it entered by itself and its entrance was blocked, its sale is permissible because blocking the entrance is a voluntary act that causes ownership, so it becomes captured. Know that he arranged many issues in one strand, and said: it is not permissible, but he did not clarify whether the sale is void or defective, and I will clarify that, Allah willing: Regarding the fish that has not been caught, the sale in it should be void if with dirhams and dinars, and defective if with goods, because it is non-valuable property, since value is by acquisition. The detail: if he delivers it, it is valid, otherwise no. And it is said: not at all, due to lack of acquisition. The disagreement is when he did not prepare it; if he prepares it, he owns it by consensus; if it gathers without his action, he does not own it, whether he can take it without a trick or not. End. [1] His statement: and blocked its entrance; until it becomes such that it cannot exit, so he has taken it, like if it fell into a net, so its sale is permissible. [2] His statement: causes ownership; as if it fell into his net. And in Sharh al-Wafi: its sale is not permissible because blocking is not acquisition, so it is like a bird that fell into a person's house and he blocked the door and window; it does not become acquired by him until he takes it. Mentioned by al-Allama al-Tahtawi from al-Allama Nuh. [3] His statement: arranged...etc.; he likened issues to pearls, and the expression to a string, and the mention to a poem. Its elegance is not hidden to one who has a taste for the science of meanings and rhetoric. [4] His statement: defective; there is discussion about it, because it is a sale of a non-existent thing, and the non-existent is not property, so its sale should be void. This is stated by al-Allama al-Shami. And al-Zahidi said in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri: As for when they gather by themselves, its sale is void in any case, due to lack of ownership. This is stated by the experts.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling for selling fish that are not yet caught or are confined in an enclosure. The original ruling states that it is not permissible to sell fish that have not been caught (i.e., still in the water and not owned) or fish that have been caught but then placed in an enclosure from which they cannot be taken without a trick (حيلة). However, if the fish can be taken from the enclosure without a trick, the sale is valid. An exception is made if the fish entered the enclosure on its own and the entrance was not blocked—then the sale is not valid because the fish are not considered owned. The commentator explains that the sale of such fish is considered valid only if they are effectively in the possession of the seller and deliverable. The buyer also has the right to see the fish, and previous sighting in water does not count because the fish's appearance may differ. Further, the text discusses the effect of blocking the entrance: if the seller blocks the entrance, it is a voluntary act that causes ownership, making the sale permissible. Then the commentator addresses whether such sales are void (باطل) or defective (فاسد). For uncaptured fish, if sold for gold or silver (dirhams/dinars), it is void; if sold for goods (عرض), it is defective. This distinction is because fish that are not owned are not considered valuable property (مال متقوم) since value requires acquisition (إحراز). The commentator also notes a disagreement: if the seller did not prepare the enclosure, the ruling differs; but if he prepared it, he owns the fish by consensus. A side discussion involves selling a village that includes mosques and graveyards: some scholars say the sale is valid because they are customarily excluded, while others say it is invalid because they are not property. The preferred view is validity due to custom. The passage uses elegant comparisons and is rich in Hanafi legal reasoning.

Key Terms
حظيرة (ḥaẓīrah): An enclosure made for animals, especially sheep, to protect them; also a place for drying dates. In this context, it is a fenced area where fish are kept.
حيلة (ḥīlah): A trick or stratagem. In this passage, it refers to any means required to retrieve the fish from the enclosure. If the fish cannot be taken without a trick, the sale is invalid.
مُخْرَزاً (mukhrazan): Something that has been caught or secured. The fish becomes 'mukhraz' (caught) when the entrance is blocked by human action, thus making the sale valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what makes the sale of fish in an enclosure valid when the fish entered on their own?
A) The fish being in the enclosure for more than a day.
B) The seller blocking the entrance after they enter.
C) The seller having prior intent to sell.
D) The fish being sold for goods rather than currency.

Key Takeaway
For a valid sale of fish in an enclosure, the fish must be owned (actively captured or entrance blocked) and deliverable without a trick.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Void vs Defective: The Hanafi Distinction for Selling Uncaptured Fish
Commentary on bāṭil vs fāsid distinction for uncaptured fish

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a void (bāṭil) and defective (fāsid) sale of uncaptured fish based on the medium of exchange and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Sale of uncaptured fish is bāṭil for currency but fāsid for goods; some scholars say bāṭil in all cases because non-existent property cannot be sold.

The text raises an important distinction: when uncaptured fish are sold, the ruling may differ depending on whether the sale is for dirhams/dinars (currency) or for goods (ʿaraḍ). The commentator argues that the sale is bāṭil (void) in the first case and fāsid (defective) in the second, because fish that are not owned are non-valuable property. However, other Hanafi scholars such as al-Shami and al-Zahidi maintain that such sales are always bāṭil, as the fish are technically non-existent as property at the time of contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling for uncaptured fish
The text states that the sale of fish that have not been caught is not permissible. Then it asks: is this bāṭil or fāsid?
The commentator is clarifying ambiguity in the original text.
2
Understand the distinction based on medium of exchange
For currency (gold/silver), the sale is bāṭil because the fish are non-valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim) and currency requires strict ownership. For goods, it is fāsid because goods allow rectification through delivery.
The key concept is taqwīm (valuation) established by iḥrāz (acquisition).
3
Examine the opposing view
Al-Shami and al-Zahidi argue that the sale is bāṭil in all cases because the fish are non-existent as property at contract time.
Their proof: selling a non-existent thing is unanimously bāṭil.
4
Follow the reasoning for the distinction
The text's reasoning: currency transactions require exact counterparts (ribawi), while goods can be exchanged for non-valuable property with the possibility of subsequent delivery making the contract valid.
This is based on the principle that a fāsid sale can be rectified by delivery of the object.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If bāṭil, the contract is null ab initio and cannot be rectified; if fāsid, the buyer has the right to take delivery and the sale becomes valid upon delivery.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon view) in later Hanafi scholarship is that the sale is bāṭil in all cases, as stated by al-Shami.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling for selling fish that are not yet caught or are confined in an enclosure. The original ruling states that it is not permissible to sell fish that have not been caught (i.e., still in the water and not owned) or fish that have been caught but then placed in an enclosure from which they cannot be taken without a trick (حيلة). However, if the fish can be taken from the enclosure without a trick, the sale is valid. An exception is made if the fish entered the enclosure on its own and the entrance was not blocked—then the sale is not valid because the fish are not considered owned. The commentator explains that the sale of such fish is considered valid only if they are effectively in the possession of the seller and deliverable. The buyer also has the right to see the fish, and previous sighting in water does not count because the fish's appearance may differ. Further, the text discusses the effect of blocking the entrance: if the seller blocks the entrance, it is a voluntary act that causes ownership, making the sale permissible. Then the commentator addresses whether such sales are void (باطل) or defective (فاسد). For uncaptured fish, if sold for gold or silver (dirhams/dinars), it is void; if sold for goods (عرض), it is defective. This distinction is because fish that are not owned are not considered valuable property (مال متقوم) since value requires acquisition (إحراز). The commentator also notes a disagreement: if the seller did not prepare the enclosure, the ruling differs; but if he prepared it, he owns the fish by consensus. A side discussion involves selling a village that includes mosques and graveyards: some scholars say the sale is valid because they are customarily excluded, while others say it is invalid because they are not property. The preferred view is validity due to custom. The passage uses elegant comparisons and is rich in Hanafi legal reasoning.

Key Terms
باطل (bāṭil): Void; a contract that is null and void ab initio, cannot be corrected by subsequent delivery.
فاسد (fāsid): Defective; a contract that is irregular due to a defect that can be cured, e.g., by subsequent delivery of the object.
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Valuable property; property that has legal value and can be sold. Value is established through acquisition (iḥrāz).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon view) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling uncaptured fish for goods?
A) It is fāsid (defective) and can be rectified by delivery.
B) It is bāṭil (void) in all cases.
C) It is valid only if the seller prepares the enclosure.
D) It is makruh (disliked) but not void.

Key Takeaway
The sale of uncaptured fish is generally bāṭil (void) in the Hanafi school, regardless of medium of exchange, because the object is non-existent property; the distinction between bāṭil and fāsid is only academic.

Selling Uncaptured Fish: Batil or Fasid?
Ruling on uncaptured fish with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on selling uncaptured fish and distinguish between batil and fasid based on payment type, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
A sale of uncaptured fish is not automatically batil; Hanafis debate whether it is batil or fasid depending on the payment type.

This passage tackles a nuanced issue in Hanafi fiqh: the sale of fish that have not been captured. The author clarifies that the base text's blanket 'not permissible' requires unpacking. We will examine the distinction between batil and fasid in this context, and explore the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling uncaptured fish. The author states it is batil if sold for cash (dirhams/dinars) and fasid if sold for goods.
2
Understand the key concept: taqawwum bil-ihraz
Validity of sale requires the item to be valuable (mutaqawwim), which requires possession (ihraz). Uncaptured fish are not possessed, hence not valuable.
Ihraz means physical control or enclosure.
3
Follow the author's distinction
Cash sale: batil because fish is non-existent as property. Goods sale: fasid because goods are valuable and delivery may cure the defect.
The fasid ruling allows the sale to become valid if the fish are delivered.
4
Analyze the opposing view
Other Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Shami, al-Zahidi) argue that the fish is completely non-existent (ma'dum), so the sale is batil irrespective of payment type.
This is also the view of the majority (al-mahirun) according to the author.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah)
Under the fasid ruling, if the fish are delivered, the sale becomes valid and the buyer owns the fish. Under the batil ruling, no cure is possible; the contract is void ab initio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أَنَّهُ نَظَمَ كثيراً من المسائل في سلك واحد، وقال: لم يجز، لكن لم يُبَيِّنْ أنَّ البيع باطل أو فاسد، وأنا أُبين ذلك إن شاء الله تعالى : ففي السمك الذي لم يُصَدْ ينبغي أن يكون البيعُ فيه باطلاً إذا كان بالدراهم والدنانير، ويكون فاسداً إذا كان بالعرض ؛ لأنه مال غير متقوم ; لأَنَّ التَّقَومَ بالإحراز التفصيل : أي إذا سلَّمه صح وإلا لا ، وقيل : لا مطلقاً; لعدم الإحراز، والخلاف فيما إذا لم يهيئها ، فإن هياها ملكه إجماعاً ، فإن اجتمع بغير صنعه لم يملكه سواء أمكنه أخذه من غير حيلةٍ أو لا». انتهى [١] قوله : وسد مدخله ; حتى صار بحيث لا يقدر على الخروج، فقد صار آخذا له، بمنزلة ما لو وقع في شبكة فيجوز بيعه.[٢] قوله : موجب للملك ; كما لو وقع في شبكته ، وفي «شرح الوافي»: لا يجوزُ بيعه ; لأنَّ السد ليس بإحراز، فصار كطير وقع في بيت إنسان، فسد الباب والكوة ; فإنه لا يصير محرزاً له ما لم يأخذه ذكره العلامة الطَّحْطَاوِي عن العلامة نوح . And know that he (the author) strung together many issues in one thread, and said: 'It is not permissible', but he did not clarify whether the sale is void (batil) or defective (fasid). I will clarify that, Allah willing: Regarding fish that has not been caught, the sale should be void (batil) if it is with dirhams and dinars (currency), and defective (fasid) if it is with goods ('arad), because it is non-valuable property; because valuation (taqawwum) is by possession (ihraz). [Detail: i.e., if he delivers it, it is valid, otherwise not. And it is said: absolutely not valid, due to lack of possession. And the disagreement is when he has not prepared them. If he has prepared them, he owns them by consensus. If they gather without his action, he does not own them, whether he can take them without trickery or not.] End. [1] His saying: 'and its entrance is blocked' - until it becomes such that it cannot exit, then it is as if he has taken it, like if it falls into a net, so its sale is valid. [2] His saying: 'causing ownership' - like falling into his net. In 'Sharh al-Wafi': its sale is not permissible because blocking is not possession, so it is like a bird that enters a person's house, then he closes the door and window; it does not become possessed by him until he actually takes it. This was stated by 'Allamah al-Shami. And al-Zahidi said in 'Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri': If they gather by themselves, their sale is void in any case, due to lack of ownership. And upon this, the experts have stated.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on selling uncaptured fish, addressing an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether the sale is totally void (batil) or merely defective (fasid). The author explains that the base text merely said 'not permissible', but did not differentiate. He clarifies: if the fish is sold for cash (dirhams or dinars), the sale is batil because the fish is not owned and thus not a valid subject of sale (ma'dum). If sold for goods ('arad), the sale is fasid, since the goods are valuable but the fish is not fully owned. The key concept is 'taqawwum bil-ihraz' (valuation by possession). 'Ihraz' refers to physical control and enclosure. If the fish is in an enclosed area that it cannot escape, it becomes possessed and saleable. However, some Hanafi scholars like 'Allamah Noh argue that blocking the entrance is not true ihraz unless the fish is actually caught. The disagreement centers on whether the fish is considered owned. The author also notes that if the fish are prepared (e.g., in a pool with a net), there is consensus on ownership. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that a sale of a non-existent item (ma'dum) is generally batil, but there are nuanced exceptions based on possession.

Key Terms
البيع الباطل (al-bay' al-batil): A sale that is void ab initio due to a fundamental defect, such as selling a non-existent item.
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is defective in a non-fundamental aspect; it can become valid if the defect is rectified (e.g., delivery).
التقوم (al-taqawwum): Legal valuation that makes an item property subject to sale.
الإحراز (al-ihraz): Physical possession or enclosure that establishes ownership of unowned items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
