Ruling on Selling a Bird in the Air
Ruling on selling birds in flight with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a bird in the air and identify the key condition that makes it permissible.

Core Idea
A bird in flight can be sold if it habitually returns — custom makes delivery certain.

This passage addresses a common question in sales: can you sell something you cannot currently deliver? The answer depends on custom. Hanafi jurists used istihsan to permit selling trained homing pigeons, applying the maxim that habitual knowledge is like actuality. We will explore the ruling, its proof, and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and its apparent implication
The base text states 'no sale of a bird in the air'. Note that this appears to be a blanket prohibition. Consider why the sale might be invalid: the seller cannot deliver at the time of contract.
This is the starting point; later we will see exceptions.
2
Examine the conditional exception from Qadi Khan
Qadi Khan's fatwa introduces a condition: if the bird habitually returns and can be taken without trouble, the sale is permissible. Identify the two criteria: habitual return and ease of capture.
This is an istihsan based on custom.
3
Understand the legal principle (maxim) used
The passage cites the maxim 'al-ma'lum 'āda ka al-wāqi' (what is known by habit is like actuality). This is a Hanafi principle in non-worship matters. How does it apply here? The bird's return is not certain, but customary return is treated as sufficient.
The maxim is contrasted with the objection from ability to deliver.
4
Analyze the objection and the Hanafi response
Some scholars object that ability to deliver at contract time is a condition (shart) for valid sale, citing the example of an absconding slave. The Hanafi response distinguishes: with a homing bird, delivery is reasonably assured by custom, whereas with an absconding slave, location is unknown.
The possibility of non-return does not invalidate; it merely rescinds if it occurs.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative) position
Despite al-Timurtashi's apparent view of impermissibility, later authorities (Qadi Khan, al-Tanwir, al-Fath) adopt the permissive view for homing birds. This is the relied-upon fatwa in the Hanafi school.
The ikhtilaf is within the school; the tarjih favors custom-based permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بيع طير في الهواء ; سواء أرسله من يده أو لا، وذكر الإمام التُّمُرْتَاشِيُّ : ولو باع طائراً يذهب ويجيء كالحمام، الظاهر أنه لا يجوز، وفي «فتاوى قاضي خان»: «وإن باع طيراً يطير في الهواء إن كان ذا جناح يعود إلى بيته، ويقدر على أخذه من غير تكلّف جازَ بيعُه وإلا فلا. انتهى ، وهكذا في «التنوير» وغيره. ووجهه : أنَّ المعلوم عادة كالواقع، وتجويز كونها لا تعود، أو عُرُوض عـدم عودها لا يَمْنَعُ جواز البيع كتجويز هلاك المبيع قبل القبض، ثم إذا عرض الهلاك انفسخ، كذا هاهنا إذا فُرِضَ وقوع عدم المعتادِ من عودها قبل القبض انفسخ. كذا في «الفتح». وأورد عليه : إِنَّ مَن شرط صحة البيع القدرة على التسليم عقبه ; ولذا لم يجز بيع الآبق.
And [there is] no sale of a bird in the air, whether he released it from his hand or not. Imam al-Timurtashi mentioned: If he sold a bird that goes and comes like a pigeon, apparently it is not permissible. And in 'Fatawa Qadi Khan': 'If he sold a bird flying in the air, if it is of a kind that returns to its home and he can take it without trouble, its sale is permissible; otherwise not.' End. And similarly in 'al-Tanwir' and others. Its justification: that which is known by habit is like actuality. The possibility that it might not return, or the occurrence of its not returning, does not prevent the permissibility of the sale, like the possibility of destruction of the sold item before delivery. Then if destruction occurs, the contract is rescinded. Similarly here, if the unusual non-return is assumed to occur before delivery, the contract is rescinded. This is in 'al-Fath'. And an objection was raised: that one of the conditions for validity of a sale is ability to deliver at the time of contract; therefore, the sale of an absconding slave is not valid.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on selling a bird that is in flight (free-flying). The base text states there is no sale of a bird in the air, implying invalidity. However, Imam al-Timurtashi notes that even for birds like pigeons that return, the apparent ruling is impermissibility. Yet, the fatwa from Qadi Khan provides a conditional exception: if the bird is of a species that habitually returns to its home and can be taken without difficulty, the sale is permissible. This is based on the legal principle that habitual knowledge is treated like actual certainty (al-ma'lum ‘āda ka al-wāqi‘). The possibility that the bird might not return does not invalidate the sale, just as the possibility of destruction of goods before delivery does not prevent a valid sale; if destruction occurs, the contract is rescinded. Some scholars object, arguing that ability to deliver at the time of contract is a condition (shart) for valid sale, citing the example of an absconding slave (al-abiq) whose sale is invalid. The difference is that a homing bird's return is reasonably certain by custom, while an absconding slave's location is unknown. Thus, the Hanafi position permits selling homing pigeons and similar birds that reliably return.

Key Terms
الطير في الهواء (al-tayr fi al-hawa'): A bird that is flying freely in the air, not in one's possession.
المعلوم عادة كالواقع (al-ma'lum 'āda ka al-wāqi'): A legal maxim meaning that what is known by custom is treated as reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Any bird that is visible in the sky
B) Only a bird that the seller is holding
C) A bird that habitually returns to its home and can be taken without trouble
D) A bird that has been released by the seller

Key Takeaway
Selling a bird in the air is permissible in the Hanafi school only if the bird habitually returns and can be taken without trouble, based on the principle that customary knowledge is treated as actuality.

Invalid Sales: Birds, Fish, and Fetus in Hanafi Fiqh
Commentary on invalid sales of birds, fish, and fetus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to: distinguish between a batil and fasid sale; classify the sale of birds in the air, fish in a pond, and animal fetus/offspring as batil or fasid; and explain the exception for homing pigeons.

Core Idea
Sale of non-existent or uncertain objects is batil; sale of owned but difficult-to-deliver property is fasid.

This lesson examines three specific sales that the Hanafi school deems invalid: selling a bird in the air, fish caught but placed in an enclosure from which it is hard to retrieve, and the unborn fetus or future offspring of an animal. Each case highlights a different defect—non-existence, uncertain existence, or difficulty in delivery—and results in a different type of invalidity: batil (void) or fasid (corrupt). Understanding these distinctions is essential for identifying valid contracts in daily transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
Read the passage and list the three commercial scenarios discussed: bird in air, fish in enclosure, and animal fetus/offspring.
The passage also mentions an exception for homing pigeons.
2
Determine the ruling for each case
Based on the commentary, state whether each sale is batil, fasid, or permissible.
Fish in enclosure is fasid; bird in air is batil (except homing pigeons); fetus/offspring is batil.
3
Analyze the reasoning
For each ruling, identify the underlying cause: lack of ownership, non-existence, or difficulty in delivery.
The fish is owned but delivery is difficult; bird is not owned/not deliverable; fetus is non-existent.
4
Examine the exception for homing pigeons
Explain why the sale of a homing pigeon is valid despite it being a bird in the air. Note the concept of customary return (al-'adah) and the possibility of contract rescission.
The objection about runaway slaves is addressed.
5
Connect to the hadith
The passage cites the hadith 'نهى عن المضامين والملاقيح وحبل الحبلة' (prohibition of uncertain sales). Explain how this hadith supports the rulings.
The hadith prohibits selling what is not yet in existence.

And as for the fish that is caught and thrown into an enclosure, not taken from it without a stratagem, it is fitting that its sale be fasid, because it is owned property, but in its delivery there is difficulty. (And no sale of a bird in the air): so it is fitting that it be batil, like the sale of game before it is hunted. And it was answered: that the permissibility of selling them when they are accustomed to return does not negate the ability to deliver, for if returning is their habit, they are deliverable, and the claim of ability to deliver immediately after the contract is not necessary; if it is supposed that it does not return after the contract quickly, the sale is dissolved. (2) His statement: in the air; it is with madd; the body subjugated between the sky and the earth, plural ahwiyah, and it is said that it is the world, and it is said for empty space. (3) His statement: like the sale...: just as the sale of game is invalid before he takes it, because the taking is not owned. If it cannot be taken except with a stratagem, then it is not permissible, as stated by al-Zayla'i in Sharh al-Kanz. (And they used to practice that in Jahiliyyah, so the Prophet nullified that by prohibition). (And the sale of the fetus and the offspring: it is fitting that it be batil, because the offspring is non-existent, so it is not property, and the fetus is of doubtful existence, so it is not property).
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from a Hanafi commentary on sales. It addresses three specific invalid sales: (1) sale of fish caught and placed in an enclosure from which it cannot be taken without effort – this is fasid (corrupt) because it is owned property but delivery is difficult. (2) Sale of a bird in the air – this is batil (void) like selling game before it is caught. However, an exception is made for birds that habitually return (e.g., homing pigeons); their sale is valid because they are deliverable by habit. The objection using the analogy of an escaped slave (who habitually returns) is rebutted by distinguishing that the inability to deliver immediately does not invalidate the contract if non-delivery is only a possibility. (3) Sale of a fetus (haml) and its future offspring (nitaaj) – both are batil because the offspring is non-existent at the time of contract, and the fetus is of uncertain existence (it may never be born). The commentator follows the Hanafi position that such sales lack the necessary object (ma'dum or mashkuk al-wujud).

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is defective in its essence or conditions, but can be corrected by removal of the defect; it is not automatically void.
بيع باطل (bay' batil): A sale that lacks a fundamental condition, rendering it void ab initio.
النتاج (al-nitaaj): The offspring of an animal that will be born in the future; non-existent at the time of sale.

D) Because the fish is non-existent at the time of contract.

Key Takeaway
Sales involving non-existent, uncertain, or difficult-to-deliver objects are invalid in Hanafi law, with a distinction between void (batil) and corrupt (fasid) based on the nature of the defect.

Invalidity of Selling a Bird in the Air, Pregnancy, and Offspring
Commentary on the invalidity of three types of sales with intra-Hanafi nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for each of the three invalid sales and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on selling pregnancy.

Core Idea
A sale is invalid if the object is non-existent or uncertain, or if the seller cannot deliver immediately.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the invalidity of three types of sales: a bird still in the air, the unborn pregnancy (fetus), and the future offspring. These sales are problematic because the subject matter is either non-existent or not under the seller's control at the time of the contract. The commentary also reveals a subtle intra-Hanafi difference of opinion on whether selling pregnancy is merely corrupt (fāsid) or absolutely invalid (bāṭil).

Guided Path
1
Identify each prohibited sale
The text discusses three specific sales: a bird in the air, pregnancy (ḥaml), and offspring (nitāj). Understand what each term refers to.
The bird is any flying creature not yet captured; pregnancy is the fetus in the womb; offspring is the future newborn.
2
Understand the reasoning for invalidity
For each sale, the commentator gives a reason: the bird is not deliverable (like prey before capture), the offspring is non-existent at contract time, and the pregnancy is of uncertain existence. These factors make the object 'not wealth' (māl) and thus not valid subject matter.
The principle is that the subject matter must exist and be deliverable at the time of contract.
3
Analyze the objection and response regarding the bird
The text presents an objection: what if the bird is accustomed to returning? The response is that ability to deliver must exist at the moment of the contract, not merely habitually. If the bird fails to return, the sale is rescinded.
This clarifies that future possibility does not cure the defect of non-deliverability at the time of contract.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi disagreement on pregnancy
The footnote mentions that in al-Durr, selling offspring is bāṭil and selling pregnancy is fāsid, while in al-Baḥr both are bāṭil. Understand the basis: certainty vs. doubt of non-existence.
The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is that both are bāṭil, following al-Baḥr and the Prophetic prohibition of ḥabal al-ḥabalah.
5
Apply the overarching principle of gharar
The prohibition of gharar (deceptive uncertainty) underlies all three cases. The hadith forbidding ḥabal al-ḥabalah and similar contracts is the primary proof.
This establishes a strong presumption against sales involving uncertain or future objects.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on the original matn (al-Wiqāyah) which declares these sales invalid. The commentator explains why each sale is invalid: for a bird in the air, it is analogized to selling prey before capture—both involve an object not yet under the seller's control. The objection that a bird accustomed to returning is deliverable is answered: the contract requires immediate ability to deliver, not future possibility. If the bird fails to return, the contract is rescinded. For pregnancy and offspring, the offspring is non-existent at the time of contract, so it cannot be considered owned property. The pregnancy (fetus) is uncertain—it may or may not be alive—so similarly not proper subject matter. The footnote [0] introduces an intra-Hanafi disagreement: in al-Durr, selling the offspring is absolutely invalid (bāṭil), while selling the pregnancy is merely corrupt (fāsid) because its non-existence is doubtful. However, in al-Baḥr, even selling pregnancy is ruled invalid based on the Prophet's prohibition of ḥabal al-ḥabalah and the presence of gharar. The passage thus establishes a strong presumption against these sales, with the preferred position being absolute invalidity.

Key Terms
طير (ṭayr): A bird in the air; includes both a single bird and the plural (birds).
حمل (ḥaml): Pregnancy; the fetus in the womb.
نتاج (nitāj): Offspring; the result of birth.
غَرَر (gharar): Deception or uncertainty in the subject matter of a contract.

Key Takeaway
A sale is invalid if the object is non-existent, uncertain, or not deliverable at the time of contract; this principle prohibits selling a bird in the air, pregnancy, and future offspring.

Ruling on Selling Milk in the Udder
Commentary on invalidity of selling milk in udder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two main causes for the invalidity of selling milk in the udder and apply them to similar cases of uncertain subject matter in sales contracts.

Core Idea
Selling milk still in the udder is invalid due to uncertainty in its composition and gradual existence mixing ownership.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah delves into the legal reasons why the Hanafi school deems the sale of milk in the udder invalid. It presents two core causes and supplements them with a Prophetic narration, providing a comprehensive rationale. Understanding these causes sharpens your ability to identify contracts invalidated by uncertainty and ownership intermingling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act under consideration is the sale of milk that remains in the udder (al-laban fi al-dar'). The ruling is that this sale is batil (invalid).
2
Locate the primary causes (illat)
Two causes are given: (1) the content of the udder is unknown – it could be milk, blood, or air, making it uncertain property; (2) milk is produced gradually, so the seller's ownership of later milk mixes with the buyer's ownership of milk already paid for, and this mixture cannot be separated.
These two causes are distinct; the second is unique to this case.
3
Note the additional causes from the commentary
The footnote adds two more causes: (3) the inevitable dispute over the method of milking (the buyer wants full milking, the seller wants to preserve the animal's milk), and (4) a Prophetic narration (marfu') forbidding such sales.
The narration is also cited from al-Ṭabarānī and Ibn Abī Shaybah.
4
Connect to the general principle
The commentary states that mixing the sold item with unsold property in a way that makes distinction difficult invalidates the sale – a principle drawn from al-ʿInāyah.
This principle applies beyond milk sales.

[٣] قوله : فملك البائع...الخ ؛ واختلاط المبيع بما ليس بمبيع من ملك البائع على وجه يتعدر تمييزه مبطل للبيع. كذا في «العناية»).
And milk in the udder: They mentioned two causes (ʿillah) concerning it: One of them is that it is not known whether it is milk, blood, or air, so on this basis the sale is invalid because it is doubtful in existence, thus it is not wealth. And the second is that milk comes into existence little by little, so the ownership of the seller mixes with the ownership of the buyer. [The commentator says]: Both are one [i.e., both lead to invalidity], and the author (musannif) chose [this view]. (1) His statement: in the udder... etc.; with fatḥah [the vowel] means the breast [for animals]. As in Muntahā al-ʾArab. And it is said in al-Miṣbāḥ: al-ḍarʿ is for cloven-hoofed animals like al-shady for a woman; the plural is ḍurūʿ, like fals and fulūs. End. [2] His statement: two causes; rather four causes: the two mentioned by the commentator, the third is the dispute about the method of milking, as the buyer would milk excessively and the seller asks him to leave the milk stimulating factor; and the fourth is what al-Shāfiʿī narrated from Ibn ʿAbbās that he used to forbid selling milk in the udder of sheep and wool on its back, and it is narrated as marfūʿ (attributed to the Prophet), but al-ʿAllāmah al-Sumunnī said that the correct is that it is mawqūf (stopped at Ibn ʿAbbās). And in Naṣb al-Rāyah: al-Ṭabarānī narrated in his Muʿjam from Ibn ʿAbbās who said: 'The Messenger of Allāh (peace be upon him) forbade that fruit be sold until it ripens, and that wool be sold on its back, and that milk be sold in the udder.' And it is narrated... And Ibn Abī Shaybah narrated in his Muṣannaf with his chain from ʿIkrimah from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'He forbade that milk be sold in the udder, or clarified butter (samn) in milk.' End. [3] His statement: so the ownership of the seller... etc.; and the mixing of the sold item with what is not sold from the seller's ownership in a way that makes distinction difficult invalidates the sale. As in al-ʿInāyah.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on invalid sales, focusing on selling milk still in the udder. The commentator presents two primary reasons (ʿillah) for invalidity: (1) the content of the udder is uncertain—it could be milk, blood, or air—so it does not qualify as definite property; (2) milk is produced gradually, meaning part of it belongs to the seller when it is milked, causing a mixing of ownership that cannot be separated. The commentator notes that the author (al-Marghīnānī) chose this view. The footnote expands this: actually there are four reasons; the third is the inherent dispute over milking quantity, and the fourth is a Prophetic narration (via Ibn ʿAbbās) explicitly forbidding the sale of milk in the udder, wool on the back, and fruit before ripening. Although some scholars consider the narration mawqūf (a companion's statement), it is strong enough to support the ruling. The mixing principle is also cited from al-ʿInāyah. Thus, the sale of milk in the udder is definitively invalid.

Key Terms
لبن (laban): Milk; in this context, it refers to milk that is still in the udder, not yet extracted.
ضرع (ḍarʿ): Udder (the milk-producing organ of a female animal).
علة (ʿillah): A reason or cause for a legal ruling; here, the rationale for invalidating the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The sale of milk in the udder is invalid because the subject matter is uncertain and because the milk's gradual existence creates an unlawful mixing of ownership.

The Ruling on Selling Milk in the Udder
Explains the prohibition and its multiple causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state two causes for the invalidity of selling milk in the udder and identify the relevant hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Selling milk in the udder is invalid because its existence is uncertain and its ownership mixes with the seller's.

In Hanafi fiqh, selling milk while it is still in the udder is a classic example of a prohibited transaction due to gharar (uncertainty). This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind the ruling, presenting both the core causes and the supporting textual evidence from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Recognize that the act is selling milk while it is still inside the udder of a cloven-hoofed animal. The Hanafi ruling is that such a sale is batil (invalid).
2
Extract the First Cause: Uncertainty of Existence
The first cause is that the substance in the udder is not known to be milk; it could be blood or wind. Since the object's existence is doubtful, it is not considered property (mal), making the sale invalid.
3
Extract the Second Cause: Mixing of Ownership
Milk forms gradually. When the buyer purchases the existing milk, it mixes with newly formed milk that still belongs to the seller, creating an inseparable mixture. This mixing invalidates the sale.
4
Note the Additional Causes
The commentary adds two more causes: potential dispute over the milking method, and a hadith prohibition. These reinforce the ruling.
5
Understand the Daleel (Evidence)
The hadith narrated by Ibn Abbas (reported by al-Tabarani and Ibn Abi Shaybah) explicitly prohibits selling milk in the udder. This serves as a primary textual proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللَّبَنُ في الضرع ) : ذكروا فيه علّتين :
أحدهما : إنَّه لا يُعْلَمُ أنَّه لَبَنٌ، أو دَم، أو ريحٌ، فعلى هذا يبطل البيع ؛ لأنَّه مشكوك الوجود، فلا يكون مالاً.
والثانيةُ : إِنَّ اللَّبَنَ يُوجَدُ شيئاً فشيئاً ، فَمُلْكُ البائع" يختلط بملك المشتري.
فيهما واحد، واختاره المصنف .
(1) قوله : في الضرع ... الخ ; بالفتح : يستان. كذا في «منتهى الأرب»، وقال في «المصباح»: «الضرعُ لذات الظلف كالشَّدي للمرأة، والجمع ضُرُوع، مثل فلس وفلوس». انتهى [٢] قوله : علتين ; بل أربع علل ; فالإثنان منها ما ذكرَهُ الشَّارِحُ . والثالثة : المنازعة في كيفية الحلب، فإنّ المشتري يستقصي في الحلب، والبائع يطالبه بأن يترك داعية اللبن. والرابعةُ : ما روى الشافعي له عن ابن عباس له إنه كان ينهى عن بيع اللبن في ضرع الغنم، والصوف على ظهرها، وروي مرفوعاً ، وقال العلامة السُّمُنِّي (٢) : لكنَّ الصحيح أنه موقوف. وفي «نصب الراية»(۳) : روى الطَّبَرَانِيُّ في «معجمه» عن ابن عباس قال : «نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يباع ثمرة حتى تطعم ، ولا يباع صوف على ظهره، ولا لبن في ضرع»، وروز ، وروى ابن أبي شَيْبَةَ في «مصنفه» بسنده عن عكرمة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «إنه نهى أن يُباع لبن في ضرع، أو سمن في لبن». انتهى.
[٣] قوله : فملك البائع...الخ ; واختلاط المبيع بما ليس بمبيع من ملك البائع على وجه يتعذر تمييزه مبطل للبيع. كذا في «العناية»).
And milk in the udder: They mentioned two causes for it: One: that it is not known whether it is milk, or blood, or wind, so according to this the sale is invalid because it is of doubtful existence, so it is not property. And the second: that milk comes into existence little by little, so the ownership of the seller mixes with the ownership of the buyer. In them one, and the author chose it. (1) He said: in the udder... with a fatha, it means 'udders'. So in Muntaha al-Arab, and he said in al-Misbah: 'al-dar'u is for cloven-hoofed animals like al-shady for women, and the plural is duru', like fals and fulus'. End. [2] He said: two causes; rather four causes; two of them are what the commentator mentioned. The third: dispute about the manner of milking, for the buyer tries to milk fully while the seller asks him to leave some milk for the lamb. The fourth: what al-Shafi'i narrated from Ibn Abbas that he used to forbid selling milk in the udder of sheep and wool on its back, and it is narrated as marfu', and al-Sumunni said: but the correct is that it is mauquf. And in Nasb al-Rayah: al-Tabarani narrated in his Mu'jam from Ibn Abbas: 'The Messenger of Allah prohibited selling fruit until it matures, and selling wool on its back, and milk in the udder', and [Ruz], and Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated in his Musannaf with his chain from Ikrimah from the Prophet: 'He prohibited selling milk in an udder or ghee in milk'. End. [3] He said: the ownership of the seller… and mixing the sold item with what is not sold from the seller's property in a way that makes separation difficult invalidates the sale. So in al-Inayah.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn statement 'And milk in the udder'. The author explains that selling milk while it is still in the udder is prohibited. Two primary reasons are given: first, it is uncertain whether the substance is actually milk, blood, or wind—since the udder is not transparent and the contents are unknown, the sale is of an object whose existence is doubtful, and such an object is not considered property (mal) according to Hanafi law. Second, milk accumulates gradually; when the buyer takes ownership, the milk that is already present mixes with milk that continues to form, which belongs to the seller. This mixing of owned property with non-owned property in an indistinguishable manner invalidates the sale. The commentator then expands, stating that the original two causes are actually four: the two mentioned plus a third cause—potential dispute over the method of milking, because the buyer wants to extract all the milk while the seller insists on leaving some for the lamb—and a fourth, a narration from Ibn Abbas (reported by al-Shafi'i) that he forbade such sales. This narration is also transmitted as a marfu' hadith (attributed to the Prophet), though some scholars like al-Sumunni consider it mauquf (a saying of Ibn Abbas). Al-Tabarani and Ibn Abi Shaybah also report a hadith explicitly prohibiting selling milk in the udder. The passage concludes by reiterating that mixing the sold item with what is not sold, in a way that prevents separation, nullifies the sale. The Hanafi position is that such a sale is batil (invalid) or fasid (voidable), and this is the chosen view.

Key Terms
الضرع (al-dar'): The udder of cloven-hoofed animals (such as sheep and goats).
علة (illa): The effective cause or legal reason for a ruling; here, the reasons why the sale is invalid.
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a transaction that renders it invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is selling milk in the udder invalid?
A. Because the animal might be sold without milk.
B. Because the milk is not considered property due to doubtful existence and because of mixing ownership.
C. Because milking requires effort.
D. Because the price cannot be agreed upon.

Key Takeaway
Selling milk while it is still in the udder is invalid due to uncertainty of its existence and mixing of ownership, as supported by a clear hadith prohibition.

Selling Wool, a Beam, and a Cubit: The Principle of Dispute-Preventing Sales
Three prohibited sales explained with proof and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a sale leading to potential dispute (munaza'ah) is fasid, by analysing three classical cases: wool on sheep, a beam in a ceiling, and a cubit of cloth.

Core Idea
Any sale that inevitably leads to dispute is fasid, even if the parties initially consent.

One might think that selling wool while it is still on the sheep is a harmless forward contract. However, Hanafi jurists prohibit it because it inevitably leads to dispute over the place and time of shearing. Similarly, a beam in a ceiling or a cubit of cloth that is damaged by cutting cannot be sold without harmful delivery or ignorance. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning (ta'lil) behind these rulings and clarifies the distinction between fasid (voidable) and batil (void) within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sales and their common flaw
Read the passage and list the three prohibited sales: wool on a sheep, a beam in a ceiling, and a cubit of a cloth that is damaged by cutting. Note that each sale involves an object that cannot be delivered without causing harm or dispute.
The common element is 'al-munaza'ah' (dispute) that the sale leads to.
2
Analyse the proof for each case
For wool: why is it an attribute (wasf) and not a separate mal? How does the growth from the bottom cause mixing? For the beam: distinguish between specified and unspecified beam, and note the double cause (harm and jahalah). For the cloth: identify the condition that cutting damages the cloth; if not, the sale is permissible.
The proofs are based on qiyas: analogy to other prohibited sales, and on sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking pretexts).
3
Resolve the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf (batil vs fasid)
The text mentions two opinions: some say the sale is batil (invalid from the start), others say fasid (voidable). Understand the difference: fasid means the contract is irregular but can be rectified by renegotiation; batil means it is null and cannot be rectified. The commentator's proof indicates fasād, which is the relied-upon view.
The practical consequence: if fasid, the parties must cancel and may re-contract properly; if batil, no legal effect at all.
4
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider a modern case: selling a single brick from a wall that will be damaged when removed. Is it permissible? Use the same reasoning: the brick is not separate; removal causes harm; potential dispute over which brick and when. Thus, the sale is fasid.
The key is whether the object can be delivered without extra harm or ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصوفُ على ظهر الغنم، وجذع في سَقْفر، وذراع من ثوب ذَكَرَ قَطْعَهُ أو لا والصوفُ على ظهر الغنم) : لأَنَّهُ يقعُ التَّنازع في موضع القطع، وكلُّ بيع يُفضي إلى المنازعة فاسد.
لل
( وجذع في سَقْف " ، وذراع من ثوب ذَكَرَ قَطْعَهُ أو لا ) : فإِنَّ البيع فيهما فاسد
[٤]، والمراد ثوب يضره القطع )
[١] قوله : لأنه ... الخ ; ولأنَّ الصوف من أوصاف الحيوان قبل الجز، فلم يَجُز جعله مقصوداً بإيراد العقد عليه ; ولأنه يثبت من أسفل، فيختلط المبيع بغيره ، والاختلاط مبطل للبيع بخلاف القوائم، فإنّها تزيد من أعلاها فلا يلزم الاختلاط
والتفصيل في «العنايه»
(1)
وهذان الدليلان يفيدان بطلان البيع، كما هو مختار بعضهم، ودليل الشارح له يفيد فساده كما اختاره البعض.
[٢]قوله : وجذع في سقف ; اعلم أنَّ بيع الجذع في السَّقفِ لا يخلو إما أن يكونَ معيناً أو غير معين، فإن كان معيَّناً مغلطة عدم جواز بيعه لزومِ الضَّرَرِ في التسليم، وإن كان غير معيَّن ، فَعِلَّةً عدم جواز بيعه لزومُ الضَّرر، وجهالة الجذع، وكلٌّ منهما علةٌ لفسادِ البيع ; فلهذا لم يقيد المصنف له في الجذع بالتعييب. والجذع : بكسر الجيم، وسكون الذال المعجمتين : تنئه خرما بن وجزان. كذا في الأرب»، وقال في «النهر»: الجدْعُ : القطعة من النخل وغيره توضع عليها الأخشاب، وخصه صاحب «القاموس») بالنخل. [۳] قوله : فاسد ; لأنه لا يمكن تسليمه إلا بضررٍ لم يوجبه العقد، ومثله لا يكون لازماً، فيتمكن من الرجوع، وتتحقق المنازعة، وبهذا التقرير يندفع ما يقال : إِنَّ هذا الضرر مرضي به فينبغي أن لا يكون مفسداً. كذا في «الدرر» (۳). [٤] قوله : والمراد ثوب يضرُّهُ القطع ; كالقميص ونحوه، وإن كان لا يضره القطع كالكرباس جاز بيع ذراع منه كالقفين من الصبرة.
And wool on the back of sheep, and a beam in a ceiling, and a cubit of cloth whether he mentioned its cutting or not. And wool on the back of sheep: because dispute occurs regarding the place of cutting, and every sale that leads to dispute is void. And (a beam in a ceiling, and a cubit of cloth whether he mentioned its cutting or not): for the sale of both is void, and the intended is a cloth that is damaged by cutting. [1] He said: because... and because wool before shearing is an attribute of the animal, so it is not permissible to make it the object of a contract; and because it grows from the bottom, so the sold item mixes with what is not sold, and mixing nullifies the sale, unlike limbs, which grow from the top so no mixing occurs. And the detail is in al-Inayah. And these two proofs indicate the invalidity (butlan) of the sale, as is the choice of some, while the commentator's proof indicates its voidability (fasād), as chosen by others. [2] He said: a beam in a ceiling; know that the sale of a beam in a ceiling is either specific or non-specific. If it is specific, the reason for its impermissibility is the harm in delivery; if non-specific, the reason is the harm and the ignorance (jahalah) of the beam, and each is a cause for the sale's voidability. For this reason, the author did not restrict the beam to being specific. Al-jadhu (beam): with a kasra on the jim and sukun on the dhal, it is a piece of palm tree or other wood placed on which planks are laid. So in Muntaha al-Arab, and he said in al-Nahr: al-jad'u is a piece from palm trees and other things on which wood is placed, and the author of al-Qamus specified it as palm. [3] He said: void because it cannot be delivered without harm that the contract did not necessitate, and such a contract is not binding, so he can rescind, and dispute occurs. With this explanation, the objection that 'this harm is consented to, so it should not be voiding' is refuted. So in al-Durar. [4] He said: and the intended is a cloth that is damaged by cutting; like a shirt and the like. But if cutting does not damage it, like coarse cloth, selling a cubit of it is permissible, like measuring from a heap.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage deals with three prohibited sales: (1) wool still on the back of a sheep, (2) a beam already installed in a ceiling, and (3) an unspecified cubit from a cloth that would be damaged by cutting. For wool, two reasons are given: first, wool is an attribute (wasf) of the living animal, not an independent object, so it cannot be sold separately; second, wool grows from the bottom causing mixing with the rest of the fleece, whereas limbs grow from the top so no mixing occurs. Two opinions exist: some say the sale is batil (invalid), others say fasid (voidable). For a beam in a ceiling, the ruling depends on whether it is specified or not. If specified, delivery would cause harm (damage to the ceiling), so sale is invalid. If unspecified, both harm and ignorance (jahalah) invalidate it. For cloth, only if cutting damages it (e.g., a shirt) is the sale invalid; if cutting does not damage it (e.g., a large piece like carpet), selling a cubit is permissible. The key principle is that any sale leading to harmful delivery or dispute is fasid or batil.

Key Terms
الصوف (al-suf): Wool; here, it is considered an attribute of the animal before shearing, not an independent commodity.
الجذع (al-jadhu): A beam or piece of wood (especially palm trunk) used in construction.
المنازعة (al-munaza'ah): Dispute; any sale that leads to dispute is considered invalid (fasid) in Hanafi law.
باطل (batil): Void from the outset; no legal effect.
فاسد (fasid): Voidable; irregular contract that can be rescinded but not inherently null.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the shirt is not a commodity that can be measured.
B) Because the cutting damages the shirt, causing harmful delivery and potential dispute.
C) Because the buyer cannot inspect the exact cubit.
D) Because the sale of any part of a garment is prohibited.

Key Takeaway
A sale is fasid if it inevitably leads to harmful delivery or dispute, even if both parties initially consent.

Three Fasid Sales: Wool, Beam, and Cloth
States rulings on three defective sales with reasons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules these three sales as fasid and identify the underlying legal principle.

Core Idea
A sale that inevitably leads to dispute is fasid, not absolutely void, and may be rectified.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya presents three classic Hanafi examples of defective sales (buyu' fasida). The rulings are based on a key principle: any sale that leads to dispute is fasid. In this lesson, we will unpack each case, see how the principle applies, and note an internal Hanafi difference of opinion on whether these sales are fasid or batil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sales
List the three specific sales described: wool on a sheep's back, a beam fixed in a ceiling, and a cubit from a cloth that cutting would harm. Note that the cloth must be one that is harmed by cutting.
These are all sales of part of a larger object where separation causes difficulty.
2
State the ruling for each
All three are ruled fasid (defective) in the Hanafi school. Fasid means the contract is valid in essence but has a defect that prevents it from being fully effective; it can be rectified if the defect is removed before rescission.
Some Hanafis consider them batil (void), but the relied-upon view is fasid.
3
Trace the proof: the general principle
The text cites the principle 'Every sale that leads to dispute is fasid' (كل بيع يفضي إلى المنازعة فاسد). This is the primary dalil. Apply it to each case: wool sale leads to dispute about where to cut; beam sale leads to dispute about delivery (harm in removal); cloth sale leads to dispute about the cut location and damage.
This principle is a general rule derived from multiple ahadith and ijma, applied via qiyas.
4
Examine additional proofs for wool sale
The commentator adds two more reasons: wool before shearing is only an attribute of the animal, not independent property, and it grows from the bottom causing mixing with unsold parts. These further indicate the sale's defectiveness.
These additional proofs are mentioned by some scholars as supporting batil rather than fasid.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafis, based on the additional proofs, hold that these sales are batil (void). However, the commentator (and the relied-upon view) says they are fasid because the defect can be removed by specifying the cut or obtaining consent, making the contract valid.
The practical difference: if the sale is fasid, the buyer may return the item and get the price back, but if the defect is removed (e.g., wool is shorn with agreement), the sale becomes valid.

The Book of Sales / Defective and Disliked Sales. And wool on the back of a sheep, and a beam in a ceiling, and a cubit from a cloth whether he mentioned cutting it or not. So the sale of wool on the back of a sheep is because dispute occurs in the place of cutting, and every sale that leads to dispute is fasid. And a beam in a ceiling, and a cubit from a cloth whether he mentioned cutting it or not, for the sale of both is fasid. And what is meant is a cloth that cutting harms it. [Commentary] Because wool before shearing is a description of the animal, so it is not permissible to make it the object of a contract. And because it grows from the bottom, so the sold item mixes with others, and mixing invalidates the sale, unlike limbs which grow from the top. These two proofs indicate the nullity of the sale according to some, while the commentator's proof indicates its defectiveness as chosen by others. Know that the sale of a beam in a ceiling is either specific or unspecific. If specific, the reason for its impermissibility is the harm in delivery; if unspecific, the reason is harm and ignorance of the beam. Each is a cause for the sale's defectiveness. Fasid because it cannot be delivered except with harm not necessitated by the contract, so it is not binding, disputes arise, and this refutes the objection that the harm is consented to. The cloth intended is one that cutting harms, like a shirt; if cutting does not harm it like coarse fabric, selling a cubit is permissible.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage states the rulings for three sales that are considered fasid (defective) in the Hanafi school. The first is selling the wool still on a sheep's back before shearing. The reason given is that it leads to dispute about the exact place of cutting, and any sale leading to dispute is fasid. The commentator adds two further reasons: the wool before shearing is only an attribute of the animal, not independent property, and it grows from the bottom causing mixing with unsold parts. The second is selling a beam already fixed in a ceiling. The commentator explains that whether the beam is specified or not, there is harm in delivery or ignorance of the exact beam, both invalidating the sale. The third is selling a cubit from a cloth that would be harmed by cutting, like a shirt. If the cloth is not harmed by cutting (e.g., coarse fabric), the sale is permissible. The key principle is that any sale that cannot be delivered without causing additional harm not agreed upon is fasid.

Key Terms
فاسد (fasid): A contract that is valid in essence but has a defect that prevents it from being fully effective; it can be rectified.
جذع (jadh'): A beam, specifically a piece of palm tree or timber used in construction, fixed in a ceiling.
ذراع (dhira'): A cubit, a unit of length; here meaning a piece of cloth measured by the cubit.

Defect Removal Cures Sale; Void Sales: Hunter's Throw & Muzabanah
Continues with conditions for validity and two void sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a defective sale becomes valid and explain why hunter's throw and muzabanah are batil.

Core Idea
A sale void due to delivery harm can be cured if the harm is removed before rescission; sales of unknown or unowned items are void.

This passage first clarifies a nuance: if the seller removes the defect (e.g., cuts the cloth or uproots the beam) before the buyer exercises the option to rescind, the sale becomes valid. Then it introduces two types of sales that are fundamentally void: selling whatever is caught by a single throw of a net (hunter's throw) and selling fresh fruit on trees for an estimated quantity of dried fruit (muzabanah). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for avoiding invalid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect in the original sale
The original sale of a beam in a ceiling or a cloth piece of a garment is defective because delivery would cause harm (e.g., damaging the ceiling or garment). This harm is not justified by the contract.
The defect is the inability to deliver without causing harm.
2
Note the condition for cure
If the seller removes the defect (uproots the beam or cuts the cloth) before the buyer rescinds, the harm is eliminated.
The removal must occur before the buyer's rescission (faskh).
3
Recognize the ruling after cure
The sale becomes valid (sahih) because the defect is gone. The contract is now executable without harm.
This shows that some defects are curable if removed in time.
4
Analyze the hunter's throw sale
This is selling whatever a net catches in one throw. The seller does not own the specific item at contract, and there is extreme uncertainty (gharar). The text analogizes it to selling a bird in the air.
Qiyas: like bird in air → batil.
5
Analyze muzabanah
This is selling fresh fruit on trees for an estimated amount of dried fruit of the same kind. The equivalence is uncertain and it leads to riba and disputes.
Prohibited in all schools due to gharar and potential riba.

Commentary
This passage explains two additional rulings. First, regarding the defective sales of a beam in a ceiling or a harmful cloth piece: if the seller removes the beam or cuts the cloth piece before the buyer exercises the option of rescission, the sale becomes valid because the defect (inability to deliver without harm) is removed. Second, two sales are declared batil (void): (1) 'ضربة القانص' (hunter's throw) – selling whatever is caught by a single throw of a net; this is void because the seller does not own the item at the time of contract and it involves excessive uncertainty (gharar). (2) 'المزابنة' (muzabanah) – selling fresh fruit on trees for an estimated amount of dried fruit of the same kind; this is prohibited because it involves uncertainty about equivalence and leads to disputes. The commentator clarifies that the defect in the first case is the harm in delivery; once removed, the contract stands. The second two are fundamentally flawed and cannot be rectified.

Key Terms
ضربة القانص (darbat al-qanis): A sale of whatever is caught by a single throw of a hunting net; considered batil.
مزابنة (muzabanah): The sale of fresh fruit on trees for an estimated amount of dried fruit of the same kind.
خرص (kharas): Estimation of the amount of fruit on trees by guess, not actual measurement.

Key Takeaway
A defective sale due to delivery harm can be cured if the defect is removed before rescission; sales involving unowned items or uncertain equivalence (hunter's throw, muzabanah) are void and cannot be cured.

Curing a Fasid Sale by Removing the Defect Before Rescission
Ruling on validity after defect removal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a fasid sale becomes valid and identify the moment when the mufsid is considered removed.

Core Idea
A fasid sale becomes valid if the defect is removed before rescission.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that a sale of a beam fixed in a ceiling or a sleeve attached to a garment is initially invalid because delivery would cause harm to the remaining property. However, if the seller removes the beam or cuts the sleeve before the buyer rescinds, the sale becomes valid. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that a voidable contract can be cured by eliminating the invalidating factor before the right of rescission is exercised.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial classification
The act is a sale of a beam in a ceiling or a sleeve of a garment. Initially, it is fasid (voidable) due to the impossibility of delivery without harming the remaining property.
Fasid in Hanafi fiqh means defective but potentially curable.
2
Identify the mufsid (invalidating factor)
The mufsid is the harm caused to the remaining property (the ceiling or the garment) when attempting to deliver the sold part. This makes the sale invalid initially.
Mufsid is the cause of the defect.
3
Determine the condition for validity
The text states: if the beam is uprooted or the sleeve is cut before the buyer rescinds, the sale returns to validity. So the condition is removal of the mufsid before rescission.
Rescission (fasakh) is the buyer's right to annul the contract due to the defect.
4
Conclude the resulting hukm
After removal, the contract becomes sahih (valid). The ruling is clear: the contract is no longer fasid because the mufsid no longer exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعود صحيحاً إن قُلِعَ، أو قطع الذراع قبل فسخ المشتري، لأنَّ المفسد قد زال.
And it returns to being valid if it is uprooted, or the sleeve is cut before the buyer's rescission, because the invalidating factor has ceased.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn explaining that a sale which is invalid due to impossibility of delivery without harm (such as selling a beam fixed in a ceiling or a sleeve from a garment) becomes valid if the seller removes the beam or cuts the sleeve before the buyer rescinds the contract. The reason is that the invalidating factor—the harm to the remaining property—has been removed before the contract is definitively voided. This is a Hanafi legal principle: a contract can be cured if the defect is removed before final annulment.

Key Terms
مفسد (mufsid): The invalidating factor that makes a contract fasid (voidable), here the impossibility of delivery without harming the remaining property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A fasid sale can be cured by removing the invalidating factor before the buyer rescinds.

Invalidity of Selling a Single Catch from a Net
Ruling statement on the invalidity of a specific sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a sale of a single catch from a net (ضربة القانص) and explain why it is invalid in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Selling a catch before it is caught is void due to non-existence and uncertainty.

This lesson examines a specific type of sale known as ضربة القانص, where the subject matter is whatever is caught in a single throw of a net. The Hanafi ruling is clear: such a transaction is invalid because the item does not exist and is not owned by the seller at the time of contract, leading to prohibited uncertainty (gharar). Understanding this ruling reinforces the fundamental principle that valid sales require a known, existing, and owned subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject matter
Recognize that the sale is of an unspecified animal caught by a single net strike — the exact item is unknown at contract time.
This is similar to selling a bird in the air.
2
Determine the ruling
The passage states that this sale is batil (void) in the Hanafi school.
Batil means it has no legal effect; ownership does not transfer.
3
Understand the reason (illah)
The invalidity is due to gharar (uncertainty) and the fact that the seller does not own the catch at the moment of sale.
The animal is not yet in the seller's possession or ownership.
4
Connect to analogous cases
The commentator analogizes to the sale of a bird in the air, which is also batil for the same reasons.
This shows consistency in Hanafi rulings on uncertain subject matter.

And the strike of the hunter: it is what is obtained from the hunt by a single strike of the net, and this sale should be invalid as we mentioned regarding the bird in the air.

Commentary
This passage defines and rules on a type of sale called 'ضربة القانص' (darbat al-qanis). It is the sale of whatever animal is caught by a single throw of a net. The ruling is that such a sale is invalid (batil) because the item sold is not owned by the seller at the time of contract (it is unknown what will be caught) and because there is gharar (uncertainty). The commentator analogizes it to the sale of a bird in the air, which is also invalid. This is a consistent Hanafi position: any sale involving unknown or non-owned subject matter is void.

Key Terms
ضربة القانص (darbat al-qanis): A sale of whatever is caught by a single strike of a net; considered invalid due to uncertainty.

Invalidity of Muzabanah: Sale of Fruit by Estimation
Definition and ruling of muzabanah with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of muzabanah, explain why it is fasid in the Hanafi school, and identify the hadith evidence for its prohibition.

Core Idea
Muzabanah is invalid because estimation creates uncertainty, resembling riba in same-commodity exchange.

This lesson examines a specific prohibited sale called muzabanah. The Hanafi school classifies it as fasid due to the suspicion of riba. Understanding this ruling requires grasping why estimation (kharṣ) in exchanging the same genus of fruit leads to prohibition. We will explore the definition, the legal reasoning, and the prophetic evidence from the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is muzabanah? What is the hukm (fasid) according to the text?
The text defines muzabanah and states it is from the invalid sales.
2
Locate the dalil
What evidence is cited? The hadith 'نهى رسول الله عن المزابنة' (prohibition of muzabanah).
This hadith is found in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the prohibition indicate that the sale is fasid? Because it involves a suspicion of riba due to estimation.
The estimation (kharṣ) prevents certainty of equality, which is required in exchange of the same genus.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Although the text does not mention disagreement, all schools of thought generally prohibit muzabanah.
The cross-madhhab status is muttafaq alayh (agreed upon).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi: muzabanah is prohibited and invalid.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is recorded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمزابنة : وهي بيعُ الثَّمر على النخيل بثمر مَجْدُوذٍ مثل كيلِهِ خَرْصَاً، فهذا البيع من البيوع الفاسدة لشبهة الربا.
And muzabanah: it is the sale of fruit on the palm trees for cut fruit like its measure by estimation, so this sale is from the invalid sales due to the suspicion of riba.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage defines muzabanah as selling fresh fruit on trees for an estimated amount of dry fruit of the same kind. The ruling is that it is a fasid (invalid) sale because it involves a suspicion of riba (interest). Since the exchange is of the same commodity (dates) but one is fresh and the other dry, and the quantity is estimated by guesswork rather than actual measurement, it resembles a riba transaction. The Hanafi school holds this sale invalid, and there is a hadith prohibiting muzabanah, as mentioned in the commentary.

Key Terms
مزابنة (muzabanah): Sale of fresh fruit on trees for an estimated amount of dry fruit of the same kind; invalid due to riba.
خرص (kharṣ): Estimation by guesswork, not actual measurement, used in muzabanah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the fruit is not ripe
B) Because it involves estimation, leading to potential riba
C) Because the price is not fixed
D) Because the seller does not own the fruit

Key Takeaway
Muzabanah is invalid because estimation (kharṣ) in same-commodity exchange creates uncertainty, resembling riba.

Three Invalid Pre-Islamic Sales: Malamasah, Ilaqa al-Hajar, and Munabadhah
Explanation of invalidity of three pre-Islamic sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of invalid pre-Islamic sales (al-malamasah, ilqa al-hajar, al-munabadhah) and state the Hanafi ruling that they are fasid due to gharar and lack of proper consent.

Core Idea
Pre-Islamic sales based on chance or force are fasid because consent is not properly expressed.

In this lesson, we examine three invalid sales from pre-Islamic Arabia that the Prophet ﷺ prohibited. The Hanafi school classifies them as fasid (invalid) because they involve uncertainty (gharar) and lack a proper offer and acceptance. Understanding these rulings helps us appreciate the importance of clear consent in Islamic contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify each sale type
Read the definitions of al-malamasah, ilqa al-hajar, and al-munabadhah. Understand the mechanism that makes each sale binding.
All three involve a binding outcome based on a physical action, not mutual oral consent.
2
Analyze the deficiency
Note that each sale lacks explicit offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) and contains uncertainty (gharar) about the terms or consent.
Gharar invalidates the contract according to the Hanafi school.
3
State the ruling
The passage concludes that these sales are fasid (invalid). Understand that they do not transfer ownership and are prohibited.
Fasid means the contract is invalid ab initio in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والملامسة، وإلقاء الحَجَر، والمنابذة : وهي أن يتساوما سلعةً لَزِمَ البيعُ إن لمسَها المشتري، أو وضع عليها حصاةً، أو نبدها البائع إليه، فهذه البيوع فاسدة.
And al-malamasah, ilqa al-hajar, and al-munabadhah: they are that two people negotiate over a commodity, the sale becomes binding if the buyer touches it, or places a pebble on it, or the seller throws it to him, so these sales are invalid.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage defines three types of pre-Islamic invalid sales: (1) الملامسة (malamasah) – a sale that becomes binding by mere touching, (2) إلقاء الحجر (ilqa al-hajar) – throwing a stone to conclude a sale, and (3) المنابذة (munabadhah) – throwing the commodity to the buyer, making the sale binding. These are all considered invalid (fasid) by the Hanafi school because they involve gharar (uncertainty) and lack a valid offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) with free consent. The rulings are based on the prophetic prohibition of such sales.

Key Terms
الملامسة (al-malamasah): A sale where mere touching by the buyer makes it binding; invalid due to gharar.
المنابذة (al-munabadhah): A sale where the seller throws the item to the buyer, making it binding; invalid due to lack of explicit consent.
إلقاء الحجر (ilqa al-hajar): A sale where the buyer throws a pebble to indicate which item he will buy, making the sale binding; invalid due to gharar.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that sales concluded by touching, throwing a pebble, or throwing the commodity are fasid because they involve gharar and lack proper consent.

Invalid Sales: Malamasa, Ilaq al-Hajar, and Munabadha
Defines three invalid pre-Islamic sales and rules them fasid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of the three prohibited sales (malamasa, ilqa' al-hajar, munabadha) and explain why they are fasid based on the hadith prohibition.

Core Idea
Sales based on touch or throw are fasid due to uncertainty and lack of proper offer and acceptance.

In pre-Islamic Arabia, certain sales relied on physical actions rather than verbal consent. This passage from a classical Hanafi text defines three such sales and declares them invalid. Understanding why helps us see the importance of clarity (gharar removal) and mutual consent in Islamic contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sales
List the three types: malamasa (sale by touch), ilqa' al-hajar (sale by throwing a stone), and munabadha (sale by throwing the item). Read their definitions carefully.
2
State the ruling
The passage rules these sales as 'fasid' (invalid). Note that fasid is not the same as batil (void) in Hanafi fiqh.
Fasid means the contract is defective and requires cancellation, but if possession was taken, the item must be returned.
3
Examine the proof
The author cites the hadith prohibition from Bukhari and Muslim. In Hanafi usul, prohibition (nahy) of a contract implies its invalidity. Also, the resemblance to riba and gambling is noted.
The hadith: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade this sale.'
4
Understand the underlying cause
These sales involve gharar (uncertainty) because the specific item or the exact moment of contract is unclear. They also lack proper offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul), which are essential for a valid sale.
In malamasa, touching substitutes for verbal consent; in ilqa' al-hajar, the item is not specifically identified beforehand.
5
Compare with other schools
The Hanafi school classifies these as fasid, while some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider them batil. Note the practical consequence: in Hanafi, if possession has been taken, the item must be returned; in Shafi'i, the contract is void ab initio.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; both positions are valid within their methodologies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والملامسة، وإلقاء الحَجَر، والمنابذة : وهي أن يتساوما سلعةً لَزِمَ البيعُ إن لمسَها المشتري، أو وضع عليها حصاةً، أو نبدها البائع إليهِ) : فهذه البيوع فاسدة الحقيقي ؛ لأنه لو وجد الكيلُ الحقيقي في البدلين لم يبقَ الثَّمر على رؤوس النخيل، بل يكون تمراً مجذوذاً كالذي يقابله من المجذوذ. كذا في «النهاية». And al-malamasa, throwing a stone, and al-munabadha: which is that they negotiate a commodity, the sale becomes binding if the buyer touches it, or places a pebble on it, or the seller throws it to him. These sales are fundamentally invalid. [Reason:] due to the suspicion of riba; because it is buying a measured thing by a measured thing of the same type, so there is the possibility of riba. The Messenger of Allah forbade this sale, as reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri and from 'Ata' ibn Abi Rabah from Jabir ibn 'Abdullah. [Regarding al-malamasa:] it is that one says: 'If I touch your garment or you touch mine, the sale is binding.' [Regarding al-munabadha:] it is that one says: 'If I throw it to you, or you throw it to me, the sale is binding.' These are like gambling.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage defines three types of invalid sales that were common in pre-Islamic times: (1) al-malamasa (touching), where the sale is concluded by simply touching the item; (2) throwing a stone, where the buyer throws a stone at the item and the sale is binding; and (3) al-munabadha (throwing), where either party throws the item to the other. The author states that these sales are fundamentally invalid (fasid). The proof is from the Prophet's ﷺ prohibition, and the underlying cause is the suspicion of riba because the transaction involves an exchange of similar measured items without specification, creating uncertainty. The commentary further defines each term, citing classical works.

Key Terms
الملامسة (al-malamasa): A sale where the buyer touches the goods, and the sale is considered binding upon that touch.
إلقاء الحجر (ilqa' al-hajar): A sale where the buyer throws a stone at the item, and the sale is binding on whichever item the stone strikes.
المنابذة (al-munabadha): A sale where one party throws the item to the other, and the sale is considered binding upon throwing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they involve a delay in payment.
B) Because they resemble gambling and involve uncertainty akin to riba.
C) Because they require a verbal offer and acceptance which is missing.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Sales concluded by touch, throwing stones, or throwing goods are fasid in Hanafi fiqh because they lack clarity and proper consent, resembling gambling and riba.

Invalid Sales: Unspecified Garments and Pasture
Rulings on selling pasture and selling one of two garments without specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for selling one of two garments without specification and for selling pasture, and explain the exceptions and reasoning (istihsan, common property).

Core Idea
Sale of unspecified items or common property is void; but a condition of choice can cure uncertainty.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya addresses two common cases of invalid sales: selling one of two garments without specifying which, and selling or leasing pasture land. Both are prohibited but for different reasons—one due to uncertainty (jahalah) and the other because the grass is common property. The commentary explains an exception for the garment sale via istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two prohibited sales
Read the matn: 'There is no sale of one garment from two... and no sale of pasture nor its lease.' Pinpoint the two types of contracts.
The matn explicitly forbids both.
2
Determine the reason for each prohibition
The commentary explains: for the garment, it is jahalah (ignorance) that leads to dispute; for pasture, it is because the grass is not owned (common property).
Also likened to gambling for the garment sale.
3
Locate the exception for the garment sale and its basis
The matn includes 'except on condition that he takes whichever he wishes'. The commentary states this is allowed by istihsan, referencing the chapter on khiyar al-shart.
Istihsan overrides the general prohibition.
4
Understand why pasture sale is void
Pasture (mara'i) means the grass (kala'), which is communal property for all Muslims. Selling it is like selling what you do not own.
The commentary clarifies that 'mara'i' is the place, but the intended sale is of the grass.
5
Note the terminological clarification
The commentator explains that 'mara'i' (pasture) is the land, but in this context it means the grass, to avoid confusion that selling the land itself is also prohibited.
Selling the land is valid; only the grass is common property.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage covers two types of invalid sales: (1) selling one of two garments without specifying which one, unless a condition of choice is included; (2) selling or leasing pasture (al-mara'i). The main text states that such sales are like gambling because the contract depends on uncertain actions. The commentary explains that selling one of two garments without specification is invalid due to jahalah (ignorance) regarding the exact item, which leads to disputes. However, if the seller gives the buyer the choice (option) to take whichever he wishes, it becomes valid by istihsan. As for pasture, it is invalid because it is not yet harvested and is common property; the commentary clarifies that the word 'mara'i' (pasture) refers to the grass (kala'), not the land, to avoid misunderstanding that selling the land is prohibited. The grass is considered common property shared by Muslims, thus its sale is void.

Key Terms
المراعي (al-mara'i): Pasture land where animals graze; the text specifies it here means the grass (kala') growing on the land.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty about the subject matter of the contract that leads to dispute.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows an exception to qiyas for a stronger reason; here it validates the sale with a condition of choice.

Key Takeaway

Invalid Sales: Unspecified Selection and Grass (Kala')
Commentary on invalid sales: unspecified selection and grass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of one of two unspecified cloths and the sale/lease of unacquired grass, and explain the conditions under which grass becomes saleable.

Core Idea
Uncertainty in the object of sale invalidates the contract, and common ownership of grass prevents sale until physical acquisition.

This lesson examines two specific invalid sales: selling one cloth from two without specifying which, and selling or leasing pasture grass. The key legal concepts are gharar (uncertainty) and the common ownership of natural resources established by hadith. We will explore how the Hanafi school distinguishes between naturally growing grass and grass that results from human cultivation, and the role of ihraz (acquisition) in making grass private property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sales and their rulings
The matn states: (1) No sale of a cloth from two cloths unless the buyer has the option to choose; (2) No sale or lease of pasture (grass). Both are invalid (fasid). Determine the underlying reason for each.
The first is invalid due to uncertainty (jahala) leading to dispute; the second because the grass is not owned by the seller.
2
Examine the proof for grass common ownership
The hadith 'Muslims are partners in three: water, grass, and fire' establishes partnership. Analyze how this hadith prevents sale unless the grass is physically acquired (ihraz).
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Distinguish between natural and cultivated grass
The commentary differentiates: naturally growing grass remains common property; grass that grows after a person irrigates and prepares the land becomes his property and can be sold. Understand the rationale behind this distinction.
This is the majority position (mufta bihi), contra al-Quduri.
4
Apply the concept of ihraz (acquisition)
Grass becomes private property only after physical cutting and possession (ihraz). Similarly, water after drawing. Without ihraz, any sale or lease is invalid.
The analogy: like a cloth that falls into another's house – owner must either allow entry or retrieve and hand over.
5
Synthesize the two rulings and their thamarah al-khilaf
Compare the two cases: both involve uncertainty or non-ownership, but the second has an intra-Hanafi dispute. Note the practical difference: if one cultivates grass, the majority permits selling it, while al-Quduri forbids it.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the seller can sell grass before cutting after irrigating the land.

[2] (And there is no sale of a cloth from two cloths except on condition that he takes whichever he wishes, nor the pasture, nor its lease) The sale of pasture: i.e., grass is invalid because it is not acquired [by possession]. As for its lease... the buyer: 'If you throw it to me, the sale is binding.' End. [1] His statement: 'like gambling' – and it is in the custom of our time every game in which it is usually stipulated that the winner takes something from the players. (2) His statement: 'No sale of a cloth from two cloths' – due to ignorance of the sold item, an ignorance that leads to dispute because of the variation in cloths; for the seller does not know what he delivers, and the buyer does not know what he receives. [3] His statement: 'except on condition...' etc.: such that he says, 'I sell you one of them on condition that you have the option, take whichever you wish' – then it is permissible by istihsan, and its explanation has passed in the chapter on option of condition. [4] His statement: 'the grass' – and this is of the type of applying the place to the thing; and he only said 'i.e., the grass' because the word 'pasture' means the place of grazing, which is the land; if he had not interpreted it thus, one might think that the sale and lease of land are not permissible, but that is not the case. And 'kala': like jabal [meaning] grass, fresh and dry, as in al-Qamus; it includes all types that livestock graze on, fresh or dry, unlike trees because 'kala' is what has no stem, and trees have stems, so they are not included; thus it is permissible to sell them if they grow in his land because they are his property; and truffles are like grass. Thus in al-Bahr. [0] His statement: 'because it is not acquired' – because partnership in it is established by the text, namely his statement: 'Muslims are partners in three: water, grass, and fire' – narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and Ibn Majah, and he added: 'and its price is unlawful' – and it is interpreted when its owner has not acquired it. The meaning of partnership in fire is warming oneself with it and lightening clothes, not taking embers except with permission; and in water: drinking and watering animals, and drawing from owned wells, cisterns, and rivers; and in grass: gathering even in owned land, except that the landowner may prevent entry, and another may say, 'I have a right in your land, so either you convey me to it, or you gather it, or you draw water and give it to me' – and it becomes like a man's cloth that falls into another man's house: either he permits the owner to enter to take it, or he brings it out to him; the detail is in al-Fath. And the place of what was mentioned is when water has not been acquired by drawing into vessels, and grass has not been acquired by cutting; but if they are acquired, their sale is permissible because by acquisition they become his property. Its place also is when it grows by itself; but if he irrigated the land and prepared it for growth and it grew, then its sale is permissible because it is his property, as in al-Dhakhira, al-Muhit, and al-Nawazil; this is the choice of al-Sadr al-Shahid and the majority, while al-Quduri forbade its sale. Thus in al-Bahr and other works. [1] His statement: 'because it...' etc.: i.e., because the contract of lease is concluded for consumption of a non-owned substance; if it were concluded for consumption of an owned substance – such as renting a cow to drink its milk – it is not permissible, so this is even more so. Thus in al-Hidayah. Thus the non-permissibility of the lease is for two reasons: 1. its occurrence on non-owned property, and 2. consumption of the substance.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling regarding invalid sales. The base text states that selling one cloth from two unspecified cloths is invalid unless the buyer is given the option to choose which one to take. Similarly, the sale or lease of pasture (i.e., grass) is invalid because the grass is not yet owned by the seller; it is commonly owned among Muslims based on the hadith: 'Muslims are partners in three: water, grass, and fire.' The commentator explains that the prohibition on selling grass applies only if it has not been physically acquired (ihraz) by cutting and possessing. Once it is cut and collected, it becomes private property and can be sold. The commentary also clarifies that 'pasture' (mar'a) refers to the place of grazing, while the actual grass (kala') is the object of sale. A key distinction is drawn between naturally growing grass (where partnership applies) and grass that results from a person's effort in irrigating and cultivating the land; in the latter case, the majority of Hanafi scholars (including al-Sadr al-Shahid) permit its sale even before cutting because it is considered his property, while al-Quduri disagreed. The lease of pasture is invalid because it involves consumption of a non-owned substance, similar to renting a cow to drink its milk.

Key Terms
مرعى (mar'a): Lit. pasture; the place where animals graze. The text explains it is used metonymically for the grass itself.
كلأ (kala'): Grass, both fresh and dry; any vegetation grazed by livestock, excluding trees because they have stems.
إحراز (ihraz): Acquisition by physical possession, e.g., cutting grass or drawing water into a vessel, which converts common property into private ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is the sale of naturally growing grass (kala') permitted?
A) Sale is never permitted because Muslims are partners in grass.
B) Sale is permitted only after the grass has been cut and physically acquired (ihraz).
C) Sale is permitted only if the grass grows naturally without human effort.
D) Sale is permitted only if the grass is sold along with the land.

Key Takeaway
Uncertainty in the object of sale (jahala) and lack of ownership (due to common property) render a sale invalid, but both can be cured – the former by an option condition, the latter by physical acquisition (ihraz) or cultivation.
