Ruling on Partnership in Water, Grass, and Fire
Explains the hadith on partnership in three natural resources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility of selling water, grass, and fire, and explain the role of acquisition (ihraz) in transforming common property into private property.

Core Idea
Water, grass, and fire are common property until physically acquired; sale is permitted only after acquisition.

The hadith 'People are partners in three: water, grass, and fire' is often misunderstood as a permanent ban on selling these necessities. The Hanafi school clarifies that the partnership applies only before acquisition. Once a person draws water into a vessel or cuts grass, it becomes their property and can be sold. This lesson unpacks the hadith, its application to fire, and a key intra-Hanafi dispute about grass grown on irrigated land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its apparent ruling
The hadith states that people are partners in three resources, implying common ownership and prohibition of sale. The addition 'and its price is forbidden' strengthens this initial understanding.
The hadith is narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and Ibn Majah.
2
Interpret the hadith: the condition of acquisition
The Hanafi school restricts the prohibition to before acquisition (ihraz). The phrase 'when its owner has not acquired it' shows that after physical possession, the item becomes owned and saleable.
Acquisition means taking physical control, e.g., drawing water into a vessel or cutting grass.
3
Apply the ruling to fire, water, and grass
For fire, partnership means the right to warm oneself and dry clothes, but taking embers requires permission. For water, drinking and watering animals is common, but drawing water makes it owned. For grass, cutting in owned land is allowed, but the landowner can restrict entry.
The text uses the analogy of a garment falling into a house to illustrate the right of access.
4
Examine the analogy of the garment
Just as someone whose garment falls into another's house has the right to retrieve it (with permission or the owner bringing it out), so too a person has a right to access water, grass, or fire on another's land for basic needs.
This analogy shows that the right is not ownership but a limited use right.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on irrigated grass
The majority, including Sadr al-Shahid, allow sale of grass grown on irrigated land because the cultivator owns it through effort. Al-Quduri forbids it, possibly relying on the hadith's generality. The relied-upon position is the majority view.
This dispute highlights how different interpretations of ownership affect saleability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شركاء في ثلاثة : في الماء والكلأ ، والنار)) رواه أحمد وأبو داود، وابن ماجة(۱)، وزاد فيه : وثمنه حرام»، وهو محمول إذا لم يحرزه صاحبه. ومعنى الشركة في النار الاصطلاءُ بها، وتخفيف الثياب لا أخذ الجمر إلا بإذن : وفي الماء الشرب وسقي الدَّواب، والاستسقاء من الآبار والحياض والأنهار المملوكة. وفي الكلأ : الاحتشاش ولو في أرض مملوكة، غير أنَّ لصاحب الأرض المنع من دخوله، ولغيره أن يقول : إنَّ لي في أرضك حقاً، فإما أن توصلني إليه أو تحشه أو تستقي وتدفعه لي، وصار كثوب رجلٍ وقع في دار رجل، إما أن يأذن للمالك في دخوله ليأخذه، وإما أن يخرجه إليه، والتفصيل في «الفتح». ومحل ما ذكر إن لم يحرز الماء بالاستقاء في آنية ، ولم يحرز الكلأ بقطعه، أما إذا (۲) أحرزا جاز بيعهما ؛ لأنه بالإحراز ملكهما. ومحله أيضاً فيما إذا نبت بنفسه، فأما إذا سقى الأرض وأعدها للإنبات فنبت ، فإنه يجوز بيعه ؛ لأنه ملكه، كما في «الذخيرة»، و«المحيط» و«النوازل»، وهو مختار الصدر الشهيد وعليه الأكثرون ومنع القُدُورِيُّ بيعه. كذا في «البحر» (۳)، وغيره.
They are partners in three: in water, grass, and fire. Reported by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and Ibn Majah, and he added: 'And its price is forbidden' – and it is interpreted as when its owner has not acquired it. And the meaning of partnership in fire is warming oneself with it and drying clothes, not taking the ember except with permission. And in water: drinking, watering animals, and drawing water from owned wells, cisterns, and rivers. And in grass: cutting it even in owned land, but the landowner can prevent entry. And the other can say: I have a right in your land, so either you let me reach it, or you cut it or draw water and give it to me. This is like the case of a man's garment falling into another's house: either he permits the owner to enter to take it, or he brings it out to him. The details are in al-Fath. And the ruling mentioned applies when water has not been acquired by drawing into vessels, and grass has not been acquired by cutting. But if they are acquired, their sale is permissible because by acquisition they become his property. And its ruling also applies when it grows by itself. But if he irrigated the land and prepared it for planting, and it grew, then its sale is permissible because it is his property, as in al-Dhakhira, al-Muhit, and al-Nawazil. This is the choice of Sadr al-Shahid and the majority, but al-Quduri forbade its sale. This is in al-Bahr and others.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage explains the hadith that people are partners in three things: water, grass, and fire. The Hanafi school interprets this to mean these resources are common property as long as they are not acquired (muhraz). Once acquired—e.g., water drawn into a vessel or grass cut—they become owned and can be sold. The ruling for water, grass, and fire is that initial benefit (drinking, pasturing, warming) is common, but taking exclusive possession (e.g., collecting embers) requires permission. A key intra-Hanafi dispute arises regarding grass grown on irrigated land: the majority (including Sadr al-Shahid) allow its sale because it is owned by the cultivator, while al-Quduri prohibits it. The relied-upon position is the majority view.

Key Terms
شركاء (shuraka'): Common owners sharing a right of use without exclusive possession.
الكلأ (al-kala'): Natural pasture or grass that grows without cultivation.
إحراز (ihraz): Acquisition through physical possession, making the object owned.
مملوكة (mamluka): Property that is privately owned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does grass become owned and thus salable?
A) When it is still growing.
B) After it is cut and collected (acquired).
C) When the landowner gives permission.
D) Never, because grass is always common property.

Key Takeaway
The hadith on partnership in water, grass, and fire establishes common ownership before acquisition; after physical acquisition, sale becomes permissible.

Selling Bees: The Hanafi Dispute on Valuable Property
Inter-madhhab dispute on the validity of selling bees alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on selling bees alone and identify which view is the mufta bihi, and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Bees alone are not valuable property according to Abu Hanifa, but the fatwa follows Muhammad's permissibility after acquisition.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses a classic dispute within the Hanafi school and across madhahib: can bees be sold without their hive? The disagreement hinges on whether bees constitute valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). We'll trace the arguments and the final operative ruling, including a legal stratagem for hiring bees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
What exactly is being debated? The validity of selling bees alone, without the hive (kuwwara).
2
State Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's position
They hold the sale is void because bees are not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). Their reasoning: bees are harmful insects (hawamm) and their benefit is not specific.
3
State Muhammad's position and his evidence
Muhammad (and al-Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) permit the sale if the bees are acquired (muhraz) and deliverable. Their evidence: bees are animals that are benefited from and transform; analogy to donkeys and mules.
4
Identify the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The fatwa in the Hanafi school is on Muhammad's view, as confirmed in al-Dhakhira, al-Minah, and al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
5
Note the legal stratagem (hila) for hiring
If one wishes to hire bees, one can hire the land for another purpose (e.g., halting animals) to the amount of the desired price, achieving the goal without directly paying for bees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا النَّحلُ إلا مع الكُوّارة، الكوارة: بالضَّم والتَّشديد: مَعْسَلُ النَّحل إذا سوي من طين، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، فينبغي أن يكون البيع باطلا عندهما؛ لعدم المال المتقوم، وعند محمد والشافعي لا يجوز إذا كان محرزاً. وقال في البحر: والحيلة في جواز إجارته أن يستأجرها أرضاً لإيقاف الدواب فيها، أو لمنفعة أخرى بقدر ما يريد صاحبه من الثمن أو الأجرة، فيحصل به غرضهما. قوله: الكوارة؛ في منتهى الأرب: كواره: خم مانندليت از شاخ درخت يا از گل درون تهي تنك سربرای عسل نهاون زنوران یا خانه زنور که دروي عسل نهد. قوله: لعدم المال المتقوم؛ لأنه من الهوام المخوفة من الأحناش، فإن الانتفاع ما يحصل منه لا بعينه فقيل: حصول ما يحصل منه لا يكون منتفعاً به. قوله: وعند محمد والشافعي؛ ومالك وأحمد: يجوز بيعه وحده إذا كان محرزاً مجموعاً، مقدور التسليم؛ لأنه حيوان ينتفع به، ويتحول فيصح بيعه، وإن لم يؤكل كالحمار والبغل؛ ولأن جواز البيع للحاجة إلى الانتفاع، والانتفاع يكون بواسطة وبلا واسطة. وفي الذخيرة: الفتوى على قول محمد، وهكذا في المنح والدر المختار. وأما جواز بيع النخل مع الكوارة، فذكر القدوري في شرحه: لو باع...
And bees are not [sold] except with the hive. Al-Kuwwara: with damm and tashdid: the beehive when made of clay. And according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the sale should be void due to lack of valuable property. According to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it is not permissible if it is acquired. And in al-Bahr: the legal stratagem for permitting its hire is that one hires the land for halting animals or for another purpose to the amount of the desired price or hire, achieving both purposes. The saying: al-Kuwwara; in Muntaha al-Arab: 'Kuwarah: a container like a pot made of tree branches or clay, hollow, thin, for honey, like the pouch or house of bees in which honey is placed.' The saying: due to lack of valuable property: because it is among harmful insects, and the benefit obtained from it is not specific, so it is said: what is obtained from it is not benefited from. The saying: according to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i; and Malik and Ahmad: its sale alone is permissible if it is acquired, collected, and deliverable, because it is an animal that is benefited from and transforms, so its sale is valid, even if not eaten, like a donkey or mule; and because the permissibility of sale is for the need of benefit, and benefit can be direct or indirect. And in al-Dhakhira: the fatwa is on the saying of Muhammad, and similarly in al-Minah and al-Durr al-Mukhtar. As for the permissibility of selling the bees with the hive, al-Quduri mentioned in his commentary: if he sold...
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of bees, a detailed issue in Hanafi fiqh. The text presents a dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider bees alone not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) because they are harmful insects and not directly beneficial; thus selling them alone is void. Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i (and also Malik and Ahmad) allow the sale if the bees are acquired and collected, analogizing them to animals like donkeys that provide benefit even if not eaten. The fatwa in the Hanafi school is on Imam Muhammad's view, as confirmed in al-Dhakhira, al-Minah, and al-Durr al-Mukhtar. The text also provides a legal stratagem (hila) for hiring bees: hire the land for another purpose and include the bees. The phrase 'قوله' introduces a super-commentary (hishiya) clarifying the text. The ruling is that bees must be sold with the hive (kuwwara) to be valid, but after acquisition, selling them alone is permissible according to the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
النحل (al-nahl): Bees, considered as a type of animal in fiqh.
الكوارة (al-kuwwara): A beehive made of clay or other material.
المال المتقوم (al-mal al-mutaqawwim): Property that has legal value and can be sold; bees were not considered such according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
الحيلة (al-hila): A permissible legal stratagem to achieve a desired outcome without violating the law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Void because bees are not valuable property.
B) Permissible if the bees are acquired and deliverable.
C) Only permissible with the hive.
D) Not allowed under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi permits selling bees alone if acquired, resolving the earlier doubt about their status as valuable property.

Sale of Silkworms and Eggs: Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale of silkworms and eggs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on selling silkworms and eggs, and explain why Muhammad's view is the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Three Hanafi imams, three rulings on silkworm sale — the fatwa follows the most permissive.

In this passage, we encounter a direct intra-Hanafi disagreement on a practical commercial issue: the sale of silkworms (dūd al-qazz) and their eggs (bayḍ al-qazz). The ruling touches on key principles: what constitutes a valid subject matter (maḥall) in a sale, the role of benefit (manfaʿah), and the concept of selling something as a follower (tābiʿ). The commentary presents all three positions of the school's founding imams and concludes with the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items under dispute
Recognize that silkworms and their eggs are the subject matter. Understand why they are problematic in Hanafi sales law: silkworms are insects (hawāmm), which are generally not considered valuable property; eggs have no immediate benefit and their future existence is uncertain (gharar).
This sets the stage for the ikhtilaf.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and rationale
Abu Hanifa holds the sale of both items to be absolutely invalid (bāṭil). His reason: the silkworm is an insect, not conventionally beneficial; the egg has no current benefit and its future benefit is uncertain, constituting gharar.
This is the strictest position.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's conditional position
Abu Yusuf distinguishes: the sale of the worm is valid if the silk (al-qazz) has already appeared, because then the worm can be sold as a follower (tābiʿ) to the silk. For eggs, he has two narrations: one allowing sale absolutely (due to necessity, aligning with Muhammad), and one prohibiting it (aligning with Abu Hanifa).
This shows that even within an imam's own corpus there can be variation.
4
Examine Muhammad's unconditional permissibility
Muhammad holds that both items can be sold unconditionally, regardless of whether silk has appeared or not. This is based on customary benefit and necessity, and it aligns with the other three Sunni schools.
This is the most expansive view.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and its authority
The commentator states that al-Sadr al-Shahid chose Muhammad's view and that fatwa is given accordingly. Al-‘Ayni and other later scholars affirm this. Thus the operative ruling for practice is unconditional permissibility.
This shows how later tarjih resolves intra-school ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ودودُ القَز وبيضه) ، فعند أبي حنيفة الله بيعهما باطل"، وعند أبي يوسف ه يجوز " إن ظَهَرَ القَزُّ تِبْعاً ، وإلا لا ، وعند محمد له يجوز مطلقاً". كوارة فيها عسل بما فيها من النَّحل يجوز عندهما تبعاً له، كما لو باع الشرب مع الأرض. وأنكر الكرخي له جواز بيع النَّحل مع العسل ، وقال : الشيء إنما يدخلُ في العقد تبعاً لغيره إذا كان من حقوقه كالشرب مع الأرض والتبع هنا ليس من حقوق المتبوع. كذا في كمال الدراية). [١] قوله : بيعهما باطل ؛ أمَّا بيعُ القزّ ؛ فلانه من الهوام، وأما بيع بيضه ؛ لأنه غير منتفع به باعتبار ذاته، بل باعتبار غيره، وذلك الغير معدوم في الحال، وفي وجوده في المآل خطر. [٢] قوله : يجوز ؛ بيعه إن ظهر القرُّ منه ، فيجوز تبعاً له، وفي البيض عن أبي يوسف له قولان : ١. في قول : يجوز بيع بيضه مطلقاً ؛ لمكان الضرورة، وهو مع محمد الله. ٢. وفي قول : لا يجوز ؛ وهو مع الإمام له فيه. [٣] قوله : يجوز مطلقاً ؛ أي سواء ظهر منه القرُّ أو لا، وهو قول مالك والشافعي وأحمد ، واختيار الصدرِ الشَّهيد وعليه الفتوى ، كما صرح به العَيْنِي ، وغيره من الكبار.
(The silkworm and its egg). According to Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, the sale of both is invalid. According to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible if the silk appears from it as a follower, otherwise not. According to Muhammad, may Allah be pleased with him, it is permissible absolutely. A beehive containing honey with the bees in it is permissible according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] as a follower, just as the sale of irrigation rights with land. And al-Karkhi rejected the permissibility of selling bees with honey, and said: Something enters the contract as a follower only if it is among its rights, like irrigation rights with land, and here the follower is not among the rights of the followed. Thus in Kamal al-Dirayah. [1] His saying: The sale of both is invalid; as for the sale of the silkworm, because it is an insect; as for the sale of its egg, because it is not benefited from in itself, but only by consideration of something else, and that something else is non-existent currently, and its existence in the future is uncertain. [2] His saying: It is permissible; its sale if the silk appears from it, so it is permissible as a follower. Regarding the egg, Abu Yusuf has two sayings: 1. In one saying: The sale of its egg is permissible absolutely, due to necessity, and he aligns with Muhammad. 2. In another saying: It is not permissible, and he aligns with the Imam [Abu Hanifa]. [3] His saying: It is permissible absolutely; meaning whether the silk appears from it or not, and it is the saying of Malik, al-Shafi‘i, and Ahmad, and the choice of al-Sadr al-Shahid, and upon it is the fatwa, as clarified by al-‘Ayni and other great scholars.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) concerning the validity of selling silkworms (dūd al-qazz) and their eggs. The base text (matn) states the two items, and the commentator explains the positions. Abu Hanifa holds the sale of both to be absolutely invalid (bāṭil) because silkworms are insects (hawāmm) and eggs are not independently beneficial; they only become beneficial when they hatch, which is uncertain. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the silk (al-qazz) is already apparent from the worm, then the worm can be sold as a follower (tābiʿ) to the silk, and the sale is valid; otherwise not. For eggs, Abu Yusuf has two narrations: one allowing sale absolutely due to necessity (ḍarūrah), aligning with Muhammad, and another prohibiting it, aligning with Abu Hanifa. Muhammad allows both items unconditionally. The commentator notes that Muhammad's view is also the position of Malik, al-Shafi‘i, and Ahmad, and was chosen by al-Sadr al-Shahid, upon whom fatwa is given. The passage also includes a related issue: selling a beehive (kuwwārah) with honey — according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the bees are permissible as a follower, but al-Karkhi objects, arguing that the follower must be among the rights of the followed, which is not the case here. This shows the Hanafi method of using qiyās and istiḥsān with careful condition-setting.

Key Terms
دود القز (dūd al-qazz): The silkworm, which produces silk.
بيض القز (bayḍ al-qazz): Silkworm eggs, which have no current benefit until they hatch.
تبعاً (tabaʿan): As a follower (tābiʿ), meaning the item is sold together with and dependent on a primary item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa in this passage, what is the ruling on selling silkworm eggs unconditionally?
A) Invalid (bāṭil) as per Abu Hanifa
B) Permissible (jāʾiz) as per Muhammad and chosen by al-Sadr al-Shahid
C) Conditional on silk appearance as per Abu Yusuf
D) Prohibited (ḥarām) because of gharar

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi on selling silkworms and eggs is absolute permissibility (Muhammad's view), chosen by al-Sadr al-Shahid.

Sale of an Absconded Slave: Fāsid or Bāṭil?
Ruling on sale of absconded slave with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on fāsid vs bāṭil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling an absconded slave, explain the proof from hadith, and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute on whether the sale is fāsid or bāṭil.

Core Idea
The sale of an absconded slave is fāsid, not bāṭil, because inability to deliver does not annihilate the subject matter.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling within the Hanafi school: the sale of a slave who has run away (ābiq). The base text declares it impermissible, but scholars differed on the legal classification—whether the contract is void (bāṭil) or merely defective (fāsid). The distinction is critical, as it determines if the contract can later become valid when the slave returns. Understanding the proof and the reasoning behind the preferred view will sharpen your ability to analyze similar cases of delivery incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Locate the passage where the author states the ruling on selling an absconded slave. Note that it is impermissible except when the seller claims the slave is with him.
The key phrase is 'والآبق إلا ممن زعم أنه عنده'.
2
Analyze the proof (dalīl)
Find the hadith cited from Ibn Majah. Identify the specific prohibition and the underlying cause ('illah): inability to deliver the subject matter.
The hadith lists several prohibited sales; the relevant one is 'شراء العبد وهو آبق'.
3
Trace the intra-school ikhtilāf
Note the two positions: ẓāhir al-riwāyah (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) says bāṭil because of absence of subject matter; later scholars (al-Karkhi, Ibn al-Humam) say fāsid because the subject matter still exists as proven by valid emancipation.
The dispute hinges on whether the subject matter is 'non-existent' or merely 'inaccessible'.
4
Understand the exception
Read the final clause: if the seller claims (zʿama) the slave is with him, the sale becomes permissible. Why? Because the prohibition applies to a slave who is actually absconded, not to one claimed present.
The seller's claim transforms the legal reality for the contracting parties.
5
Determine the muftā bihi
Identify the preferred view as stated in Fatḥ al-Qadīr and Manḥ al-Ghaffār: the sale is fāsid. Recognize that this is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
Authority: later Hanafi masters (mutā'akhkhirūn).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآبق إلا ممن زعم أنه عنده) [٤]قوله : والآبق ؛ أي لا يجوز بيعه، لما رواه ابن ماجه في «سننه» : عن أبي سعيدٍ الخدري له : «إنَّ النَّبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن شراء ما في بطون الأنعام حتى تضع، وعن بيع ما في ضروعها، وعن شراء العبد وهو ابق، وعن شراء المغانم حتى تُقسم، وعن شراء الصدقات حتى تُقبض ، وعن ضربة الغائص، ولعجزه عن التسليم. [١] قوله : فاسد؛ وصرَّحَ بفسادِ هذا البيع في «الدار» وغيره أيضاً، لكن قال في «البحر» : ولو باعَهُ ثمَّ عاد من الإباقِ لا يتمُّ ذلك العقد ؛ لأنه وقع باطلاً ؛ لانعدام المحلية ؛ كبيع الطير في الهواء ، وعن أبي حنيفة : إنه يتم العقد إذا لم يفسخ ، وهكذا يروى عن محمد. كذا في «الهداية» ، والأوَّلُ ظاهر الرواية وبه كان يُفتي أبو عبد الله البلخي . كما في «الذخيرة». ورجح في «فتح القدير» القولُ بالفساد بعد حكاية الخلاف فيه ، بقوله : «والوجه عندي أنَّ عدم القدرة على التّسليم مفسد لا مبطل». انتهى. وفي «منح الغفار» : «ومما يقوّى به القولُ بالفسادِ صحة إعتاقه وتدبيره ، وهو دليل بقاء المحل ولو فات المحل لما جاز، وبهذا القول أخذ الكرخي وجماعة من مشايخنا. كما في «العناية»، و«فتح القدير» ». انتهى. وفيه بحث ؛ لأنَّ الإعتاق إبطال الملك فلا نُسلّم تواه بالإباق، وأما البيع فإثبات الملك للمشتري والتوى ينافيه فلا يثبت كذا في «الكفاية». [٢] قوله : فحينئذ يجوز؛ لأنّ المنهي بيع آبق في حق المتعاقدين، وهو غير آبق في حقهما فجاز.
(And the absconded slave except from one who claims that he is with him). [4] His saying: And the absconded slave; meaning its sale is not permissible, due to what Ibn Majah narrated in his Sunan from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'The Prophet ﷺ forbade buying what is in the bellies of animals until they give birth, and selling what is in their udders, and buying a slave while he is absconded, and buying spoils of war until they are divided, and buying charities until they are received, and the diver's catch.' And due to the inability to deliver. [1] His saying: Defective (fāsid); and he explicitly declared this sale defective in al-Dār and others, but he said in al-Bahr: If he sold it and then it returned from absconding, the contract is not completed, because it occurred as void (bāṭil) due to the absence of the subject matter, like selling a bird in the air. And from Abu Hanifa: The contract is completed if he does not rescind, and thus it is narrated from Muhammad. Thus in al-Hidāyah, and the first is the apparent narration (ẓāhir al-riwāyah) and Abu Abdullah al-Balkhi used to give fatwa accordingly, as in al-Dhakhīrah. And in Fatḥ al-Qadīr he preferred the saying of defectiveness (fasād) after relating the difference, saying: 'The opinion according to me is that inability to deliver makes the sale defective, not void.' End. And in Manḥ al-Ghaffār: 'What strengthens the saying of defectiveness is the validity of his emancipation and tadbīr, which is evidence of the persistence of the subject matter; if the subject matter had perished it would not be permissible.' And with this saying al-Karkhi and a group of our shaykhs took, as in al-‘Ināyah and Fatḥ al-Qadīr. End. And there is a discussion in it, because emancipation is nullification of ownership, so we do not accept its disappearance due to absconding; as for sale, it is establishing ownership for the buyer, and disappearance negates it, so it is not established. Thus in al-Kifāyah. [2] His saying: Then it is permissible; because the forbidden is the sale of an absconded slave with respect to the two contracting parties, and he is not absconded with respect to them, so it is permissible.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on the sale of an absconded slave (ābiq). The base text says it is not permissible except with the seller claiming the slave is with him. The commentator begins by citing a hadith from Ibn Majah where the Prophet ﷺ forbade several types of sales, including buying an absconded slave, due to the seller's inability to deliver (al-‘ajz ʿan al-taslīm). The passage then dives into a dispute within the Hanafi school: whether such a sale is fāsid (defective) or bāṭil (void). The apparent narration (ẓāhir al-riwāyah) from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is that the sale is void (bāṭil) because the subject matter (the slave) is not present, like selling a bird in the air. However, some early scholars like Abu Abdullah al-Balkhi gave fatwa on that view. But the later preferred view (as in Fatḥ al-Qadīr) is that it is only fāsid, not bāṭil. The reasoning is that inability to deliver makes the contract defective but not void; evidence is that the owner can still validly emancipate or make a tadbīr (promise of freedom after death) of the absconded slave, which shows the slave still exists as property (the subject matter persists). The counter-argument from al-Kifāyah is that emancipation is an act of nullifying ownership, which does not require delivery, so it is different from sale. The commentator also clarifies the exception: if the seller claims (zʿama) that the slave is with him, the sale is permissible because the prohibition applies only to a slave who is absconded in reality, and here the seller's claim makes the slave legally not absconded with respect to the contracting parties.

Key Terms
آبق (ābiq): A slave who has absconded (run away) from his owner.
بيع فاسد (bayʿ fāsid): A sale that is legally defective but can become valid if the defect is rectified (e.g., inability to deliver is resolved).
بيع باطل (bayʿ bāṭil): A sale void from the outset, lacking an essential element (e.g., subject matter).
قدرة على التسليم (qudrah ʿalā al-taslīm): Ability to deliver the sold item at the time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), the sale of an absconded slave is classified as:
A) Bāṭil (void) because of absence of subject matter
B) Fāsid (defective) because inability to deliver does not nullify the subject matter's existence
C) Ṣaḥīḥ (valid) if the seller claims the slave is with him
D) Makrūh (disliked) but valid

Key Takeaway
The sale of an absconded slave is fāsid (defective) according to the later Hanafi position, and if the slave returns before rescission, the contract can become valid.

Sale of a Runaway Slave: Fasid vs Batil
Intra-Hanafi debate on fasid vs batil for inability to deliver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a fasid and batil sale in the Hanafi school and apply the rule to cases of inability to deliver.

Core Idea
A sale is fasid, not batil, when the subject matter exists but delivery is temporarily impossible.

This lesson examines a classical intra-Hanafi debate: is the sale of a runaway slave void (batil) or merely defective (fasid)? The answer hinges on whether the subject matter is considered non-existent or merely inaccessible. We will trace the arguments for both positions and see why later Hanafi authorities favoured the fasid view, which allows the sale to become valid once delivery is possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage is ruling upon: the sale of a runaway slave (ābiq). Identify the two possible hukm levels: fasid or batil.
The hukm depends on whether inability to deliver nullifies the contract or merely defects it.
2
Understand the two views
Distinguish between the Zahir al-Riwayah view (batil) and the later preferred view (fasid). Note the key scholars supporting each.
Zahir al-Riwayah: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad reportedly say it becomes valid if not revoked, but the 'first view' (al-Bahr, al-Hidayah) holds batil. Fath al-Qadir prefers fasid.
3
Analyze the evidence for each view
Examine the proofs: the batil view compares to selling a bird in the air (qiyas). The fasid view relies on the owner's ability to emancipate the slave, showing the subject matter still exists, and on the principle that inability to deliver corrupts but does not nullify.
The counter-argument: emancipation nullifies ownership, so it does not prove existence for sale.
4
Apply the preferred view
State the mufta bihi: the sale is fasid. If the buyer later takes possession (or the seller regains ability to deliver), the sale becomes valid. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Imam al-Karkhi and many later scholars adopted this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زَعَمَ : أي قال ، فهذا بيعُ فاسد ; لوجود المالِ المُتَقوم إلا أَنَّهُ لا قدرة على تسليمه، فإذا قال المشتري : إنَّه عندي فحينئذٍ يجوزُ . قوله : فاسد ; وصرَّحَ بفسادِ هذا البيع في «الدار» وغيره أيضاً، لكن قال في «البحر» : ولو باعَهُ ثمَّ عاد من الإباقِ لا يتمُّ ذلك العقد ; لأنه وقع باطلاً ; لانعدام المحلية ; كبيع الطير في الهواء ، وعن أبي حنيفة : إنه يتم العقد إذا لم يفسخ ، وهكذا يروى عن محمد. كذا في «الهداية» ، والأوَّلُ ظاهر الرواية وبه كان يُفتي أبو عبد الله البلخي . كما في «الذخيرة». ورجح في «فتح القدير» القولُ بالفساد بعد حكاية الخلاف فيه ، بقوله : والوجه عندي أنَّ عدم القدرة على التّسليم مفسد لا مبطل. انتهى. وفي «منح الغفار» : ومما يقوّى به القولُ بالفسادِ صحة إعتاقه وتدبيره ، وهو دليل بقاء المحل ولو فات المحل لما جاز، وبهذا القول أخذ الكرخي وجماعة من مشايخنا. كما في «العناية» و«فتح القدير». انتهى. وفيه بحث ; لأنَّ الإعتاق إبطال الملك فلا نُسلّم تواه بالإباق، وأما البيع فإثبات الملك للمشتري والتوى ينافيه فلا يثبت كذا في «الكفاية». [٢] قوله : فحينئذ يجوز; لأنّ المنهي بيع آبق في حق المتعاقدين، وهو غير آبق في حق المشتري; ولأنه لما انتفى العجز عن التسليم; لكونه مقبوضاً، فانعدم المانع.
He claimed: i.e. he said, so this is a defective sale due to the existence of valuable property except that there is no ability to deliver it. So if the buyer says: It is with me, then at that time it is permissible. He (the commentator) said: 'Fasid' — and he explicitly stated the defectiveness of this sale in al-Dar and elsewhere as well. However, he said in al-Bahr: And if he sold it and then it returned from running away, that contract is not completed because it occurred as void due to the non-existence of the subject matter, like the sale of a bird in the air. And from Abu Hanifah: that the contract is completed if he does not revoke it, and similarly it is narrated from Muhammad. This is as in al-Hidayah. And the first is the apparent narration, and Abu Abdullah al-Balkhi used to give fatwa accordingly, as in al-Dhakhirah. And the author of Fath al-Qadir preferred the view of defectiveness (fasid) after mentioning the disagreement, saying: The opinion in my view is that inability to deliver is a cause of defectiveness (ifsad) not invalidation (ibat). End. And in Mnh al-Ghaffar: Among what strengthens the view of defectiveness is the validity of his emancipation and tadbir (arrangement for freedom), which is proof of the survival of the subject matter; if the subject matter had vanished it would not be permissible. And Karkhi and a group of our scholars adopted this view, as in al-Inayah and Fath al-Qadir. End. And there is a discussion in it (this view): because emancipation nullifies ownership, so we do not accept its destruction (tawihi) due to running away. As for sale, it establishes ownership for the buyer, and running away contradicts it, so it is not established. This is as in al-Kifayah. [2] He said: At that time it is permissible, because the prohibition is of selling a runaway slave with respect to the contracting parties, and he is not a runaway with respect to the buyer; and because when the inability to deliver is removed, due to its being in possession, the impediment is removed.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of selling a runaway slave (abiq). The text begins by stating that if a seller claims that a slave is present, the sale is defective (fasid) because although the slave is valuable property, there is no ability to deliver him. However, if the buyer says the slave is with him, then the sale becomes permissible. The commentary then expands on the nature of the defect: some scholars, as quoted from al-Bahr, argue that the contract is void (batil) because the subject matter is non-existent, likening it to selling a bird in the air. In contrast, Abu Hanifah holds that the contract becomes valid if not revoked, and Muhammad al-Shaybani also narrates this. The 'first view' (batil) is the Zahir al-Riwayah position, used by Abu Abdullah al-Balkhi for fatwa. However, Fath al-Qadir prefers the view of fasid (defective) over batil, reasoning that inability to deliver corrupts (mufsid) but does not nullify (mubtil). Further support for fasid is drawn from the fact that the owner can still emancipate or arrange tadbir for the slave, showing the subject matter (the slave) still exists. A counter-argument is presented: emancipation nullifies ownership, so it does not prove the slave's existence, whereas sale establishes ownership, which is incompatible with the slave's absence. Finally, the second part explains why the sale becomes permissible when the buyer states the slave is with him: the prohibition of selling a runaway applies only from the seller's perspective; from the buyer's perspective, the slave is not a runaway since he can take delivery.

Key Terms
آبِق (ābiq): A runaway slave who has escaped from his master.
بَيْعٌ فَاسِدٌ (bayʿun fāsidun): A defective sale that is valid in essence but has a deficiency that can be rectified.
بَيْعٌ بَاطِلٌ (bayʿun bāṭilun): A void sale that is invalid from inception due to lack of subject matter or other essential condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (mufta bihi) in the passage, what is the ruling of selling a runaway slave?
A) The sale is valid unconditionally
B) The sale is void (batil) because the subject matter is non-existent
C) The sale is defective (fasid) and becomes valid if the buyer takes possession
D) The sale is prohibited and never valid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, inability to deliver makes a sale fasid, not batil, if the subject matter exists; the sale becomes valid upon subsequent delivery.

Sale of Human Milk: Invalid Due to Human Dignity
Ruling on selling human milk and its reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling human milk, explain the reasoning (talil) behind its invalidity, and distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Human milk is not property (mal) because it is part of an honored human; its sale is batil.

This lesson deals with a unique commodity: human milk. While animal milk is clearly saleable, human milk raises questions about dignity and property. The Hanafi school ruling—and the nuanced disagreement within it—hinge on a core definition: what counts as property (mal)? Let us trace the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
State the Zahir al-Riwayah ruling: the sale of human milk (from free or slave women) is batil (invalid). The text emphasizes 'in a cup' to avoid confusion with milk in the udder.
The cup clarifies that the invalidity is not due to location but due to the nature of the substance.
2
Understand the talil (causal reasoning)
The milk is part of a human being (juz' al-adami). Humans are inviolable; their parts are not property. The definition of mal excludes human parts because they are not created for trade and are not subject to miserliness (shuhh) and temptation (fitna).
The Shariah's establishment of breastfeeding (radha') as a cause of mahramiyyah confirms the 'partial connection' (ba'diyya) treating milk as part of the human.
3
Note the external dispute with al-Shafi'i
Al-Shafi'i holds that human milk is a pure drink (mashrub tahir) and hence saleable. The Hanafi response: it is not an absolute drink; when unnecessary, drinking it is haram, and in necessity it is nutrition but not property.
This dispute shows how definitions of property affect rulings.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf permits the sale of a slave woman's milk. His reasoning: the slave woman herself is property, and her milk is generated from her, so it can be sold as part of her.
The text criticizes the commentator for not phrasing the dispute as 'and from Abu Yusuf' rather than 'and according to Abu Yusuf'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The chosen view for fatwa (al-mukhtar li al-fatwa) is Abu Yusuf's position for slave women, as recorded in Mukhtar al-Fatawa.
Thus, while Zahir al-Riwayah rules invalid for all, later practice adopts the exceptional view for slave women.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولَبَنُ امراة في قَدَح ، إنّما قال في قَدَح ; لأنَّ بيع اللبن في الضرع قد ذكر، فلبنُ المرأةِ إنَّما يبطل بيعُهُ ; لأنَّه من أجزاء الآدمي، فلا يكون مالاً ، وفيه خلاف الشَّافِعِيّ. وعند أبي يوسف يجوز بيع لبن الأمة اعتباراً. [١] قوله : إنما قال...الخ ; حاصله : أنه لو لم يذكر القدح لتوهم أنه يجوز بيعه إذا كان في القدح، وإنما لا يجوز بسبب كونه في الثدي كما هو الحكم في ألبان سائر الحيوانات إذا باعَهُ وهو في الضرع لا يجوز، وإذا كان في القدح أو في غيره من الإناء يجوز. [٢] قوله : لأنه من أجزاء الآدمي ; بدلالة أنَّ الشرع أثبت به حرمة الرّضاع لمعنى البعضية، والآدمي بجميع أجزائه مكرَّم مصون عن الابتذال في البيع، فلا يكون مالاً ; لأنَّ المال ما خلق لمصلحة الآدمي مما يجري فيه الشح والفتنة، ومحل البيع هو المال، حتى لا ينعقد في غيره أصلاً. [٣] قوله : وفيه خلافُ الشَّافِعِيّ ; فإنّ عنده يجوز بيعه ; لأنَّه مشروب طاهر، والجواب أنَّ المراد به إما كونه مشروباً مطلقاً ، أو في حال الضرورة، والأوّل ممنوع ; لأنه إذا استغنى عنه حرم شربه، والثاني : مسلّم ; لأنه غذاء عند الضرورة وليس بمال، فلا يجوز بيعه. كذا في «البناية». [٤] قوله : وعند أبي يوسف ; قال في «الهداية»: ولا فرق في ظاهر الرواية بين لبن الحرة والأمة، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يجوز بيع لبن الأمة. انتهى. فلو قال الشارح: وعن أبي يوسف لكان أولى. [٥] قوله : اعتباراً ... الخ ; حاصله : أنَّ اللَّبنَ متولد من الأمة فيكون جزءاً منها ، وإيراد العقد على نفسها جائز ، فكذا على جزئها هذا. وهو المختار للفتوى كما في «مختار الفتاوى».
And the milk of a woman in a cup. He only said 'in a cup' because the sale of milk in the udder has been mentioned. The sale of a woman's milk is invalid because it is part of a human being, so it is not property. And there is the disagreement of al-Shafi'i concerning it. And according to Abu Yusuf, the sale of a slave woman's milk is permissible as a consideration. [1] He said: 'In a cup...' etc.; its gist: if he had not mentioned the cup, it would be misunderstood that it is permissible to sell it when it is in a cup. Rather, it is not permissible because it is in the breast, just as the ruling with the milk of other animals: if he sells it while it is in the udder, it is not permissible, and if it is in a cup or other vessel, it is permissible. [2] He said: 'Because it is part of a human being'; as evidence that the Shariah has established the prohibition of breastfeeding due to the meaning of partial connection (ba'diyya), and a human being in all his parts is honored and protected from being degraded in sale, so it is not property. Because property is what is created for the benefit of a human being, that which may be subject to miserliness and temptation, and the subject of sale is property, so that it is not concluded on other things at all. [3] He said: 'And in it is the disagreement of al-Shafi'i'; for according to him, its sale is permissible because it is a pure drink. The answer is that what is meant by it is either that it is a drink absolutely, or during necessity. The first is rejected because if one can do without it, drinking it is forbidden. The second is conceded because it is nutrition during necessity but it is not property, so its sale is not permissible. This is as in al-Binayah. [4] He said: 'And according to Abu Yusuf'; he said in al-Hidayah: 'And there is no difference in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) between the milk of a free woman and a slave woman, and from Abu Yusuf that the sale of a slave woman's milk is permissible.' End. So if the commentator had said 'And from Abu Yusuf' it would have been more appropriate. [5] He said: 'As a consideration...' etc.; its gist: the milk is generated from the slave woman, so it is a part of her, and entering into a contract on herself is permissible, so likewise on this part of her. And it is the chosen view for fatwa as in Mukhtar al-Fatawa.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage deals with the sale of human milk. The text first specifies 'milk of a woman in a cup' to distinguish from selling milk while still in the breast (which is prohibited for all animals). The sale of a woman's milk is invalid (batil) because it is part of a human being, and therefore not considered property (mal) according to the Hanafi school. The commentary explains that human beings are honored and their parts are not subject to commercial sale. This is supported by the fact that breastfeeding establishes a relationship of mahramiyyah (partial connection), showing that milk is part of the human. However, Imam al-Shafi'i permits the sale of human milk, arguing it is a pure drink. The Hanafi response counters that it is not an absolute drink; when not needed, drinking it is prohibited, and when needed (in necessity), it is nutrition but still not property, hence not saleable. The passage also records an intra-Hanafi dispute: in Zahir al-Riwayah, there is no distinction between the milk of a free woman and a slave woman—both are invalid to sell. But Abu Yusuf permits the sale of a slave woman's milk, arguing that since the slave woman herself can be bought and sold, her parts (like milk) can also be sold. The commentator notes that the preferred position (mufta bihi) appears to be Abu Yusuf's view for slave women, as stated in Mukhtar al-Fatawa. However, the standard texts (mutun) and their commentaries generally follow Abu Hanifah's view that it is invalid.

Key Terms
لَبَنُ امْرَأَةٍ (labanu imra'atin): Human milk, specifically from a woman.
جُزْءُ الآدَمِي (juz'u al-ādami): A part of a human being, which is inviolable and cannot be sold.
مَالٌ (mālun): Property; something created for human benefit that is subject to miserliness and temptation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on selling the milk of a free woman according to the Zahir al-Riwayah?
A) It is valid because milk is a pure drink
B) It is invalid (batil) because human milk is not property
C) It is makruh but valid
D) It is permissible only if the woman is a slave

Key Takeaway
The sale of human milk is invalid in the Hanafi school because it is part of a human being and not property (mal); however, Abu Yusuf permits it for slave women, which is the preferred view for fatwa.

The Invalidity of Selling Pig Hair Despite Permissible Use
Ruling with proof on selling pig hair

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the Hanafi school rules the sale of pig hair as invalid and explain the distinction between permissible use and saleability using the concepts of najis al-ayn and mal.

Core Idea
An inherently impure item cannot be sold even if necessity allows its use.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a key Hanafi principle: that inherent impurity (najis al-ayn) strips an item of its status as property (mal), thus making its sale invalid. Even though pig hair may be used for stitching in dire necessity, that permission does not extend to commercial sale. The commentary addresses objections by comparing with other impure items like dung and human milk, illustrating the school's nuanced reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage explicitly states that the sale of pig hair is batil (invalid). Locate the phrase that declares this.
The ruling is encapsulated in the phrase 'فإن البيع فيه باطل'.
2
Understand the underlying cause (illa)
The reason (illa) for invalidity is that pig hair is najis al-ayn, making it non-property (mal).
The text says 'لأنه نجس العين، فلا يكون مالاً'.
3
Note the exception for use
Despite the prohibition on sale, using pig hair for stitching is permissible in necessity. This does not change its legal status as non-property.
The permission is conditional upon necessity and does not imply property status.
4
Analyze the objection about dung
The passage contrasts pig hair with dung, which is also impure but its sale is allowed under certain conditions. Understand why dung is treated differently: pure dung cannot be sold, but impure dung can due to beneficial use.
This shows that the ruling depends on the nature of impurity (inherent vs. acquired) and the degree of benefit.
5
Appreciate the rational reasoning (dalil aqli)
The commentary uses rational argument about honor/dishonor to explain why permissibility of use does not imply permissibility of sale. Recognize that the reasoning is based on legal principles, not explicit scriptural text.
The argument about burning being honor or dishonor depending on context illustrates the contextual nature of legal valuation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشعر الخنزير، وإن حَلَّ الانتفاع به للخرز ضرورة. [1] قوله: فإن البيع فيه باطل؛ لأنه نجس العين، فلا يكون مالاً، فلا يجوز بيعه إهانة له. فإن قلت: لم جعل البيع في لبن الآدمي دليل الإهانة، وهاهنا دليل الإعزاز؟ قلت: الإعزاز والإهانة يتفاوتان بتفاوتِ محليهما، كالإحراق إهانة في الآدمي، وإعزاز في الحطب. كذا في «النهاية»، وأوردَ في «الفتح» على هذا التعليل؛ أي تعليل بطلان البيع بالنَّجاسة بيع السرقين، فإنّه جائز مع أنه نجس، فجوازه للانتفاع به، وقال في «النهر»: بل الصحيح عن الإمام أنَّ الانتفاع بالعذرة الخالصة جائز مع أنه لا يجوز بيعها خالصة. [2] قوله: للخرز؛ خرز بالفتح دوختن موزة و كفش. كذا في «المنتخب»، يقال: خرز الخف وغيره خرزاً: درز موزه و جز آن را. كذا في منتهى الأرب»، فيستعمله الخفاف في زمانهم، وكذا تستعمله النسوان لتسوية الكتان. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر». [3] قوله: ضرورة؛ لأن غيره لا يعمل عمله، فإنَّ مبدأ شعره قدر أصبع صلابة وبعده لين يصلح لوصل الخيط، صرَّح به القُهُسْتانِي، والله أعلم. فإن قيل: إذا كان يجوز الانتفاع به ينبغي أن يجوز بيعه؟
And pig hair, even though benefiting from it is permissible for stitching in necessity. [1] His saying: 'For the sale of it is invalid because it is inherently impure, so it is not property, so its sale is not permissible out of dishonor to it.' If you say: Why is sale in human milk considered evidence of dishonor, and here evidence of honor? I say: Honor and dishonor vary according to their locations, like burning: dishonor to a human, honor to firewood. As in al-Nihaya. And al-Fath commented on this reasoning, i.e., the reasoning of invalidity of sale due to impurity, regarding the sale of dung, which is permissible even though it is impure, because its permissibility is for benefiting from it. And al-Nahr said: Rather, the correct view from the Imam is that benefiting from pure excrement is permissible while its sale is not permissible when it is pure. [2] His saying: 'for stitching'; stitching is the perforation of boots and shoes. As in al-Muntakhab. It is said: He stitched the leather and other things, stitching meaning sewing the boot and its parts. As in Muntaha al-Arab. The cobblers used it in their time, and likewise women use it for straightening flax. As in Majma' al-Anhur. [3] His saying: 'in necessity' because other than it does not do its work. For the beginning of its hair is the size of a finger in hardness and after that it is soft, suitable for joining thread, as stated by al-Quhustani, and Allah knows best. If it is said: If benefiting from it is permissible, then its sale should be permissible.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on selling pig hair. The base text states that even though using pig hair for stitching is permissible in necessity, its sale is invalid. The commentary gives the reason: pig hair is inherently impure (najis al-ayn), so it is not property and selling it would be dishonorable to it. A potential objection is raised: why is selling human milk considered dishonor, while here it is considered honor? The answer is that honor and dishonor depend on the context; e.g., burning is dishonor to a human but honor to firewood (since it serves its purpose). Another objection: if dung (which is impure) can be sold, why not pig hair? The commentator notes that dung is permissible to sell because it has a beneficial use (as fuel), even though it is impure. But the correct view from the Imam is that pure dung (urine-free) cannot be sold because it has no value, while impure dung can be sold for its benefit. The passage also explains the meaning of 'stitching' (خرز) and the condition of necessity for using pig hair, as no other material suffices. The final question (if beneficial use is allowed, why not sale?) is left unanswered in the text but the implication is that the prohibition of sale remains due to the inherent impurity.

Key Terms
نجس العين (Najis al-ayn): Inherently impure: the substance itself is considered impure by Islamic law, such as pig hair, blood, etc.
خرز (Kharz): Stitching or perforating leather, especially for boots or shoes; used by cobblers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the sale of pig hair invalid even though using it for stitching is permissible in necessity?
A) Because it is not a property (mal) as it is inherently impure.
B) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) specifically forbade selling it.
C) Because it causes dishonor to the pig.
D) Because it has no beneficial use at all.

Key Takeaway
The sale of pig hair is invalid because it is inherently impure and thus not property; permissible use in necessity does not affect its unsaleability.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Sale and Use of Pig Hair: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale and use of pig hair

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale and use of pig hair, identify the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf, and apply the principle of darurah to determine when use is permitted.

Core Idea
Pig hair cannot be sold because it is najis al-ayn, but its use is permitted only when no alternative exists.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale and use of pig hair for lacing shoes. The base matn declares the sale invalid but permits use out of necessity. The commentary elaborates on the reasoning, addresses objections, and records differences among the three eponyms of the school. Understanding this dispute clarifies the criteria for permissible use of impure substances and the conditions under which sale may be allowed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The matn states: sale of pig hair is invalid, use for lacing is permitted due to necessity. Note the twin rulings and the darurah condition.
This is the foundation: najis al-ayn items are not property.
2
Examine the reason for sale invalidity
The commentary explains: pig hair is najis al-ayn, hence not property. Selling it would be an indignity (ihanah). Compare with human milk: selling is indignity there, but here it's considered honor.
The ihanah/ikram distinction depends on context.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Objection: manure (sirqin) is impure but its sale is allowed. Response: manure has beneficial use, so its sale is permitted. The analogy fails because pig hair's use is only for necessity, and sale is not needed as it is mubah al-asl.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa: sale invalid, use allowed for necessity. Muhammad: same but if only available by sale, sale allowed but price not tayyib. Abu Yusuf: use is makruh because najis. Note the practical consequences.
5
Apply the modern relevance
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling) today: sale invalid, use only for necessity; if sold out of need, the price is not tayyib for the seller. Consider how this applies to modern products like paintbrushes made from pig hair.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشعر الخنزير، فإنَّ البيع فيه باطل ، وإن حَلَّ الانتفاع به للخرز ضرورة . قوله : فإن البيع فيه باطل ؛ لأنه نجس العين، فلا يكون مالاً ، فلا يجوز بيعه إهانة له. فإن قلت : لم جعل البيع في لبن الآدمي دليل الإهانة، وهاهنا دليل الإعزاز؟ قلت : الإعزاز والإهانة يتفاوتان بتفاوتِ محليهما، كالإحراق إهانة في الآدمي، وإعزاز في الحطب. كذا في «النهاية»، وأوردَ في «الفتح» على هذا التعليل ؛ أي تعليل بطلان البيع بالنَّجاسة بيع السرقين، فإنّه جائز مع أنه نجس، فجوازه للانتفاع به، وقال في «النهر» : بل الصحيح عن الإمام أنَّ الانتفاع بالعذرة الخالصة جائز مع أنه لا يجوز بيعها خالصة. قوله : للخرز ؛ خرز بالفتح دوختن موزة و كفش. كذا في «المنتخب»، يقال: خرز الخف وغيره خرزاً : درز موزه و جز آن را. كذا في منتهى الأرب»، فيستعمله الخفاف في زمانهم، وكذا تستعمله النسوان لتسوية الكتان. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر». قوله : ضرورة ؛ لأن غيره لا يعمل عمله، فإنَّ مبدأ شعره قدر أصبع صلابة وبعده لين يصلح لوصل الخيط، صرَّح به القُهُسْتانِي، والله أعلم. فإن قيل : إذا كان يجوز الانتفاع به ينبغي أن يجوز بيعه؟ أجيب: إنَّ شعر الخنزير يوجد مباح الأصل فلا ضرورة إلى بيعه، كالكلا والماء ، وعلى هذا قيل : إذا كان لم يوجد إلا بالبيع جاز بيعه لكنَّ الثمن لا يطيب بالبائع ، صرح به العلامة العيني. وقيل : هذا إذا كان منتوفاً ، فالمقطوع يكون طاهراً. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر». وعن أبي يوسف : يكره الخرز به ؛ لأنه نجس ؛ ولذا لم يلبس السلف مثل هذا الخف، كما ذكره القهستاني ، ولعلَّ هذا ؛ أي جواز الانتفاع بشعر الخنزير في زمانهم، وأما في زماننا فلا حاجة إليه كما لا يخفى. كذا في «الدر المختار». قال محمد الله في «الجامع الصغير» : ولا يجوز بيع شعر الخنزير ويجوز الانتفاع به للخرز. وقال الفقيه أبو الليث : إن كانت الساكفة لا يجدون الخنزير إلا بالشراء ينبغي أن يجوز لهم الشراء، ولا بأس للأساكفة أن يصلوا مع شعر الخنزير وإن كان أكثر من قدر الدرهم. كما في «التبيين». وفي «العناية» : ويجوز الانتفاع به للخرز للضرورة ؛ لأن غيره لا يعمل عمله. فإن قيل : إذا كان كذلك وجب أن يجوز بيعه أجاب بأنه يوجد مباح الأصل فلا ضرورة إلى بيعه وعلى هذا قيل : إذا كان لا يوجد إلا بالبيع لكن الثمن لا يطيب للبائع. ومثله في مجمع الأنهر. وفي رد المحتار : «وقال الزيلعي : إطلاق الانتفاع به دليل طهارته ، وهذا يفيد عدم تقييد حل الانتفاع به بالضرورة، ويفيد جواز بيعه ؛ ولذا قال في النهر : وينبغي أن يطيب للبائع الثمن على قول محمد ... أما في زماننا فلا حاجة إليه ؛ للاستغناء عنه بالمخارز والإبر، قال في «البحر»: ظاهر كلامهم منع الانتفاع به عند عدم الضرورة بأن أمكن الخرز بغيره». ومن هذه النصوص يفهم أنه يجوز الانتفاع بريشة الرسم المصنوعة من شعر الخنزير على قول محمد وكذا بيعها مع طيب الثمن للبائع، وعند غيره فلا يجوز إلا للضرورة إلا إذا كان غيرها لا يعمل عملها، فإنه يجوز لكن لا يطيب الثمن للبائع. والله أعلم وعلمه أحكم.
And pig hair: its sale is invalid, and its use for lacing is permitted due to necessity. He said: its sale is invalid because it is inherently impure, so it is not property, thus its sale is not allowed as it is an indignity to it. If you say: why is the sale of human milk considered indignity, while here it is considered honor? I say: honor and indignity differ according to their contexts, like burning is an indignity for a human but an honor for firewood. Thus in al-Nihaya. And in al-Fath an objection was raised to this reasoning – i.e., the reasoning that sale is invalid due to impurity – with the sale of manure, for it is permissible even though it is impure; its permissibility is due to benefiting from it. And in al-Nahr: rather the correct from the Imam is that benefiting from pure dung is permissible even though its sale is not allowed when pure. He said: for lacing; with fath, meaning stitching socks and shoes. Thus in al-Muntakhab. It is said: he laced the khuff and other things, meaning he stitched them. Thus in Muntaha al-Arab. So the shoemakers use it in their time, and women use it for smoothing flax. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur. He said: necessity; because nothing else does its job, for the beginning of the hair is of finger hardness and after that softness that suits joining the thread, as stated by al-Quhustani, and Allah knows best. If it is said: if its use is permissible, then its sale should also be permissible? The answer: pig hair exists as originally permissible (mubah al-asl), so there is no need to sell it, like grass and water. And on this basis it was said: if it is not found except through sale, its sale is permissible but the price is not good for the seller, as stated by al-Ayni. And it was said: this is if it is plucked; if cut, it is pure. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur. And from Abu Yusuf: it is disliked to use it for lacing because it is impure; hence the early Muslims did not wear such a khuff, as mentioned by al-Quhustani. And perhaps this – i.e., the permissibility of benefiting from pig hair – was in their time, but in our time there is no need for it, as is not hidden. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: the sale of pig hair is not allowed, but its use for lacing is permitted. And Abu al-Layth said: if the shoemakers cannot find pig hair except by purchase, it is permissible for them to buy, and there is no harm for shoemakers to pray with pig hair even if it is more than a dirham's amount. As in al-Tabyin. And in al-Inayah: its use for lacing is permitted for necessity because nothing else does its job. If it is said: if so, its sale must be permitted, he answered that it exists as originally permissible, so there is no need to sell it. And on this it was said: if it is not found except through sale, still the price is not good for the seller. Similarly in Majma' al-Anhur. And in Radd al-Muhtar: al-Zayla'i said: absolute permissibility of benefiting from it indicates its purity, and this indicates that the permission of use is not restricted to necessity, and also indicates the permissibility of its sale; hence al-Nahr said: the price should be good for the seller according to Muhammad. But in our time there is no need for it due to being able to replace it with awls and needles. Al-Bahr said: the apparent meaning of their statements is prohibition of use when there is no necessity, if lacing with something else is possible. From these texts it is understood that it is permissible to benefit from a brush made of pig hair according to Muhammad, and also its sale with the price being good for the seller. According to others, it is not permissible except for necessity, unless nothing else does its job, then it is permissible but the price is not good for the seller. And Allah knows best, and His knowledge is most wise.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on pig hair in sale and use. The base matn states that sale of pig hair is invalid, but its use for lacing (خرز) is permitted out of necessity. The commentary first explains the reason for the invalidity of sale: pig hair is najis al-ayn (inherently impure), hence it is not considered property (مال), and selling it would be an indignity. A comparison is made: just as burning is an indignity for a human but honor for firewood, the context determines whether something is honoring or dishonoring. An objection is raised: if pig hair is impure, why is manure (سرقين) saleable even though it is impure? The answer: manure is permissible to sell because it has beneficial use (fertilizer). Similarly, pig hair use for lacing is beneficial, but its sale is prohibited because it is an inherently impure substance. However, the correct view from Imam Abu Hanifa regarding pure dung is that use is allowed but sale is not. Then the meaning of 'خرز' is clarified: it refers to stitching or lacing shoes and socks. Necessity is emphasized because no other material performs the same function due to the hair's unique texture. A logical objection follows: if use is allowed, why not sale? The answer: pig hair is originally permissible (مباح الأصل) like grass and water, so there is no need to sell it; it is freely available. But if it is only obtainable through sale, then sale is permitted, but the seller does not get a good (طيب) price—meaning the earnings are not considered wholesome. Another view distinguishes between plucked hair (natuuf) and cut hair (maqtuu'); cut hair is considered pure, so different rulings may apply. Abu Yusuf holds that using pig hair for lacing is makruh because it is impure, and the pious predecessors did not wear shoes with such lacing. The author concludes that the permissibility of use might have been in their time when alternatives were scarce; in our time, there is no need as awls and needles suffice. Further references: Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir confirms the ruling: no sale, but use for lacing allowed. Abu al-Layth allows purchase by shoemakers if necessary, and even allows praying with it even if more than a dirham. Al-Zayla'i's view suggests it indicates purity of the hair, thus use is not restricted to necessity, and sale should be allowed. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that use is only for necessity and sale is invalid, but if necessary the price is not halal. The practical upshot: brushes made of pig hair (like for drawing) are permissible to use and sell according to Muhammad's view with the price being wholesome; according to others, only for necessity and the price is not wholesome.

Key Terms
شعر الخنزير (sha‘r al-khinzir): Hair of a pig, considered najis al-ayn in the Hanafi school.
خرز (khazz): Lacing or stitching shoes or socks, using a thread through holes; here specifically using pig hair as thread.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity that permits using an otherwise prohibited substance, here due to lack of alternative material.
مباح الأصل (mubah al-asl): Something that is originally permissible and available without cost, like grass and water.
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): Something that is inherently impure, such that it cannot be purified and is not considered property (مال).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), what is the ruling on selling pig hair?
A) It is permissible because it can be used for lacing.
B) It is invalid because it is najis al-ayn and not considered property.
C) It is permissible only if the seller is a shoemaker.
D) It is makruh tanzihi and should be avoided.

Key Takeaway
Pig hair cannot be sold because it is najis al-ayn, but use for lacing is permitted only when no alternative exists, and if sold out of need, the price is not wholesome for the seller.

intermediate

Ruling on Sale and Use of Pig Hair
Extended discussion on sale and use of pig hair

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling and using pig hair, distinguish the positions of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the principle of darurah to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
Pig hair cannot be sold, but its use is permitted only when no alternative exists.

This passage examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school and across madhahib about the status of pig hair. While impurity generally prevents sale or use, necessity (darurah) may allow use for sewing when alternatives are unavailable. We will explore the reasoning behind the rulings and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the clear ruling from Muhammad al-Shaybani: selling pig hair is invalid; using it for sewing is permissible.
The permissibility of use is conditioned on necessity.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf considers even use disliked (makruh) due to impurity; early generations avoided it.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Abu Yusuf relies on the impurity of pig hair and the practice of the Salaf.
Impurity necessitates avoidance unless absolutely necessary.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Muhammad's position allows use in necessity because impurity is overlooked for genuine need; prayer remains valid.
The principle of darurah overrides the general prohibition.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Muhammad allows praying with pig hair even if more than dirham; Abu Yusuf considers it disliked.
The mufta bihi follows Muhammad, but only when no alternative exists.

هذه النصوص يفهم أنه يجوز الانتفاع بريشة الرسم المصنوعة من شعر الخنزير على قول محمد ه وكذا بيعها مع طيب الثمن للبائع، وعند غيره فلا يجوز إلا للضرورة إلا إذا كان غيرها لا يعمل عملها، فإنه يجوز لكن لا يطيب الثمن للبائع. والله أعلم وعلمه أحكم.
It was answered: Indeed, pig hair is found as permissible in origin, so there is no necessity to sell it, like hay and water. (1) And based on this, it was said: If it cannot be found except by sale, then its sale is permissible, but the price is not permissible for the seller. This was explicitly stated by Allamah al-'Ayni ~(1) 6. And it was said: This is if it is plucked, for the cut one is pure. As in 'Majma' al-Anhur' (3). And from Abu Yusuf: It is disliked to sew with it because it is impure; therefore the early generations did not wear such a khuff, as al-Qahistani mentioned (4). And perhaps this, i.e., the permissibility of benefiting from pig hair was in their time, but in our time there is no need for it, as is not hidden. As in 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar'. (1) Muhammad (al-Shaybani) said in 'al-Jami' al-Saghir' (p. 328): 'It is not permissible to sell pig hair, but it is permissible to benefit from it for sewing.' And Abu al-Layth al-Imam said: If the cobblers cannot find pig hair except by purchase, they should be allowed to purchase it, and there is no harm for cobblers to pray with pig hair even if it is more than the size of a dirham. As in 'al-Tabyeen' (4:51). And in 'al-'Inayah' (6:425): It is permissible to benefit from it for sewing due to necessity because nothing else can do its work. If it is said: If that is the case, then its sale should be permissible, he answers that it is found as permissible in origin, so there is no necessity to sell it. And on this, it was said: If it is not found except by sale, then the price is not permissible for the seller. And similar in 'Majma' al-Anhur' (2:59). And in 'Radd al-Muhtar' (5:72-73): 'Al-Zayla'i said: The absolute permission to benefit from it is evidence of its purity, and this indicates that the permissibility of benefiting from it is not restricted to necessity, and it indicates the permissibility of selling it. Therefore, he said in al-Nahr: It ought to be that the price is permissible for the seller according to Muhammad... However, in our time there is no need for it because of being free from need for it due to availability of awls and needles. He said in 'al-Bahr': The apparent meaning of their speech is to prohibit benefiting from it in the absence of necessity, if sewing can be done with something else.' And from these texts it is understood that it is permissible to benefit from a drawing feather made of pig hair according to Muhammad, and likewise its sale with the price being permissible for the seller. And according to others, it is not permissible except for necessity, unless nothing else can do its work, then it is permissible but the price is not permissible for the seller. And Allah knows best and His knowledge is most wise.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion (inter-madhhab dispute and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf) concerning the ruling on pig hair. The primary issue is whether pig hair can be sold and used for sewing. The base ruling from Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Jami' al-Saghir is that selling pig hair is not permissible, but using it for sewing (e.g., by cobblers) is allowed. The reason is that pig hair, coming from an impure animal (khinzir), is not considered pure property (mal) that can be sold, and its impurity prevents it from being a valid object of sale. However, necessity allows use for sewing because alternatives (like thorns or other materials) do not perform the same function. Many later scholars, such as Abu al-Layth, extend this to allow purchase of pig hair if it cannot be obtained otherwise, but the seller does not benefit from a pure profit (al-thaman la yatib). Abu Yusuf differs, considering even use for sewing disliked due to impurity, and the early generations avoided it. Later Hanafi scholars, such as al-Zayla'i, argue that if use is absolutely permitted, then the hair is pure, and sale should be permissible. Others restrict permission to necessity; if no necessity, use and sale are not allowed. In contemporary times, alternatives like synthetic materials exist, so the need is gone, and both use and sale are prohibited. The final takeaway: the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that pig hair cannot be sold as it is not pure property, and use is only allowed when necessary. The text also notes that cut hair is considered pure, while plucked hair retains impurity. This difference affects permissibility of use.

Key Terms
شعر الخنزير (sha'r al-khinzir): Hair of the pig; considered impure (najis) in Hanafi school.
خرز (kharz): Sewing or piercing (e.g., making shoes or bead work). In context, using pig hair as thread for sewing.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity; a condition that allows otherwise prohibited actions, when no alternative exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling on selling pig hair and using it for sewing?
A) Both sale and use are permissible without restriction.
B) Sale is valid, but use is only permissible in necessity.
C) Sale is invalid, but use is permissible only when necessary and no alternatives exist.
D) Both sale and use are never permissible.

Key Takeaway
Pig hair cannot be sold; its use for sewing is allowed only when no alternative exists, reflecting the Hanafi principle of necessity overriding impurity.

Human Hair: Prohibition of Sale and Use
Ruling on human hair with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on human hair (sale and use are invalid) and explain the rationale based on human dignity, while acknowledging the intra-Hanafi difference.

Core Idea
Human hair cannot be sold or used because humans are honored (mukarram) and their parts must not be commodified.

This lesson focuses on the Hanafi ruling regarding human hair. The matn declares its sale invalid and prohibits any use. The commentary explains that this is because humans are honored beings; thus, their body parts should not be treated with contempt. However, there is a narration from Imam Muhammad that permits use based on the Prophet distributing his hair. The relied-upon position, as in al-Hidayah, is prohibition. We will walk through the ruling, its evidence, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The matn states: 'And not human hair, for its sale is invalid, and not benefiting from it.' Recognize that this is a categorical prohibition (bāṭil).
Bāṭil means void and without legal effect.
2
Understand the rationale
The commentary from al-Hidayah says the reason is because the human is honored (mukarram) and not to be demeaned (lā yubtadhal).
This is a legal reasoning (ta'līl) based on dignity.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybānī permitted using human hair, citing the Prophet distributing his hair to companions.
This is a weaker narration (nādir) and not the muftā bihī.
4
Determine the muftā bihī
The relied-upon position is prohibition, as affirmed in al-Hidayah and other later works.
Authority weight: ẓāhir al-riwāyah.

ولا شعرُ الآدمي) ، فإنَّ بيعه باطل) ، (ولا الانتفاع به . ولا جلد الميتة قبل دبغه) ، فإنَّ بيعه باطل) ، (وإن صَحَ بيعُهُ والانتفاع بـ بعده كعظمها، وعصيها وصوفها وشعرها ، وقَرْنِها ، ووَبَرِها) : فإنَّ بيع هذه الأشياء صحيح، وكذا الانتفاع بها ؛ لأنَّ الموتَ غيرُ حال في هذه الأشياء. وفي «حاشيته»(۱) للطحطاوي ؛ للاستغناء عنه بالمخارز والإبر». قال في «البحر» :
(۲)
«ظاهر كلامه منع الانتفاع عند عدم الضرورة بأن أمكن الخرز بغيره». انتهى (٢). [١] قوله : باطل ؛ لأنَّ الآدمي مكرَّم لا مبتذل، فلا يجوز أن يكون شيء من أجزائه مهاناً مُبتذلاً. كذا في «الهداية»(۳).
[٢] قوله : ولا الانتفاع به ؛ وروي عن محمد الله أنه يجوز الانتفاع به مستدلاً بما روي أنه : «حين حلق رأسَهُ قسَّم شعره بين أصحابه ، وكانوا يتبركون به» (٤) ، كما لا يخفى على من طالع كتب السير والحديث ، فلو لم يجز الانتفاع به لما فعل. والله
أعلم. [٣] قوله : باطل ؛ إلا أنه غير منتفع به، وليس بمال لنجاسته فيبطل، بخلاف الثوب النجس، فإنَّ نجاسته عارضة.
[٤] قوله : لأن ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنها طاهرة؛ إذ لا حياة فيها، حتى يحلّها الموت، فإنَّ الموت لا يحل إلا في محل الحياة، وعند الشافعي : لا يجوز بيعها ولا الانتفاع بها ؛ لأنها من أجزاء الميتة، وبه قال أحمد الله في رواية.
وعند مالك : عظم الميتة نجس دون شعرها، وكذلك يجوز عندنا بيع جلد الكلب مدبوغاً، خلافاً للشافعي لله والحسن ، صرح به العلامة العيني في «شرح
And not human hair, for its sale is invalid, and not benefiting from it. And not the skin of a dead animal before its tanning, for its sale is invalid. And its sale and benefiting from it after it (tanning) are valid, like its bones, its sinews, its wool, its hair, its horn, and its fur. For the sale of these things is valid, and likewise benefiting from them—because death does not take effect in these things. And in its gloss by al-Tahawi: '...due to being free from need for it because of awls and needles.' He said in al-Bahr: 'The apparent content of his speech is to prohibit benefiting in the absence of necessity, if sewing can be done with something else.' End. [1] He said: It is invalid because the human being is honored, not demeaned, so it is not permissible for any of his parts to be treated with contempt and degradation. As in al-Hidayah. [2] He said: And not benefiting from it; and it is narrated from Muhammad that he permitted benefiting from it, using as evidence that when he (the Prophet) shaved his head, he distributed his hair among his companions, and they sought blessing through it, as is not hidden from whoever studies books of biography and hadith. So if benefiting were not permitted, he would not have done that. And Allah knows best. [3] He said: It is invalid because it is not used, and it is not property due to its impurity, so the sale is invalid; unlike a impure garment, for its impurity is accidental. [4] He said: Because...; its summary is that they are pure, for there is no life in them for death to affect; death only affects the locus of life. According to al-Shafi'i, it is not permissible to sell them nor benefit from them because they are parts of a dead animal. And Ahmad said the same in one narration. According to Malik, the bone of a dead animal is impure but its hair is not. Similarly, it is permissible according to us to sell the skin of a dog after tanning, contrary to al-Shafi'i and al-Hasan. This was stated explicitly by al-'Allamah al-'Ayni in 'Sharh...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement followed by commentary. The main matn prohibits the sale and use of human hair, and the skin of a dead animal before tanning. After tanning, the skin and other parts like bones, sinews, wool, hair, horn, and fur become pure and their sale and use are valid. The commentary explains the reasons: human dignity prohibits using human parts; the impurity of dead animal skin before tanning prevents its sale; after tanning it becomes pure. For bones and such, because they have no life, death does not affect them, so they remain pure. This is the Hanafi position and differs from Shafi'is who consider all parts of a dead animal impure. The text also includes a narration from Imam Muhammad that human hair can be used based on the Prophet distributing his hair. However, the relied-upon position is that human hair cannot be sold or used due to respect for humans. The passage also mentions that dog skin after tanning is permissible to sell, which is a Hanafi ruling contrary to Shafi'i.

Key Terms
شعر الآدمي (sha'r al-adami): Human hair; considered to be treated with dignity; cannot be sold or used for any purpose.
جلد الميتة (jild al-maytah): Skin of a dead animal (not slaughtered properly); impure before tanning, pure after.
دبغ (dabgh): Tanning; a process that purifies the skin of a dead animal, making it permissible to use and sell.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the sale of human hair?
A) Sale is valid, but use is not.
B) Sale is invalid due to human dignity.
C) Sale is valid only after tanning.
D) Sale is permissible for necessity.

Key Takeaway
Human hair cannot be sold or used because of the principle of human dignity (karāmah al-insān).
